Kibble v. Weeks Dredging & Constr. Co.


A-38 September Term 1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


161 N.J. 178; 735 A.2d 1142; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 1001


March 2, 1999, Argued  
August 9, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On certification to Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), for determination whether worker who settled workers' compensation claim pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:15-20 simultaneously waived future right of appellant spouse to assert statutory claim for dependency benefits upon worker's death.


OVERVIEW: The court certified the question of whether a worker who settled a workers' compensation claim pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:15-20 simultaneously waived any future right of appellant spouse to assert a statutory claim for dependency benefits upon worker's death. The court determined that a spouse must knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily waive the right to future dependency claims in all future § 20 settlements which contemplated the employee's death as result of conditions encompassed by the employee's compensation claim. The court reversed the lower court's decision that affirmed the New Jersey Division of Workers' Compensation's holding that the worker's waiver of his spouse's dependency claim was binding on appellant and remanded to the Division of Workers' Compensation, concluding that although the ruling would apply only prospectively, appellant should receive the benefit of having successfully litigated the claim upon which the new rule of law was based.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and remanded because spouse must knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily waive right to future dependency claims in all future section 20 settlements which contemplate employee's death as result of conditions encompassed by employee's compensation claim.

3 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Dale v. BSA


A-195/196 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


160 N.J. 562; 734 A.2d 1196; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 995


January 5, 1999, Argued  
August 4, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 14, 2000, Reported at: 528 U.S. 1109, 120 S. Ct. 865, 145 L. Ed. 2d 725, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 509.
Writ of certiorari granted BSA v. Dale, 528 U.S. 1109, 120 S. Ct. 865, 145 L. Ed. 2d 725, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 509 (2000)
Reversed by, Remanded by BSA v. Dale, 530 U.S. 640, 120 S. Ct. 2446, 147 L. Ed. 2d 554, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 4487 (2000)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 308 N.J. Super. 516, 706 A.2d 270 (1998).
Dale v. BSA, 308 N.J. Super. 516, 706 A.2d 270, 1998 N.J. Super. LEXIS 79 (App.Div., 1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, collectively the Boy Scouts, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that the Law Against Discrimination, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-1 to 49, did not violate defendants' freedom of association under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, an assistant scoutmaster, was expelled solely because of the Boy Scouts' policy of excluding avowed homosexuals from membership. He filed suit under the Law Against Discrimination (LAD), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-1 to -49. Defendants appealed, claiming that the LAD violated their First Amendment rights and that they were exempt from the LAD. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-5l, neither the distinctly private exception, the religious educational facility exception, nor the in loco parentis exception to the LAD applied to defendants. Because plaintiff's scoutmaster position was a privilege of membership, and because defendants revoked plaintiff's membership based on his avowed homosexuality, defendants violated the LAD. Finally, the court found that the LAD did not violate defendants' First Amendment rights, because plaintiff's presence in the organization was not tantamount to defendants' endorsement of homosexuality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment.

 

4 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Trump Hotels & Casino Resorts


A-225 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


160 N.J. 505; 734 A.2d 1160; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 991


January 21, 1999, Argued  
August 2, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 314 N.J. Super. 536, 715 A.2d 994 (1998).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant casino appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) holding the 1984 amendments to the Casino Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-144.1 did not unconstitutionally divert "state revenues" away from senior citizens and disabled residents.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the proceeds of investments authorized by the 1984 amendments to the Casino Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-144.1 (amendments) did not constitute "state revenues" and that such proceeds had not been unconstitutionally diverted away from the eligible senior citizens and disabled residents. The court found that the purpose of the amendments was to stimulate investment by licensees in Atlantic City. The parking fees authorized by the amendment constituted state revenue but that revenue was not revenue derived from the operation of gambling establishments nor were they the type of casino-related revenue that the legislature intended to be dedicated to eligible senior and disabled citizens. Accordingly, the constitutionality of the parking fee legislation was sustained.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because proceeds of casino investments were not state revenues and casino gambling proceeds had not been unconstitutionally diverted away from the eligible senior citizens and disabled residents.

 

5 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Twp. of Pennsauken v. Schad


A-197/A-198 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


160 N.J. 156; 733 A.2d 1159; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 974


March 1, 1999, Argued  
July 28, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 307 N.J. Super. 493, 704 A.2d 1337 (1998).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Township petitioned for certification to review decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) which determined that transparency displays placed inside the windows of defendant's adult entertainment premises were not "signs" within the meaning of the plaintiff's sign ordinance and defendant cross appealed to address his constitutional claims.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating plaintiff's sign ordinance. The court held that defendant's interior illuminated transparencies were devices used for visual communication, display, identification and publicity for the purpose of communicating the message that beautiful girls were inside. The court, employing the four-part test pronounced in Central Hudson Gas and Electric Corp. v. New York Public Service Commission, held that the ordinance did not restrict speech based on its content and that its regulatory restrictions examined under standards of intermediate scrutiny applicable to commercial speech were not an unconstitutional limitation of defendant's speech. Procedural due process was satisfied by the prompt judicial review accorded by a defendant's right to appeal. The ordinance was not facially vague because the defendant presented insufficient evidence of disparate treatment, and although the amount of the fine for violation of the sign ordinance was large, it was not unconstitutionally excessive.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed because the ordinance applied to defendant's interior displays, its restrictions were not an unconstitutional limitation of defendant's speech, satisfied due process, and though the fine was large it was not unconstitutionally excessive.

 

6 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Martini


A-222 September 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


160 N.J. 248; 734 A.2d 257; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 844


October 14, 1998, Submitted  
July 27, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Martini v. Hendricks, 188 F. Supp.2d 505, 2002 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 4413 (D.N.J., 2002)
Motion granted by, Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Martini, 179 N.J. 306, 845 A.2d 132, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 316 (N.J., Mar. 9, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County.
State v. Martini, 148 N.J. 453, 690 A.2d 603, 1997 N.J. LEXIS 139 (1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner-appellant, the Public Defender, filed a petition for post-conviction relief over the objections of defendant-respondent for post-conviction relief from a conviction of murder and sentence of death by the Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County (New Jersey).


OVERVIEW: Defendant-respondent was convicted of a murder. Defendant-respondent appealed and the conviction was affirmed. Although defendant-respondent did not wish to continue the appeal process, petitioner-appellant, the Public Defender, sought further appeal, alleging ineffective counsel for failing to present mitigating evidence. The appellate court remanded for consideration. The trial court found that the mitigating evidence would not have changed the trial outcome. Petitioner-appellant appealed. Affirming the denial of defendant-respondent's petition for post-conviction relief, the court held defendant-respondent's counsel was not ineffective for (1) failing to submit certain mitigating evidence because the evidence had limited mitigating value and possessed potential harm, and the defendant-respondent did not want it disclosed, (2) defendant-respondent did not suffer a manifest injustice by the failure to present the evidence, and the (3) failure to present evidence of burn screens would not have substantially affected the jury's deliberations.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed. Defendant-respondent's counsel was not ineffective for failing to present mitigating evidence because the evidence had limited mitigating value, possessed potential harm, and would not have substantially affected the jury's deliberations.

 

7 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Purnell


A-208 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


161 N.J. 44; 735 A.2d 513; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 832


January 20, 1999, Argued  
July 7, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Purnell v. Hendricks, 2000 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 16327 (D.N.J., Oct. 16, 2000)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 310 N.J. Super. 407, 708 A.2d 1196 (1998).
State v. Purnell, 310 N.J. Super. 407, 708 A.2d 1196, 1998 N.J. Super. LEXIS 118 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which retroactively applied the rule that the element of materiality in the crime of perjury must be submitted to the jury to defendant's collateral attack made in his post conviction relief application and reversed his perjury conviction.


OVERVIEW: Two months after defendant's direct appeal was completed, the New Jersey Supreme Court held that the determination of the element of materiality in the crime of perjury must be submitted to the jury. The court held that it would not retroactively apply the new rule to defendant's collateral attack of his perjury conviction. The court, reviewing a three-pronged retroactivity test, determined that the purpose of the new rule would not be advanced by retroactivity, that the prior rule did not substantially impair the truth-finding process, and the impact of retroactive application of the new rule of law on the administration of justice would be too severe. The court reached the same conclusion by applying the federal retroactivity test. The procedure employed in defendant's trial for perjury was far different from the kind of absolute prerequisite to the fundamental fairness that was implicit in the concept of ordered liberty.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed holding that defendant was not permitted to collaterally attack his prior judgment of conviction for perjury by retroactively applying the new rule that the element of materiality in the crime of perjury must be submitted to the jury.

 

8 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Apprendi


A-164 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


159 N.J. 7; 731 A.2d 485; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 824


October 13, 1998, Argued  
June 24, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted November 29, 1999, Reported at: 528 U.S. 1018, 120 S. Ct. 525, 145 L. Ed. 2d 407, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 7695.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 304 N.J. Super. 147, 698 A.2d 1265 (1997).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from upholding conviction by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), arguing that provision of hate crime law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:44-3(e), which allowed judge to enhance sentence if he found biased purpose, was unconstitutional unless jury found that purpose to have existed beyond reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentencing, arguing that the provision of the hate crime law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:44-3(e), which allowed judge to enhance sentence if he found biased purpose, was unconstitutional because it should have been jury who found that biased purpose existed beyond reasonable doubt. The court examined whether the given portion of the statute constituted an element of an offense and found it instead to be sentencing provision. Thus, the factual determination could be made by the sentencer. The court also found the provision was narrowly tailored to meet First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend I, concerns. A finding of a biased motive or purpose to intimidate was a very traditional sentencing factor. The claim that the statute conflicted with a constitutional right was far from clear beyond a reasonable doubt. The statute did not punish thought but did punish more severely crimes involving particularly vulnerable victims.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because biased purpose was a sentencing provision that could be determined by the sentencer; the enhancement was not unconstitutional. It was narrowly tailored to meet First Amendment concerns.

 

9 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cuni


A-141 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


159 N.J. 584; 733 A.2d 414; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 669


September 29, 1998, Argued  
June 14, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 303 N.J. Super. 584, 697 A.2d 550 (1997).
State v. Cuni, 303 N.J. Super. 584, 697 A.2d 550, 1997 N.J. Super. LEXIS 334 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) which affirmed his convictions for sexual assault of a mentally defective person.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the sexual assault of a mentally defective person. Defendant attempted to introduce evidence of the victim's past sexual experiences through the cross-examination of plaintiff's expert psychologist. According to defendant, the purpose of that evidence was to demonstrate that the victim had the capacity to consent to sexual relations despite her mental condition. The court held that only in situations where the relevance and probative worth of prior sexual experience were clear and substantial should the Rape Shield Law bend to the confrontation right of defendant. That was not the case here and any further probative value in admitting evidence of the victim's past sexual experience was clearly outweighed by its prejudicial effect and capacity to confuse; judgment affirmed.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for sexual assault. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and ruled that there was no probative value in admitting evidence of the victim's past sexual experience.

 

10 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Chew


A-148 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


159 N.J. 183; 731 A.2d 1070; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 658


March 17, 1998, Argued  
June 3, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Chew v. New Jersey, 528 U.S. 1052, 145 L. Ed. 2d 493, 120 S. Ct. 593, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 8116 (1999)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Chew, 179 N.J. 186, 844 A.2d 487, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 153 (N.J., Mar. 25, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On proportionality review of a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.
State v. Chew, 150 N.J. 30, 695 A.2d 1301, 1997 N.J. LEXIS 182 (1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3e, defendant sought review of the proportionality of a death sentence he received in the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County (New Jersey).


OVERVIEW: Defendant murdered his girlfriend, having told various witnesses that he planned to collect on an insurance policy she owned. He was convicted of purposeful or knowing murder by his own conduct under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3a(1) or (2) and possession of a weapon for an unlawful purpose under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:39-4d and was sentenced to death. The reviewing court affirmed defendant's death sentence, holding that he failed to meet his burden of demonstrating that his sentence was disproportionate when compared to similar cases. Specifically, using both the salient-factors test and the index-of-outcomes test for frequency review, the court found no showing of randomness or aberration. In this regard, the court compared him with the entire group of pecuniary-motive killers, finding such comparison necessary because only one other defendant remained in the same subcategory as defendant. Similarly, under the precedent-seeking approach, the second component of proportionality review, the court held that defendant was not singled out unfairly for capital punishment, nor was his sentence aberrational.


OUTCOME: Finding no disproportionality, the court affirmed defendant's death sentence, holding that it was not disproportionate because it was neither random nor aberrant when it was viewed using either the frequency approach or the precedent-seeking approach.

 

11 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cooper


A-147 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


159 N.J. 55; 731 A.2d 1000; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 659


September 15, 1998, Argued  
June 3, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Cooper, 175 N.J. 70, 812 A.2d 1104, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1825 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: On proportionality review of a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.
State v. Cooper, 151 N.J. 326, 700 A.2d 306, 1997 N.J. LEXIS 259 (1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant requested proportionality review of his death sentence imposed by the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County (New Jersey).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder for the killing of a six-year old girl. At the penalty phase, the jury determined that aggravating factors outweighed mitigating factors beyond a reasonable doubt and sentenced defendant to death. The conviction and sentence were affirmed on appeal. Thereafter, the court granted defendant's request for proportionality review and affirmed the sentence. In applying the salient-factors test, the court concluded that defendant's sentence was not disproportionate to other defendants who sexually assaulted and murdered their victims. Further, the application of the index-of-outcomes test did not demonstrate that defendant's sentence was disproportionate. The diminished reliability of the index-of-outcomes test, however, required the court to give enhanced weight to the process of precedent-seeking review. Under the precedent-seeking review test, the court concluded that defendant's culpability was quite substantial, the degree of victimization was extreme, and his moral blameworthiness was very high. Thus, in comparing defendant to defendants who committed similar crimes, the court found that the sentence was not aberrational.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence, holding that defendant's sentence was not disproportionate to other defendants under the salient-factors test and the index-of-outcomes test. Further, under the precedent-seeking review test, defendant's sentence was not aberrational when compared with defendants who committed similar crimes.

State v. Harvey


A-146 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


159 N.J. 277; 731 A.2d 1121; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 1186

June 3, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction proceeding at, Motion granted by, Motion denied by State v. Harvey, 171 N.J. 439, 794 A.2d 179, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 506 (Mar. 19, 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On proportionality review of a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.
State v. Harvey, 151 N.J. 117, 699 A.2d 596, 1997 N.J. LEXIS 261 (July 30, 1997)

OVERVIEW: Defendant broke into the apartment of his victim and brutally murdered her, administering approximately 15 blows to her head. The victim's body showed triangular pressure marks on either side of her neck, some of her teeth were knocked out, and her jaw was broken. The jury returned a unanimous verdict that defendant had committed the murder for the purpose of avoiding apprehension under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(f) and in the course of a robbery and burglary under N. J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(g). The trial court sentenced defendant to death. The reviewing court affirmed defendant's death sentence, holding that defendant failed to meet his burden of demonstrating that his sentence was disproportionate when compared to similar cases. Specifically, using both the salient-factors test and the index-of-outcomes test for frequency review, the court found no showing of randomness or aberration. Similarly, under the precedent-seeking approach, the second component of proportionality review, the court held that defendant was not singled out unfairly for capital punishment, nor was his sentence aberrational.


OUTCOME: Finding no disproportionality, the court affirmed defendant's death sentence, holding that it was not disproportionate because it was neither random nor aberrant when it was viewed using either the frequency approach or the precedent-seeking approach.

 

13 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Casamasino v. City of Jersey City


A-201/A-202/A-203 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


158 N.J. 333; 730 A.2d 287; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 654


October 13, 1998, Argued  
May 27, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 304 N.J. Super. 226, 699 A.2d 697 (1997).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties petitioned for certification upon an order by the appellate court, affirming the decision that plaintiff had properly acquired tenure as tax assessor for defendant city. Defendants sought review of that decision, and plaintiff sought review of the court's dismissal of his defamation and punitive damages claims.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff assessor was employed by defendant city as assistant tax assessor. Plaintiff was then appointed tax assessor to complete the term of the assessor who died while in office. Although the mayor appointed plaintiff to a four-year term upon the expiration of the current term, the city council never formally approved the appointment. When plaintiff finished his term, the mayor took no action to reappoint him. Plaintiff continued to serve as assessor, and defendant city continued to pay him. When a new mayor was elected, he notified plaintiff that he was no longer to serve as assessor. Plaintiff brought suit, and the trial court found that defendants had consented to plaintiff's appointment through silence and acquiescence, and that the continued silence was essentially a reappointment to the position, thus giving rise to tenure. Defendants appealed the decision, and plaintiff cross-appealed the dismissal of his defamation and punitive damages claims. The appellate court affirmed, and the parties petitioned for certification. The court reversed the affirmance, holding that a tax assessor could not acquire tenure without undergoing the statutory reappointment process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's affirmance, holding that defendant city had not approved plaintiff's appointment as tax assessor through proper reappointment process, and plaintiff therefore did not acquire tenure as assessor.

 

14 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


2nd Roc-Jersey Assocs. v. Town of Morristown


A-137/A-138 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


158 N.J. 581; 731 A.2d 1; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 545


September 28, 1998, Argued  
May 13, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), determination that defendant's exclusions on special assessments on real property pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40.56-66(b) were not unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the lower court judgment that found that the special assessments that defendant municipality imposed upon plaintiff businesses pursuant to state law was not a violation of the New Jersey Constitution simply because residential properties were excluded from the assessment. The requirement that all property taxes be assessed according to the same standard of value did not apply, because the assessment was not a real property tax and was designed to benefit particular properties. The statute in question was designed to better commercial properties and promote economic growth in the business community. The assessments were not clearly out of proportion to the benefits from them, and were presumed to be correct. The exclusion of residential properties did not constitute an invalid exemption under N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 1, para. 2, because the assessments provided identifiable benefits to plaintiffs, and because the legislative history indicated an intent to exempt residential properties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the finding that defendant's special assessments were not a violation of the state constitution because they were not taxes, given that it benefited specific properties, and because the benefits received were not clearly out of relation to the amount assessed.

 

15 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Loftin


A-86 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


157 N.J. 253; 724 A.2d 129; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 15


March 18, 1997, Argued  
February 1, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Post-conviction relief granted at, Remanded by State v. Loftin, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 605 (N.J., June 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: On Proportionality Review of a Death Sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.
State v. Loftin, 146 N.J. 295, 680 A.2d 677, 1996 N.J. LEXIS 970 (1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant requested a proportionality review, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3e, of a judgment of the Superior Court's Law Division, Mercer County (New Jersey), which sentenced defendant to death after convicting him of murder.


OVERVIEW: After the court's affirmance of defendant's murder conviction and capital sentence, defendant requested a proportionality review of his sentence pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3e, alleging that his sentence was disproportionately imposed based on his race. In conducting the proportionality review, the court first considered the constitutionality of a recent amendment to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3e, limiting proportionality review to a specific group of similar capital cases actually imposing the death penalty. The court appointed a special master to further review the constitutionality issue. In its initial review of the amendment, however, the court abandoned that portion of its proportionality review analysis focusing on the numerical preponderance of aggravating and mitigating factors test. Concerning the review of defendant's sentence, the court affirmed, finding that defendant had not met his burden of proving that his death sentence was disproportionate or that race operated as an impermissible factor in the imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant's capital sentence for murder was upheld, because defendant failed to meet his burden of proving that his death sentence was disproportionate or that race operated as an impermissible factor in the imposition of the death penalty.

 

16 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Twp. of Saddle Brook v. A.B. Family Ctr.


A-180 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


156 N.J. 587; 722 A.2d 530; 1999 N.J. LEXIS 14


November 30, 1998, Argued  
January 28, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 307 N.J. Super. 16, 704 A.2d 81 (1998).
Township of Saddle Brook v. A.B. Family Ctr., 307 N.J. Super. 16, 704 A.2d 81, 1998 N.J. Super. LEXIS 2 (App.Div., 1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case involving the validity of governmental regulations that effectively barred an adult book store from operating in a certain township, the court granted certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), for purposes of resolving the issue as to which party bore the burden of proving the availability of sites for sexually oriented businesses within reasonable proximity to petitioner's site in the township.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner operated an adult bookstore contrary to a township's ordinances. The township sued to enjoin the violation of its ordinances, and later alleged that petitioner's business violated N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-7, which prohibited the operation of a sexually oriented business within 1,000 feet of certain religious and public places. The trial court concluded that § 2C:34-7 was unconstitutional because there was no area within the township that did not fall within the 1,000 foot barrier. The appellate court rejected that holding and remanded the matter to the trial court for an evidentiary hearing on the availability of sites outside the township for sexually oriented businesses that were close to a then-existing site. The burden of proving the availability of such sites was placed on the township. Upon certification, the court affirmed that allocation of the burden of proof in recognition of the fairness of imposing on the public body that sought to restrict protected speech the obligation of showing that its restrictions 1) were reasonably tailored to achieve its objectives, 2) were not more intrusive than necessary, and 3) provided adequate alternative means of communication.


OUTCOME: On certification, the court affirmed the appellate court's allocation of the burden of proof upon a township for ascertaining the availability of sites outside the township's boundaries for sexually oriented businesses, within a close proximity to petitioner's existing site. The court ruled as such in recognition of the fairness of imposing such an obligation on a public body to show that its restrictions upon speech were constitutional.

 

17 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Donis


A-134 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


157 N.J. 44; 723 A.2d 35; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 1789


April 27, 1998, Argued  
December 10, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed an order from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirming denial of defendants' motions to suppress evidence obtained from police officers' search of a state driver's license bureau's computer records. Defendants claimed random police use of the records through license plate checks violated their privacy rights under N.J. Const. art. 1.


OVERVIEW: In unrelated incidents, police used defendant drivers' vehicle license plate information to obtain computerized vehicle registration and license information. Upon learning defendants' driver's licenses were suspended, the police stopped defendants and issued citations. Both defendants brought motions to suppress the evidence obtained from the computer searches. The trial court denied suppression, and the appellate court affirmed. Defendants appealed, claiming they had a reasonable expectation of privacy in the records. The court affirmed, holding that, where the license plate information was openly displayed and a state statute authorized use of the information for law enforcement purposes, no privacy rights were implicated. Thus, defendants had no reasonable expectation of privacy in vehicle registration and license information where N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:2-3.4(c)(1) authorized the use of license plate information for law enforcement purposes.. The court suggested a two-tier process for accessing such information, with personal information being searchable only upon a finding that the car was unregistered or stolen or that the driver was in violation of licensing laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant automobile drivers' motions to suppress evidence obtained from a police search of computerized vehicle and license records. Where license plate information was publicly displayed and a state statute authorized use of vehicular and license information for law enforcement purposes, defendants' privacy rights were not implicated, and no articulable suspicion of criminal conduct was required.

State v. Morton


A-18/19 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


155 N.J. 383; 715 A.2d 228; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 646


February 3, 1998, Argued  
July 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Later proceeding at State v. Morton, 165 N.J. 235, 757 A.2d 184, 2000 N.J. LEXIS 1015 (2000)
Writ of certiorari denied Morton v. New Jersey, 532 U.S. 931, 149 L. Ed. 2d 306, 121 S. Ct. 1380, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 2358 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his convictions, in the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County (New Jersey), for purposeful-or-knowing murder by his own conduct, felony murder, first-degree robbery, armed robbery, second-degree aggravated assault, and third-degree aggravated assault, and the imposition of a death sentence and a non-capital sentence of 40 years imprisonment with 20 years of parole ineligibility.


OVERVIEW: The defendant challenged his convictions for purposeful-or-knowing murder by his own conduct and related offenses, and the trial court's imposition of a death sentence and a non-capital sentence of 40 years imprisonment with 20 years of parole ineligibility. The court affirmed and held that the defendant was not entitled to the original tapes of his confessions for testing, the prosecutor's statements in summation did not constitute reversible error, the trial court sufficiently tailored the charge to the facts of the case, the trial court's jury instructions were proper, the defendant received effective assistance of counsel, the defendant effectively waived his right to be present during the penalty phase, the defense counsels' motion to exclude defendant from the penalty phase did not result from a conflict of interest, the defendant's absence at a pre-trial hearing did not constitute reversible error, the defendant's in-custody statements were voluntary, and the trial court did not err in severing two counts. While the court found that the trial court erred in allowing the defendant to submit his parole ineligibility as mitigating evidence, the court found the error harmless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the trial court committed no errors in connection with the admission of evidence or the charges to the jury. The court held that the defendant received effective assistance of counsel. The court held that the defendants' absence during certain portions of the trial was not in error. The trial court's error in allowing the defendant to submit his parole ineligibility as a mitigating factor was harmless.

State v. Harris


A-17 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


156 N.J. 122; 716 A.2d 458; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 644


September 23, 1997, Argued  
July 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at State v. Harris, 165 N.J. 303, 757 A.2d 221, 2000 N.J. LEXIS 1023 (2000)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Harris v. New Jersey, 532 U.S. 1057, 121 S. Ct. 2204, 149 L. Ed. 2d 1034, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 4091 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.
State v. Harris, 282 N.J. Super. 409, 660 A.2d 539, 1995 N.J. Super. LEXIS 208 (App.Div., 1995)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his murder conviction and death sentence from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County (New Jersey), arguing that he was denied a fair trial because the trial court did not take strong measures to assure that the trial was free from prejudicial publicity.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of murder and imposed a sentence of death. Defendant appealed, arguing that he was denied a fair trial because the trial court failed to take strong measures to assure that his trial was free from the outside influence of prejudicial publicity. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence, finding that the measures taken by the trial court ensured that defendant's trial was free of extraneous influences. Although the trial court agreed with defendant that there was a realistic likelihood of prejudice from prejudicial trial publicity but did not grant a change of venue, the court found no error in the trial court's decision to empanel a foreign jury rather than to transfer venue. Further, the trial court's use of searching voir dire examinations and the impaneling of the out-of-county jury were trial management techniques that were specifically approved to ensure the right to an impartial jury. The court, however, noted that in future capital cases a trial court was required to change venue when there was a realistic likelihood of the continuation of presumptively prejudicial publicity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's murder conviction and sentence of death, holding that the measures taken by the trial court, which included the selection of the jury of out-of-county residents and extensive questioning of the jurors, ensured that defendant's trial was free of extraneous influences and, thus, defendant was not denied a fair trial.

State v. Feaster


A-1 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


156 N.J. 1; 716 A.2d 395; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 645


October 21, 1997, Argued  
July 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Subsequent appeal at State v. Feaster, 165 N.J. 388, 757 A.2d 266, 2000 N.J. LEXIS 1016 (2000)
Writ of certiorari denied Kenney v. New Jersey, 532 U.S. 932, 121 S.Ct. 1380, 149 L.Ed.2d 306, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 2360 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Gloucester County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his murder conviction and death sentence from the Superior Court, Law Division, Gloucester County (New Jersey), arguing that the trial court's rigid sequencing of the charge and deliberations improperly coerced the jury into reaching a death-eligible verdict.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of purposeful-or-knowing murder by his own conduct and imposed a penalty of death. On his appeal as of right, defendant challenged the trial court's sequential presentation of own-conduct and accomplice liability murder and an admonition that the jury reach the accomplice liability question only after acquitting on own-conduct murder. The court affirmed the decision. Accomplice liability was an alternative rather than a lesser-included form of murder. Further, where there was a rational basis for the jury to convict on the non-death-eligible alternative of accomplice liability, the trial court was required to charge the offense in a manner allowing simultaneous consideration of it with the death eligible purposeful-or-knowing murder. Thus, the charge that the jury did not have to consider accomplice liability unless it first acquitted of own-conduct murder was error. Under the circumstances of the case, however, the charge was not plain error possessing the clear capacity to bring about an unjust result because the jury's finding that defendant was the shooter reflected a consideration and rejection of accomplice liability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's murder conviction and sentence of death. Although the trial court erred by instructing the jury that it did not have to consider accomplice liability unless it first acquitted of own-conduct murder, the error was harmless under the circumstances of the case because the non-death-eligible option of accomplice liability was considered and rejected by the jury.

State v. Nelson


A-151 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


155 N.J. 487; 715 A.2d 281; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 647


March 3, 1998, Argued  
July 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 19, 1999, Reported at: 525 U.S. 1114, 119 S. Ct. 890, 142 L. Ed. 2d 788, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 737.
Writ of certiorari denied, Motion granted by New Jersey v. Nelson, 525 U.S. 1114, 119 S. Ct. 890, 142 L. Ed. 2d 788, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 737 (1999)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by State v. Nelson, 173 N.J. 417, 803 A.2d 1, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1089 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a death sentence imposed by the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County (New Jersey), following the defendant's convictions for two counts of capital murder and one count of second-degree aggravated assault based upon the defendant's pleas of guilty to all three counts.


OVERVIEW: During the execution of a search warrant by police officers, the defendant shot two police officers and seriously wounded another officer. The defendant pled guilty to two counts of capital murder and one count of second-degree aggravated assault. As a result, the trial court convicted the defendant on all three counts. The defendant received a sentence of death. On review, the court vacated the death sentence, because the prosecution failed to disclose information regarding a civil suit filed the police officer who survived the shoot-out. In the civil action, the police officer alleged that he was provided with inadequate training to serve a search warrant on the defendant, an individual with a history of severe mental health concerns. The court explained that information about the officer's civil action would have supported the "catch-all" mitigating factor advanced by the defendant. The court concluded that the prosecution violated the Brady rule by its failure to provide evidence, which was favorable to the defense and material to the jury's determination as to whether the defendant deserved the death penalty. Thus, the court remanded the case for a sentencing retrial.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence of death. The court held that the prosecution's failure to disclose a civil action by one of the police officers, which alleged inadequate training, violated the defendant's right to all evidence that was material to guilt or punishment. Accordingly, the court remanded the case for a sentencing retrial.

Hamilton Amusement Ctr. v. Verniero


A-64 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


156 N.J. 254; 716 A.2d 1137; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 628


January 22, 1998, Argued  
July 21, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied June 21, 1999, Reported at: 527 U.S. 1021, 119 S. Ct. 2365, 144 L. Ed. 2d 770, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 4212.
Writ of certiorari denied Hamilton Amusement Ctr. v. Verniero, 527 U.S. 1021, 119 S. Ct. 2365, 144 L. Ed. 2d 770, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 4212 (1999)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 298 N.J. Super. 230, 689 A.2d 201 (1997).
Hamilton Amusement Ctr. v. Poritz, 298 N.J. Super. 230, 689 A.2d 201, 1997 N.J. Super. LEXIS 94 (App.Div.1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sexually oriented businesses challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which found N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-7c did not violate federal or state constitutional guarantees of freedom of speech and that the statute was not void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sexually oriented businesses challenged the constitutionality of a provision of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-7, which regulated signage for their businesses. The trial court determined that § 2C:34-7c was a content-based restriction on speech, applied strict scrutiny, and found the provision unconstitutional under N.J. Const. art. I, P 6. The appellate division reversed, finding that the statute targeted only commercial speech, which was not subject to strict scrutiny. The court affirmed the appellate court decision, concluding that because the statute only applied to commercial speech, an intermediate level of scrutiny was required, and using such a level of scrutiny, the state met its required burden of demonstrating that the statute advanced a legitimate state interest and was narrowly tailored to serve that interest. The court also held that the statute did not violate equal protection guarantees, was not void for vagueness, and was not a prior restraint on speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division, holding that, under an intermediate level of scrutiny, the state showed that the statute advanced a strong governmental interest and that the statute was narrowly tailored to support the state's asserted interests. Furthermore, the statute was not unconstitutional for vagueness, was not a prior restraint on speech, and did not violate equal protection guarantees.

State v. One 1990 Honda Accord


A-122 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


154 N.J. 373; 712 A.2d 1148; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 610


March 16, 1998, Argued  
July 15, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by State v. One 1990 Honda Accord, 156 N.J. 378, 718 A.2d 1207, 1998 N.J. LEXIS 1402 (1998)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 302 N.J. Super. 225, 695 A.2d 303 (1997).
State v. One 1990 Honda Accord, 302 N.J. Super. 225, 695 A.2d 303, 1997 N.J. Super. LEXIS 288 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), finding that automobile owner was entitled to jury trial before forfeiture of her automobile as innocent property and that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:64-3f was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state brought a civil in rem action under the Forfeiture Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:64-1-:64-9, seeking forfeiture of an automobile used by owner's son for illegal activities. The automobile's owner counterclaimed for a declaration that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:64-3f, which provided for forfeiture without a jury trial, was unconstitutional. The lower court found that the owner was entitled to a jury trial and that the forfeiture provision was unconstitutional. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed, finding that historically, cases involving the forfeiture of innocent property generally resulted in a trial by jury. The court found that actions to forfeit automobiles should be treated similarly as actions to forfeit other kinds of innocent property, such as ships, which always were subject to trial by jury. The inconvenience to the state when it sought to forfeit innocent property was not a justification for the denial of a constitutional right to a jury trial of N.J. Const. art 1, para. 9.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision upon review brought by appellant state, finding that automobile owner was entitled to a jury trial in forfeiture of her automobile because the provision for forfeiture of innocent property without a trial by jury was unconstitutional.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court (New Jersey), Appellate Division, which denied the appellant parole and deferred his future parole eligibility for 10 years.


OVERVIEW: Based on one count of murder, the appellant received a sentence under the predecessor criminal statute to the New Jersey Code of Criminal Justice. Subsequently, the parole board denied the appellant parole and deferred his future parole eligibility for 10 years. The intermediate appellate court affirmed the parole board's decision. On review, the court set aside the portion of the intermediate appellate court's decision, insofar as it sustained the board's final decision denying the appellant parole and fixing an extended future parole eligibility date. The court explained that the parole board's ultimate determination of parole fitness must be based on whether there was a likelihood that the appellant would again engage in criminal activity. The court found that it was unclear whether the parole board's findings of fact were adequately tailored to the correct test and whether the underlying evidence was sufficient to support a determination conforming to that test. The court remanded the case to the intermediate appellate court with instructions that the intermediate appellate court direct the board to redetermine appellant's parole eligibility.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the portion of the intermediate appellate court's decision, which denied the appellant parole and deferred reconsideration of his eligibility for parole for a period of 10 years. The court remanded the case to the intermediate appellate court and instructed the intermediate appellate court to direct the parole board to reconsider the appellant's parole eligibility based on appellant's rehabilitation.

State v. Black


A-53 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


153 N.J. 438; 710 A.2d 428; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 447


November 17, 1997, Argued  
May 14, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 295 N.J. Super. 453, 685 A.2d 485 (1996).
State v. Black, 295 N.J. Super. 453, 685 A.2d 485, 1996 N.J. Super. LEXIS 444 (App.Div., 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant violated the conditions of his parole by failing to report to his parole officer, failing to refrain from drug use, and relocating his residence to another state, his parole was revoked.. He was also convicted of third-degree absconding from parole pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2c:29-5b. He appealed to the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), and the appellate court affirmed. He then sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's parole was revoked, and he was convicted for third-degree absconding from parole pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2c:29-5b. The appellate court affirmed, and defendant sought review. The court affirmed holding that parole revocation under the Parole Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30:4-123.60 to -123.62, was remedial and rehabilitative in both its essential purpose and its essential effect, and thus, it could not be viewed as punishment triggering the protections against double jeopardy of the state and federal constitutions. The court held that because the purpose of the parole revocation proceeding was not punitive, the fact that defendant faced what could be viewed as multiple sanctions as a result of the same, or substantially the same, conduct did not violate the constitutional protections against double jeopardy. The court held that the imposition of both sanctions was not fundamentally unfair, and thus declined to apply the doctrine of fundamental fairness to prohibit what the constitutional protections against double jeopardy did not.


OUTCOME: Revocation of defendant's parole and his conviction for absconding from parole were affirmed because the parole revocation was rehabilitative in nature and did not trigger double jeopardy protections, the fact that defendant faced the same sanctions as a result of his conduct did not violate double jeopardy protections, and the imposition of both punishments was not fundamentally unfair.

Mehlman v. Mobil Oil Corp.


A-5 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


153 N.J. 163; 707 A.2d 1000; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 242; 13 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1441


September 9, 1997, Argued  
March 26, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Mehlman v. Mobil Oil Corp., 291 N.J. Super. 98, 676 A.2d 1143, 1996 N.J. Super. LEXIS 243 (App.Div., 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant oil company and its workers appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which, inter alia, reversed a judgment notwithstanding the verdict and reinstated a verdict for plaintiff former employee on a claim under the Conscientious Employee Protection Act (CEPA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:19-1 et seq., which was brought after he was fired for objecting to the sale in Japan of gasoline with excess benzene.


OVERVIEW: After defendant oil company fired him, plaintiff former employee sued it and defendant workers for, inter alia, retaliatory discharge, in violation of public policy and the Conscientious Employee Protection Act (CEPA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:19-1 et seq. The evidence at trial showed that, although his job evaluations had always been positive, plaintiff was fired after he expressed objections to the sales in Japan of gasoline containing more than five percent benzene, which scientific evidence showed to be hazardous to humans. The jury found for plaintiff, but the trial court entered a judgment notwithstanding the verdict for defendants. The appellate division reversed and reinstated the jury verdict. Defendants appealed. In affirming, the court ruled that evidence of a Japanese regulatory prohibition on sales of high-benzene gasoline supported the finding that plaintiff's discharge was contrary to a clear mandate of public policy, which was a question of law under CEPA. Moreover, the extraterritorial application of CEPA was not an unconstitutional regulation of foreign commerce and did not render CEPA vague and overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the evidence supported the verdict that plaintiff was wrongfully discharged in retaliation for objecting to defendant's sale in Japan of hazardous gasoline containing more than five percent benzene, which was, as a matter of law under CEPA, contrary to a clear mandate of public policy under Japanese regulations and because the extraterritorial application of CEPA was not unconstitutional.

Karins v. Atl.City


A-6 September Term 1997


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


152 N.J. 532; 706 A.2d 706; 1998 N.J. LEXIS 91; 13 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1303; 76 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 97


October 20, 1997, Argued  
February 18, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant firefighter sought review by the administrative law office of appellee city's decision suspending him without pay. The administrative law judge found in appellant's favor, and the merit system board adopted the ALJ's findings, determining appellant had not violated specified employment rules and regulations of appellee, which determination was affirmed by the Superior Court (New Jersey).


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment, which affirmed the merit system board's determination that appellant firefighter had not violated specified employment rules and regulations of appellee city when, while off-duty, appellant uttered a racial epithet to a police officer while appellant was being interviewed during a drunk driving stop. The court held that appellee's regulation prohibiting unbecoming conduct was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad because the regulation was clearly aimed at conduct, rather than speech, and implicitly excluded constitutionally-protected conduct. Appellant's racial slur was not protected by the free speech clause of U.S. Const. amend. I because it was not even remotely related to any matter of public concern, and appellee's interest in maintaining order and a professional working relationship between the police and fire departments substantially outweighed appellant's right to make abusive, insulting, racially-motivated comments. Appellant was properly disciplined because such conduct adversely affected the morale, public trust, and confidence necessary to the effective operation of both the police and fire departments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court because the racial epithet uttered by appellant was not protected speech, the rules and regulations he was found to have violated were not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad, and he was properly disciplined.

State v. P.Z.


A-21 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


152 N.J. 86; 703 A.2d 901; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 530


October 8, 1996, Argued  
November 26, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 285 N.J. Super. 219, 666 A.2d 1000 (1995).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the Superior Court (New Jersey) affirming the order suppressing respondent father's inculpatory statement admitting that he had shaken and injured his infant daughter.


OVERVIEW: Respondent father, while under investigation for child abuse by child protective services, and possibly subject to criminal prosecution for the same abusive behavior, admitted to his caseworker that he had shaken and injured his infant. The trial court ruled that his inculpatory statement was inadmissible in his criminal trial and the lower appellate court affirmed. On appeal the court reversed, establishing that respondent was not in custody when he was questioned by his caseworker. Rather, the circumstances surrounding his interview failed to demonstrate a coercive atmosphere as respondent was interviewed at home, during the day, with his father nearby. The court explained that although respondent was free to remain silent and to insist upon his lawyer's presence, the caseworker was not required to stop the interview when respondent stated that he had been advised not to discuss the matter. The reference to his attorney did not, in this setting, invoke his right to remain silent such that the caseworker was required to end the interview. Further, the court found, respondent was not illegally asked to choose between his children and the exercise of his right to remain silent.


OUTCOME: The judgment affirming the order of the trial court suppressing respondent father's inculpatory statement was reversed because respondent's statement was not constitutionally protected where his interview by a child protective services caseworker was noncustodial and noncoercive.

State ex rel. J.G.


A-74 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


151 N.J. 565; 701 A.2d 1260; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 331


January 6, 1997, Argued  
September 25, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 289 N.J. Super. 575, 674 A.2d 625 (1996).
State ex rel. J.G., 289 N.J. Super. 575, 674 A.2d 625, 1996 N.J. Super. LEXIS 163 (App.Div., 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), reversing the finding that the statutes mandating serological testing for sexual assault perpetrators was unconstitutional as an impingement upon privacy, search and seizure, and due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant were charged with and pled guilty to juvenile delinquency for sexually assaulting a mentally retarded girl. The state moved, at the victim's request, for orders compelling appellants to submit to AIDS testing. Appellants contended that the statutes mandating serological testing were unconstitutional because they impinged upon their due process and search and seizure rights. The trial court found that the tests constituted a search within the meaning of U.S. Const. amend. IV and that the statutes at issue were unconstitutional. The lower appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed holding that such testing and disclosure of an assailant's AIDS status could provide information that was helpful to victims of sexual assault. Because the dissemination of test results was carefully restricted by statute, the court found that it was reasonable to require assailants to submit to this intrusion upon their privacy. However, the court modified the judgment by requiring that before testing could be ordered, the lower court must find probable cause to believe that the victim had been exposed to a risk that transmission of the AIDS virus may have occurred.


OUTCOME: The order reversing the finding that state's statutes mandating AIDS testing for perpetrators of sexual assault was unconstitutional was affirmed because such testing was beneficial to victims; however, the order was modified to ensure that the lower courts only mandate testing where there was probable cause to believe that the victim had been exposed to a risk of AIDS transmission.

 

31 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


N.J. Transit PBA Local 304 v. N.J. Transit Corp.


A-130 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


151 N.J. 531; 701 A.2d 1243; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 330; 13 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 449


April 28, 1997, Argued  
September 25, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 290 N.J. Super. 406, 675 A.2d 1180 (1996).
New Jersey Transit PBA Local 304 v. New Jersey Transit Corp., 290 N.J. Super. 406, 675 A.2d 1180, 1996 N.J. Super. LEXIS 214 (App.Div., 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff transit union local sought certified review of a decision from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), granting defendant state transit agency summary judgment as to plaintiff's claim that defendant's mandatory random drug testing policy violated the right of plaintiff's members, transit police officers, to be free of unreasonable searches and seizures under N. J. Constitution art. 1, para. 7.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff transit union local challenged the appellate court's decision granting summary judgment to defendant state transit agency as to plaintiff's constitutional challenge to defendant's new mandatory random drug testing policy. Defendant adopted the new policy to comply with federal regulations passed pursuant to the Omnibus Transportation Employee Testing Act of 1991, 49 U.S.C.S. § 5331(b), requiring testing of employees such as plaintiff's members, transit police officers, that were responsible for safety-sensitive functions. On appeal, the state's highest court held that random drug testing did not violate the state or federal constitutions. As employees of a highly regulated industry, plaintiff's members were subject to suspicionless drug testing. Although requiring urine samples was the equivalent of conducting warrantless searches, defendant's random drug testing policy was carefully drawn to protect plaintiff's members' privacy rights. The test results were kept confidential. The government had a compelling state interest in insuring that armed transit officers posed no safety threat to the public. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The state's highest court affirmed the appellate court's decision granting defendant state transit agency summary judgment and holding that plaintiff transit union local's armed transit officer members could be constitutionally subjected to mandatory random drug testing by defendant pursuant to a state policy based upon federal regulations. The state's compelling state interest in public safety outweighed plaintiff's members' privacy concerns.

State v. Cooper


A-99/100 September Term 1996

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant directly appealed his conviction for capital murder and sentence of death, pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(3), entered in the Superior Court (New Jersey).


OVERVIEW: Defendant lured a six-year-old girl playing in her backyard away from the other children, picked her up, and walked away with her. After she was reported missing, the police found her under the porch of an abandoned house where defendant lived, strangled to death and sexually assaulted. Defendant was arrested, tried and convicted of kidnapping, aggravated sexual assault, felony murder, and purposeful-or-knowing murder and sentenced to death. OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and sentence of death. Except for the aggravated-sexual-assault conviction the court held must merge with the kidnapping conviction, all of defendant's convictions and capital and noncapital sentences were affirmed. The court further granted defendant's request that the court conduct a proportionality review of his death sentence.

South Jersey Catholic Sch. Teachers Org. v. St. Teresa of the Infant Jesus Church Elem. Sch.


A-120 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


150 N.J. 575; 696 A.2d 709; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 224; 155 L.R.R.M. 2972; 135 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,340


March 18, 1997, Argued  
July 24, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 290 N.J. Super. 359, 675 A.2d 1155 (1996).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church-operated elementary school sought review of the order of the Superior Court (New Jersey) reversing the finding that appellant could not be compelled to collectively bargain with respondent labor organization due to the Free Exercise and Establishment clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Lay teachers in appellant church-operated elementary school sought to organize and engage in collective bargaining through respondent labor organization with respect to the secular terms and conditions of their employment. The trial court refused to compel appellant to bargain with respondent citing the Free Exercise and Establishment clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I. The lower appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed and remanded for a representational election, holding that the lay teachers had a constitutional right to unionize and to engage in collective bargaining. The scope of the negotiations, however, was limited by the Religion Clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I to secular terms or conditions of employment. Such activity, the court held, would not inhibit appellant's religion by interfering with issues of structure and indoctrination. By limiting the scope of collective bargaining to secular issues, the court explained, religion was neither advanced nor inhibited. Finally, the court reasoned that the compelling state interest of protecting the right to organize outweighed the burden on appellant's free exercise rights.


OUTCOME: The order reversing the finding that appellant church-operated elementary school could not be compelled to bargain with respondent labor organization was affirmed because requiring appellant to bargain collectively over secular terms and conditions of employment did not violate the Religion Clauses of the U.S. Constitution. The case was remanded for an order to hold an official representational election.

State v. Chew


A-77 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


150 N.J. 30; 695 A.2d 1301; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 182


October 22, 1996, Argued  
June 26, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied December 6, 1999, Reported at: 528 U.S. 1052, 120 S.Ct 593, 145 L. Ed. 2d 493, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 8116.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County (New Jersey), which found him guilty of murder and sentenced him to death, arguing the police violated his Miranda rights and right against self-incrimination; that he was denied his right to have the jury consider the non-capital verdict of murder as an accomplice; and that the N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3(c)(4)(d) aggravating factor was not applicable.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed his female companion after taking out a large insurance policy. After the victim was discovered, the police arrested defendant and interrogated him without giving him his Miranda rights. Thereafter, the police gave defendant his Miranda rights, and defendant subsequently made incriminating statements. Defendant was convicted of murder, and the jury sentenced him to death. The jury found applicable the aggravated factor under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3(c)(4)(d) based on defendant's receipt of life insurance proceeds. Defendant challenged his conviction, and the court held that after defendant had invoked his right to remain silent and to have the assistance of counsel, he voluntarily initiated further conversation with police and waived the privilege against self-incrimination. The court also determined that the trial court correctly denied a jury instruction on murder as an accomplice, because the evidence did not support it. Finally, the court concluded that the aggravating factor was properly applied to the insurance proceeds defendant received on the victim's life.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, and held that defendant was properly convicted of murder and sentenced to death after the trial court properly refused a jury instruction on murder as an accomplice; defendant's incriminating statements to the police was properly admitted after he waived his right to remain silent; and the aggravated factor of murder for pecuniary gain applied to life insurance proceeds defendant received.

 

38 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hoffman


A-78 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


149 N.J. 564; 695 A.2d 236; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 183


January 21, 1997, Argued  
June 25, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Hoffman, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 533 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at: 290 N.J. Super. 588, 676 A.2d 565 (1996).
State v. Hoffman, 290 N.J. Super. 588, 676 A.2d 565, 1996 N.J. Super. LEXIS 204 (App.Div., 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a ruling that found appellee, a divorcee, violated the harassment statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-4(a), and a final domestic violence restraining order when he mailed, on two occasions, a torn-up support order to his former spouse.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state argued that mailings by appellee of a torn-up support order to his former spouse violated the harassment statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-4(a). The court disagreed, ruling that the manner of communication did not establish the requisite harassing intent to annoy or alarm. While evidence showed that appellee's purpose was to harass, as mailings had no legitimate purpose, his manner was not likely to cause alarm. In so ruling, the court declined to view the statute as constitutionally vague for failing to define the level of objectionable annoyance, because the state of mind the statute required was sufficiently clear. However, the provision was construed to encompass only those methods of communication that were invasive of the recipient's privacy. Nonintrusive speech did not lose its constitutional protection even if annoying, the court added, noting that the mailings did not include offensive language, occurred during convenient hours, and were in appellee's own name. Conversely, appellee did violate the contempt statute, § 2C:25-29(b), by making mailings that contravened his restraining order, as the order effectively barred any contact between former spouses.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed that appellee did not violate harassment statute by mailing torn-up support order to former spouse, because the mailing, while harassing in intent, did not invade the spouse's privacy, as it occurred during convenient hours and was in appellee's name, but judgment that appellee did not violate domestic violence restraining order by making the mailing reversed, as order effectively barred any contact between former spouses.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


149 N.J. 145; 693 A.2d 417; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 141

May 14, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected July 11, 1997.
On remand at Abbott by Abbott v. Burke, 1998 N.J. Super. LEXIS 25 (N.J. Super. Ct. Ch. Div., Jan. 22, 1998)
Request denied by, Request granted Abbott v. Burke, 172 N.J. 294, 798 A.2d 602, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 736 (2002)
Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Abbott v. Burke, 2003 N.J. LEXIS 461 (N.J., Apr. 29, 2003)

Abbott by Abbott v. Burke, 136 N.J. 444, 643 A.2d 575, 1994 N.J. LEXIS 624 (1994)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, children in special needs school districts, brought a motion for judicial relief arguing that the Comprehensive Educational Improvement and Financing Act of 1996, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:7F-1 - 7F-33, failed to assure them a thorough and efficient education.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were children attending public schools in school districts located in poor urban areas, classified as "special needs districts." The court previously held that in the absence of legislation that would assure a constitutionally adequate education, plaintiffs were entitled to judicial relief directed toward the improvement of the educational opportunity available to them. Subsequently, plaintiffs brought a motion seeking judicial relief, contending that the Comprehensive Educational Improvement and Financing Act of 1996 (the Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:7F-1 - 7F-33, failed to assure them a thorough education. The court held that the Act was unconstitutional as applied to the special needs districts. The court reasoned that the new act was incapable of assuring opportunity for children in the special needs districts. Also, the court held that the supplemental aid provided by the Act bore no demonstrable relationship to the real needs of the plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for judicial relief, holding that the Comprehensive Educational Improvement and Financing Act was inadequate to meet plaintiffs' educational needs.

State v. Delibero


A-85 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


149 N.J. 90; 692 A.2d 981; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 138


February 3, 1997, Argued  
May 8, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from the Superior Court Appellate Division (New Jersey) judgment reversing defendant's criminal convictions on the basis that the jury instructions on insanity impermissibly shifted the burden of proof to defendant to establish innocence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of several offenses related to burglary of a home, having pleaded both the insanity defense and diminished capacity. On appeal, defendant contended that the jury instructions regarding the insanity defense impermissibly shifted the burden of proof to defendant. In those instructions, the trial court directed that the jury consider the insanity defense after having determined defendant's guilt of the offenses. Defendant claimed that the jury was prevented from considering evidence of insanity when determining whether defendant had the requisite mental state to be guilty of the offenses. The appellate court reversed appellant's conviction and the state appealed. The court held that while the instructions could have been improved with regard to whether evidence of insanity could be applied to the issue of defendant's mental state, they did not impermissibly shift the burden of proof of innocence. The instructions, in their entirety, stressed the burden upon the state of proving guilt. The court reversed the appellate court judgment, and reinstated defendant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The court overturned the appellate court judgment reversing defendant's convictions on the basis of jury instructions regarding insanity. The court held that the presumption of innocence was not impermissibly shifted to defendant, and reinstated defendant's convictions. The matter was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

State Troopers Fraternal Ass'n v. State


A-59 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


149 N.J. 38; 692 A.2d 519; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 124; 156 L.R.R.M. 2824


January 6, 1997, Argued  
April 24, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State Troopers Fraternal Ass'n v. State, 145 N.J. 373, 678 A.2d 714, 1996 N.J. LEXIS 1335 (1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) that granted plaintiffs', state troopers that had resigned, retroactive salary increases under a collective bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: The labor contract expired under which State Troopers Fraternal Association worked. A regulation was passed, during collective negotiations, by the Department of Personnel, N.J. Admin. Code, tit.4A, § 3-4.20, that allowed retroactive pay adjustments only for current employees and employees who had retired or died during the negotiations. A collective bargaining agreement was then reached that provided for a retroactive pay increase. Plaintiffs, troopers that had resigned during the negotiations, sought the increase. The appellate court found in favor of plaintiffs. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court affirmed awarding retroactive pay increases to those troopers who resigned prior to the enactment of the regulation forbidding such increases. The court held that there was a prior practice of providing troopers with the retroactive increases, and those troopers that resigned prior to the regulation placed reasonable reliance on the continued existence of the practice. However, the court reversed the award granted to troopers who resigned after the regulation was enacted. The court held that those troopers could not have reasonably relied on the past practice.


OUTCOME: The court declined to provide all plaintiffs retroactive pay increases. The court affirmed the portion of the order granting troopers increases that had resigned prior to the enactment of the regulation prohibiting such increases. The court reversed that portion of the order that awarded increases to troopers that had resigned after the regulation's enactment.
