Nelson v. Bd. of Educ.


A-94/95 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


148 N.J. 358; 689 A.2d 1342; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 81


January 22, 1997, Argued  
March 12, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township board of education and the state board of education filed petitions for certification for review of an order of the Superior Court (New Jersey) that concluded that respondent teacher's tenure under the supervisor endorsement of his administrative certificate was transferable to the principal endorsement of that same certificate, despite the fact that he had never served in such capacity under his principal endorsement.


OVERVIEW: Respondent teacher possessed an administrative certificate with separate principal and supervisor endorsements, but had never served in the capacity of principal. After respondent was overlooked for a principal position, he filed a challenge claiming tenure rights to the position. The Administrative Law Judge (ALJ) granted summary judgment to respondent and ordered that he be given the principal position. The state commissioner of education reversed the ALJ's position and held that respondent had no tenure claim to the principal position pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18:28-5. Appellant township board of education adopted the commissioner's decision and the superior court then reversed. Upon the petitions for certification of appellant and the state board of education, the court reversed the superior court's judgment. The court stated that § 18:28-5 required service in the separately listed positions before tenure could be achieved in them. Moreover, the court stated that 1996 N.J. Laws c. 58 § 1, which amended § 18:28-5, required separate service in the positions and that the retroactive application of this amendment would not result in manifest injustice.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petitions for certification. The court reversed the superior court's order, which allowed respondent teacher to transfer his tenure rights under the supervisor endorsement of his administrative certificate to a principal endorsement of the same certificate. Pursuant to statutory law, the positions listed in respondent's certificate were separately tenurable and that respondent had no claim to the principal position.

State v. Marshall

SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


148 N.J. 89; 690 A.2d 1; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 70

Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 522 U.S. 850, 118 S. Ct. 140, 139 L. Ed. 2d 88, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 5191.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Atlantic County (New Jersey), convicting him of murdering and conspiring to murder his wife and sentencing him to die.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and given the death penalty. On direct appeal, the court affirmed the decision. Defendant filed a petition for post-conviction relief (PCR). The trial court denied defendant's PCR application and defendant appealed. On appeal, the court held that post-conviction relief proceedings were not a substitute for direct appeal. Moreover, the court rejected defendant's claims that the prosecution improperly withheld exculpatory evidence from defendant. The court held that there was no reasonable possibility that a different verdict would have arisen had the evidence been disclosed. Further, the court rejected defendant's claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. The court reasoned that few of the allegations of ineffective assistance at trial involved significant deficiencies in the quality of his counsel's representation, and those that did were not material to the trial result. The court affirmed the trial court's decision not to order the prosecution to allow him to inspect the prosecution's entire file. The court reasoned that the general discovery obligations did not extend to post conviction proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence for murder and affirmed the trial court's decision denying defendant's petition for post-conviction relief. The court found defendant's contentions of ineffectiveness of counsel, prosecutorial misconduct, and other claims were meritless. Further, the court found no error in the post-conviction proceedings.

Whitaker v. DeVilla


A-60/61/62 September Term 1996


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


147 N.J. 341; 687 A.2d 738; 1997 N.J. LEXIS 32


November 18, 1996, Argued  
February 3, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 287 N.J. Super. 370, 671 A.2d 163 (1996).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant driver, a resident of New Jersey, filed a petition for certification based on an order of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which reversed the lower court's grant of summary judgment for defendant and found that plaintiff motorist, a resident of Pennsylvania, was entitled to non-economic damages pursuant to the deemer statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:28-1.4.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff motorist, a resident of Pennsylvania, was in an automobile accident in New Jersey with defendant driver, a resident of New Jersey. Plaintiff brought an action against defendant seeking recovery for both economic and non-economic losses. Defendant filed a motion for summary judgment, and the trial court granted said motion, finding that pursuant to the deemer statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:28-1.4, plaintiff was not entitled to non-economic damages, because plaintiff's injuries did not satisfy the verbal threshold. Plaintiff appealed, and the appellate court reversed the decision. Defendant filed a petition for certification, and plaintiff filed a cross-petition challenging the constitutionality of the statute. The court found that the statute was a rational and understandable determination by the legislature to attempt to contain the cost of automobile insurance premiums for New Jersey residents. The statute's application did not implicate any fundamental constitutional right or any suspect or quasi-suspect classification. Therefore, the appellate court's order was reversed.


OUTCOME: The order finding that plaintiff motorist was entitled to non-economic damages was reversed, because the deemer statute, under which plaintiff was not entitled to non-economic damages, was constitutional, in that it was a rational attempt to contain the cost of automobile insurance premiums for New Jersey residents.

Strasenburgh v. Straubmuller


A-137/159/169 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


146 N.J. 527; 683 A.2d 818; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 1076


April 29, 1996, Argued  
October 23, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division whose opinion is reported at 284 N.J. Super. 168, 664 A.2d 497 (1995) (Strasenburgh v. Straubmuller). On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (Wheaton, Inc. v. Smith). On certification to Superior Court, Chancery Division, Cumberland County (Wheaton, Inc. v. Smith).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two causes were appealed from the Superior Court (New Jersey) by plaintiff-appellant corporation seeking to dismiss an appeal and by defendant-appellant dissenting shareholders to establish a fair determination of their share-value rights as well as actions by minority shareholders seeking special injury claims against more senior respondent shareholders.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff-appellant corporation transferred all of its assets to wholly-owned subsidiaries. Defendant-appellant shareholders dissented and sought appraisal rights based upon that transfer. Plaintiff-appellant subsequently sought to rescind its action and contended that doing so would defeat appraisal rights. These actions as well as an action against defendant-respondent shareholders that defendant-respondent's were impeding the company's growth were consolidated on appeal to the supreme court. The supreme court found that plaintiff-appellant's attempted rescission of its action was delayed too long to be actionable without causing harm to defendant appellant shareholders. Plaintiff-appellant's motion to dismiss its appeal and defendant-appellant's request to affirm rulings below each would result in appraisal proceedings. Therefore, the rescission motion ruling and the retroactivity motion were affirmed. Actions by defendant-respondent shareholders impacted all shareholders similarly and did not, therefore, present a claim of special injury for individual shareholders. Therefore these claims as well as a federal Racketeer Influence and Corrupt Organizations claim were dismissed.


OUTCOME: Orders denying plaintiff-appellant corporation's motions to dismiss an appraisal action were affirmed and the order was remanded for further proceedings. Defendant-appellants dissenting shareholders' actions for determination of share-value rights was granted. Judgments regarding special injury and RICO violations were appropriately dismissed, as they were subject to derivative and not individual actions.

State v. Loftin


A-39 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


146 N.J. 295; 680 A.2d 677; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 970


April 30, 1996, Argued  
August 8, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his murder conviction from the Superior Court of Mercer County (New Jersey), which denied his pretrial motions, including a motion to strike aggravating factor N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(f) for murder to escape apprehension for another offense, and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his murder conviction. At the penalty-phase hearing, a separately empanelled jury returned a death-penalty verdict. The trial court denied defendant's pretrial motions including a motion to strike aggravating factor N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(f) for murder to escape apprehension for another offense, and sentenced him to death. The supreme court affirmed defendant's murder and related offenses convictions and his sentence of death. Death-penalty statute did not violate the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment contained in U.S. Const. amend. VIII. Defense counsel's strategic decision did not provide grounds for a finding of plain error. Trial court was correct in refusing to allow cross-examination about other perpetrators. Trial court's failure to provide instruction did not disturb defendant's conviction or sentence. The supreme court had carefully reviewed the errors identified in the course of the opinion. It was satisfied, in the context of a trial that produced overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt, that the combined effect of those errors was not clearly capable of affecting either defendant's convictions or his sentences.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's murder and related convictions and his sentence of death because it carefully reviewed the errors identified in the case, and it was satisfied that, in the context of a trial that produced overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt, the combined effect of those errors was not clearly capable of affecting either defendant's convictions or his sentences.

State v. Hightower


A-22 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


146 N.J. 239; 680 A.2d 649; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 972


November 6, 1995, Argued  
August 8, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington county.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Burlington County (New Jersey), which, after the court's original death sentence for capital murder was reversed, again entered a death sentence on remand.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was convicted of capital murder, the lower court entered a sentence of death, which the court reversed and remanded. At the second penalty trial, defendant was again sentenced to death. The court reversed and remanded for a new penalty phase, holding that juror misconduct during jury deliberations exposed the jury to extraneous influences. The court held that the introduction of victim impact information into the jury's deliberation had the clear capacity to prejudice defendant. The court held that the lower court abused its discretion in denying defendant's application for a mistrial. The court rejected defendant's claim that the New Jersey Death Penalty Act violated the U.S. Const. amend. VII. The court held that the trial court improperly expanded the scope of N.J. R. Crim. P. 1:8-2(d)'s inability-to-continue standard when it removed a juror from the deliberating jury. The court held that although the juror violated the oath by informing other jurors that the victim had children, the misconduct did not fall within the scope of the inability-to-continue standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the death sentence imposed upon defendant and remanded for a new penalty phase because juror misconduct during jury deliberations exposed the jury to extraneous influences that had the clear capacity to prejudice defendant.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This appeal challenged the decision of the Superior Court (New Jersey) that reversed a trial court judgment, which ordered the involuntary commitment of appellant individual to a state mental hospital. Appellant challenged the authority of the Attorney General to initiate and participate in commitment proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The trial court ordered appellant individual committed to a state mental hospital after the Attorney General sought and obtained court authorization to compel appellant to submit to psychiatric evaluations to determine whether appellant should undergo civil involuntary commitment proceedings. The appellate court reversed the decision which ordered appellant's initial temporary involuntary commitment. Appellant challenged the constitutional and statutory validity of the involuntary commitment and, particularly, the authority of the Attorney General to initiate and participate in commitment proceedings. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, holding that the trial court's determinations, initially to compel appellant to undergo psychiatric evaluation, and later for continued commitment, were adequately supported by the factual record. The court held that the authority conferred on the Attorney General to initiate commitment proceedings in the interest of public safety was intended to apply retroactively and was intended to apply to released convicted sexual offenders, like appellant, even though their release antedated the enactment of the amendments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which reversed the trial court's order involuntarily committing appellant individual because the trial court's determinations, initially to compel appellant to undergo psychiatric evaluation, and later for continued commitment, were adequately supported by the factual record.

In re N.N.


A-70 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


146 N.J. 112; 679 A.2d 1174; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 967


January 16, 1996, Argued  
August 5, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior court, Law Division, Cumberland County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor challenged the decision of a New Jersey trial court, which committed appellant to a psychiatric hospital after finding that appellant needed inpatient, intensive attention and because appellant was subject to, displayed, or had a disturbance of mood.


OVERVIEW: Appellant minor was committed to a psychiatric hospital after the lower court found that appellant needed inpatient, intensive attention because appellant was subject to, or did display, or did have a disturbance of mood. Appellant had originally entered the hospital voluntarily for a seven-day examination after an assaultive episode with another youth. The rule under which appellant was committed, provided that a minor could be involuntarily committed if the court found that the minor was in need of therapy that could not be provided anywhere but in a psychiatric hospital. The court reversed the judgment, holding that a standard that would authorize the involuntary commitment of a minor under fourteen years of age must first require a showing that the minor was mentally ill, as defined in terms of childhood mental illness. The court held that the evidence was insufficient to establish that appellant suffered a childhood mental illness and it was not established that appellant required intensive, institutional psychiatric therapy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment committing appellant minor to a psychiatric hospital because the evidence was insufficient to establish that appellant suffered a childhood mental illness, and it was not established that appellant required intensive, institutional psychiatric therapy.

State v. Kirk


A-130 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


145 N.J. 159; 678 A.2d 233; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 942


March 26, 1996, Argued  
July 10, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for certification and challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed defendant's sentence of an extended term under the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1987, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-6f. Defendant was sentenced to an extended term of 50 years of imprisonment with 25 years of parole eligibility for convictions for possession of cocaine and possession with intent to distribute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving co-defendant's car while co-defendant was a passenger when he was stopped by police for driving erratically. A search of the car revealed cocaine and a briefcase containing $ 8,000. A jury convicted defendant of third-degree possession of cocaine, a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-10a(1) and of first-degree possession of six and one-half pounds of cocaine with the intent to distribute, a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:35-5a(1), 2C:35-5b(1). Defendant had prior similar convictions in New York. The state's motion for an extended term sentence pursuant to the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1987, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-6f was granted and defendant was sentenced to an extended term of 50 years of imprisonment with 25 years of parole ineligibility. The appellate court reversed. The supreme court reversed and held that the state sufficiently explained its reasoning on the record and cited ample evidence demonstrating an escalating pattern of drug activity. However, the supreme court remanded and held that the trial court failed to impose a term of parole ineligibility that was not at or near the top of the range of the sentencing guidelines.


OUTCOME: The supreme court granted certification and reversed defendant's extended sentence under the Comprehensive Drug Reform Act of 1987 as to defendant's parole ineligibility because the parole ineligibility exceeded the statutory required minimum. The case was remanded to redetermine the term of parole ineligibility.

State v. Gerns


A-64 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


145 N.J. 216; 678 A.2d 634; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 897


January 2, 1996, Argued  
July 9, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which remanded for resentencing the judgment of the trial court convicting and sentencing defendant for distribution of marijuana, distribution of marijuana within 1,000 feet of school property, possession of marijuana with intent to distribute, and possession of marijuana with intent to distribute within 1,000 feet of school property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sold marijuana to an undercover detective. Defendant was indicted on various counts of distribution of, and possession with intent to distribute, marijuana, including counts on distribution and possession within 1,000 feet of school property. As part of a plea agreement, defendant executed a contract of cooperation with the State, under which it was understood that his cooperation could result in an unspecified shorter sentence, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-12. The prosecutor withheld any recommendation that defendant's term of parole ineligibility be shortened, and the trial court sentenced defendant accordingly. The appellate court reversed and remanded for resentencing, and the State sought review of its decision. The court reversed the appellate court's judgment. The court held that it was neither arbitrary nor capricious for a prosecutor to base the decision to recommend a waiver of the mandatory sentence required by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7 on the value of the cooperation received from a defendant. The court directed, though, that when such leniency was offered, the prosecutor was required to state precisely the sentence recommendation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which had remanded for resentencing the trial court's judgment sentencing defendant for distribution of and possession with intent to distribute marijuana, because the State was entitled to take into consideration the value of defendant's cooperation in recommending whether his sentence should be shortened under the statutory scheme.

State v. Kittrell


A-107 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


145 N.J. 112; 678 A.2d 209; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 891


February 13, 1996, Argued  
July 3, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Kittrell, 2008 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 655 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction for the offense of maintaining or operating a controlled dangerous substance production facility under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-4.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was repeatedly observed entering another's apartment, placing objects on a ledge outside the apartment, having short conversations with people when they left the building, then removing objects from the ledge and exchanging them for money. Defendant was arrested. A search of the apartment produced small packaged quantities of cocaine. The occupant of the apartment gave a statement incriminating defendant, but when called to testify repudiated it. Defendant was convicted and sentenced for maintaining or operating a controlled dangerous substance production facility under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-4 and related offenses. The appellate court affirmed the convictions, but ordered merger and found the sentence excessive. Defendant sought review. The court affirmed. The court stated that based on the plain language of § 2C:35-4, defendant's conduct fell within its purview, and that § 2C:35-4 was neither vague nor applicable only to "commercial" manufacturing facilities. The court found the evidence sufficient to sustain the conviction, and found no error in a properly qualified jury instruction that referred to moral certainty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court affirming defendant's conviction for maintaining or operating a controlled dangerous substance production facility, because defendant's conduct came within purview of the statute, the statute was neither vague nor restricted to large-scale operations, and there was no error in a jury instruction that referred to moral certainty in describing the reasonable doubt standard.

State v. Muhammad


A-138/A-139 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


145 N.J. 23; 678 A.2d 164; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 892


February 27, 1996, Argued  
June 28, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State sought clarification of the order of the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey), which declared N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(6) unconstitutional under the state and federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for several crimes, including capital murder, after he attacked a little girl. The state served notice of four aggravating factors. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(6), the victim impact statute under both the New Jersey and United States Constitutions. The trial court granted defendant's motion and declared the statute unconstitutional under both constitutions. The trial court, however, declined to reach the question of whether the New Jersey Constitution prohibited the use of victim impact evidence. The court rejected defendant's argument that the application of victim impact statute to him would violate the Ex Post Facto Clauses of the state and federal constitutions. On state's motion for clarification, the court found that the victim impact statute did not violate the federal or state constitution because it provided sufficient procedural safeguards for defendants. The court also found that applying the statute to defendant would not violate either ex post facto clause. The court reversed the trial court and allowed the introduction of victim impact evidence at the penalty stage of defendant's trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the holding of the trial court because the victim impact statute was constitutional under the state and federal constitutions. The court agreed that application of the statute to defendant did not violate any the Ex Post Facto clauses in either constitution. The court remanded for and allowed the state to introduce victim impact evidence at the penalty phase of defendant's trial.

A-164 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY

June 28, 1996, Decided

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public defender challenged the order of the Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County (New Jersey), which denied her motion to pursue post-conviction relief on behalf of defendant who was convicted for murder and sentenced to death and who refused to consent to such application for relief. The trial court ruled that defendant was competent to waive post-conviction proceedings.


OVERVIEW: After kidnapping and killing a victim, defendant was convicted of, among other offenses, purposeful and knowing murder and was sentenced him to death. His conviction and sentence were affirmed. Thereafter, appellant public defender appointed to represent defendant, applied for permission to pursue post-conviction relief on defendant's behalf over his objection, or for an evidentiary hearing on his competency, as well as for a stay of execution pending post-conviction relief proceedings. The trial court appointed independent counsel for him and a psychiatrist to determine his competence to waive post-conviction proceedings. After a hearing, the trial court ruled that defendant was competent to waive post-conviction proceedings, and that appellant could not seek post-conviction relief over defendant's objection. The supreme court reversed and held that even though it was satisfied that defendant was competent to make the decision to prosecute no further appeals, the supreme court had a constitutional responsibility to ensure reliability in the implementation of the death penalty. As such, the supreme court outlined an abbreviated post-conviction relief process and remanded.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the dismissal of appellant public defender's post-conviction relief application on behalf of defendant who was convicted of murder and sentenced to death and refused to consent to the application for such relief. The supreme court remanded for further abbreviated proceedings specifically outlined in the opinion to determine if any post-conviction relief was warranted.

OVERVIEW: Plaintiff salesperson petitioned for certification of a judgment from the lower court. Bunk v. Port Auth.


A-53/A-54 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


144 N.J. 176; 676 A.2d 118; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 618


January 2, 1996, Argued  
May 22, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 279 N.J. Super. 613, 653 A.2d 1170 (1995).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Port Authority sought review of the order of the Superior Court (New Jersey) granting petitioner individual a workers' compensation award when petitioner was receiving a disability retirement pension.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner individual sought workers' compensation benefits in New Jersey after being injured in New York while working for respondent Port Authority of New York and New Jersey. He applied for and was receiving Social Security disability benefits and a disability retirement pension from respondent. The trial court denied petitioner's request for a workers' compensation award because petitioner was receiving a disability retirement pension. The appellate court reversed the compensation judge's decision, and respondent sought review. The court reversed the appellate court's ruling and held that petitioner was barred from receiving compensation for his work injury under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:15-43. The court concluded that the legislative intent was that workers' compensation benefits that were afforded to respondent's employees were required to be integrated with the disability retirement benefits afforded to public employees for the same injury. The court reasoned that the only way to integrate petitioner's benefits was to apply the statutory bar. Then, petitioner was able to process his workers' compensation claim in New York.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's ruling because petitioner individual was barred from receiving a workers' compensation award for his work injury. The applicable statute required workers' compensation benefits that were afforded to respondent port authority's employees to be integrated with the disability retirement benefits afforded to public employees.

The court found that respondent's failure to disclose and explain items of his candidate statement demonstrated a lack of fitness for admission to the bar.

OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowners held land adjacent to which defendant school board began building a school. OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and affirmed in part, reinstating claims against defendants school board and city because some damages occurred within the time limitations and, on remand, plaintiffs were entitled to an opportunity to prove that the public entities were liable for maintaining a dangerous condition.

Rivkin v. Dover Township Rent Leveling Bd.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 277 N.J. Super. 559, 649 A.2d 1356 (1994).
GMC v. City of Linden, 143 N.J. 336, 671 A.2d 560, 1996 N.J. LEXIS 39 (1996)
Rivkin v. Dover Township Rent Leveling Bd., 277 N.J. Super. 559, 649 A.2d 1356, 1994 N.J. Super. LEXIS 494 (App.Div., 1994)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both parties appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a remand of a decision of appellee Rent Leveling Board to redetermine whether appellant property owners could increase rent in their mobile home park, and reversed a grant of attorney's fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 (1988).


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners applied to appellant Dover Township Rent Leveling Board for rental increases under Dover Code § 104-35 due to capital improvements they made. Appellee conducted four hearings on the application. The tenant member of appellee, a resident of appellants' park, challenged appellant's personally during the hearings and appellants requested that he be removed from the proceedings. appellee denied these requests and, granted only part of the increase, finding that the characterization of the improvements was improper. Appellants appealed challenged the decision as arbitrary and capricious and claimed a civil rights violation under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 (1988), seeking damages and attorney's fees. The trial court remanded the matter to appellee to be reconsidered without the tenant member. The trial court refused to review the substantive decision and awarded damages and attorney's fees. The appellate court affirmed the remand but denied section 1983 damages. Both parties appealed and the court affirmed finding that the tenant member's actions did create violations of due process or equal protection and appellant was not entitled to relief under section 1983.

61 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


GMC v. City of Linden


A-66/A-109 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


143 N.J. 336; 671 A.2d 560; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 39


October 24, 1995, Argued  
February 29, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 279 N.J. Super. 449, 653 A.2d 568 (1995).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city, tax assessor, and appraiser, sought review of a judgment entered by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which determined that the trial court had jurisdiction under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 to entertain plaintiff corporation's action alleging that defendants engaged in discriminatory conduct in the course of assessing plaintiff's property.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation filed suit under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 alleging that defendants, city, tax assessor, and appraiser, assessed its automobile assembly plant at an excessive rate and thereby violated its due process rights. Plaintiff sought compensatory and punitive damages. The trial court determined that it did not have jurisdiction over the action because plaintiff had an appeal pending in the tax court. The appellate court reversed, holding that the trial court had jurisdiction to entertain the action. The court reversed, concluding that state courts could not exercise jurisdiction over § 1983 claims arising from the assessment of a state tax if the taxpayer had an adequate, alternative state forum in which it could raise a constitutional challenge to the assessment, regardless of whether injunctive or monetary relief was sought. The court thus held that the trial court did not have jurisdiction over plaintiff's § 1983 claim because the State of New Jersey offered plaintiff an alternative avenue for challenging the constitutionality of defendant's assessment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and held that the trial court did not have jurisdiction to entertain plaintiff corporation's challenge under federal law to the constitutionality of the tax assessment made by defendants, city, tax assessor, and appraiser, because plaintiff had an adequate, alternative state forum for raising the constitutional challenge.

 

62 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sanchez


A-29 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


143 N.J. 273; 670 A.2d 535; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 8


October 10, 1995, Argued  
February 5, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the decision of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's conviction of defendant for second-degree burglary and related offenses, alleging that the lower court erred in determining that the trial court abused its discretion in denying defendant's severance motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his brother were charged with second-degree robbery and related offenses. The trial court denied defendant's motion for severance, even after defendant claimed that his brother would provide exculpatory testimony if the two were tried separately. Defendant and his brother were both convicted of the crimes charged. The lower court reversed defendant's conviction holding that severance was warranted because there was a substantial likelihood that exculpatory evidence would have been offered. The state appealed. The court determined that discrepancies in brothers' stories undermined the exculpatory value of the proffered testimony and supported the trial court's suspicion that the brothers were attempting to use the severance motion as a ploy to get one of them acquitted. Furthermore, the court concluded that because defendant failed to show that substantially exculpatory testimony would have been forthcoming had his severance motion been granted, he did not demonstrate prejudice sufficient to compel severance under N.J. Ct. R. 3: 15-2(b). Therefore, the trial court did not abuse its discretion. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision reversing the trial court's conviction of defendant and his brother for robbery and related offenses because the court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's motion for severance where defendant failed to demonstrate that substantially exculpatory evidence would have been given had his severance motion been granted.

 

63 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Fischer v. Canario


A-56/91 September Term 1995


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


143 N.J. 235; 670 A.2d 516; 1996 N.J. LEXIS 36


October 23, 1995, Argued  
January 30, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 277 N.J. Super 302, 649 A.2d 875 (1994).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant doctor challenged the decision of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which entered a judgment in favor of appellee, son of decedent, in appellee's action against appellant for medical malpractice.


OVERVIEW: While decedent was hospitalized, an x-ray was taken of her chest, which revealed a tumor on decedent's chest. Appellant doctor did not see this x-ray and did not inform decedent of the tumor. After decedent's death, appellee, son of decedent, filed an action against appellee for medical malpractice. The jury entered a verdict in favor of appellee. Both parties appealed to the lower court. Appellant doctor argued that a judgment notwithstanding the verdict should have been entered in his favor because the evidence failed to establish that he had a duty to inform decedent about her chest x-ray. Appellee cross-appealed from the judgment contending that the trial court's denial of his request for an ultimate outcome charge was prejudicial error. The court reasoned that to allow full recovery for all of appellee's damages against a medical provider whose negligence caused appellee only a lost chance of recover was fundamentally unfair. Furthermore, the court determined that the trial court erred in denying appellee's request for an ultimate outcome charge, an such an error was misleading to the jury. The court reversed the lower court's judgment and ordered a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's verdict in favor of appellee, son of decedent, in appellee's medical malpractice action because the court determined that it was fundamentally unfair to allow full recovery against a medical provider whose negligence only caused a lost chance to recover.

Sherman v. Citibank (S.D.), N.A.


A-102 September Term 1994


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


143 N.J. 35; 668 A.2d 1036; 1995 N.J. LEXIS 1355


February 15, 1995, Argued  
November 28, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted June 10, 1996, Reported at: 517 U.S. 1241, 116 S. Ct. 2493, 135 L. Ed. 2d 186, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 3723.


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 272 N.J. Super. 435, 640 A.2d 325 (1994).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) which granted defendant's motion to dismiss in a case involving the New Jersey's Retail Installment Sales Act of 1960, (RISA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:16C-50, -54, and the National Bank Act, (NBA) 12 U.S.C.S. § 85.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff argued that the New Jersey's Retail Installment Sales Act of 1960, (RISA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:16C-50, -54 forbids national banks that issue credit-cards to consumers from charging late payment fees. Plaintiff also argued that defendant's failure to disclose in its card member agreements and advertising that late payment fees were prohibited by state law violated New Jersey's Consumer Fraud Act (CFA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 56:8-2, -19. Finally, plaintiff contended late payment fees constituted a common-law breach of contract and conversion. The court held the phrase "interest at a rate allowed by the laws of the State where the bank is located" referred only to the periodic percentage rate charged on outstanding balances. Therefore, plaintiff's state-law defenses to defendant's charges did not conflict with federal law, were not preempted, and late payment fees were illegal under state law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court stating that plaintiff's state-law defenses to defendant's charges did not conflict with federal law, were not preempted, and late payment fees were illegal under state law.

Silverman v. Berkson


A-97 September Term 1994


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


141 N.J. 412; 661 A.2d 1266; 1995 N.J. LEXIS 533


March 13, 1995, Argued  
August 2, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 276 N.J. Super. 6, 647 A.2d 160 (1994).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), in which the court quashed a subpoena issued by the New Jersey Bureau of Securities requiring defendant, a New York resident, to appear in New Jersey during an investigation of securities transaction involving New Jersey residents.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, the Chief of the New Jersey Bureau of Securities, issued a subpoena to defendant, a New York resident, in the course of investigating securities transactions involving New Jersey residents. Defendant declined to appear in response to the subpoena, and plaintiff sought a court order enforcing the subpoena. The trial court granted the order. The appellate division reversed. The court reversed the appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court. The court held that it was reasonable to infer that the New Jersey Legislature would intend that plaintiff's administrative power to compel the attendance of witnesses was coextensive with its substantive mandate to investigate securities transactions within or outside of New Jersey. The court stated that consistent with principles of due process, that authority would be limited to subpoenaing witnesses who had purposefully availed themselves of the privilege to enter the New Jersey securities market.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellate court and remanded the case to trial court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion. The held that plaintiff's administrative power to compel the attendance of witnesses was coextensive with its substantive mandate to investigate securities transactions within or outside of New Jersey.

Doe v. Poritz


A-170/171 September Term 1994


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


142 N.J. 1; 662 A.2d 367; 1995 N.J. LEXIS 519; 36 A.L.R.5th 711


May 2, 1995, Argued  
July 25, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 29, 1995. Second Correction November 8, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County, whose opinion is reported at     N.J. Super.     (1995).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, individually and on behalf of others similarly situated, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Burlington County (New Jersey), which held that two of the statutes generally referred to as Megan's Law, the Registration Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:7-1 to :7-5 and the Community Notification Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:7-6 to :7-11, were constitutional, and the court granted certification to the lower court.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged the constitutionality of two statutes concerning sex offenders that were enacted as part of a group of laws generally referred to as Megan's Law. The Registration Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:7-1 to :7-5, required that certain convicted sex offenders register with law enforcement authorities. The Community Notification Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:7-6 to :7-11, provided for notice to the community of the presence of such offenders. In ruling that both laws were constitutional, the court held that the constitution did not prevent society from attempting to protect itself from convicted sex offenders, so long as the means of protection were reasonably designed for that purpose, and only for that purpose, and were not designed to punish. The court found, however, that appellant had a protectable liberty interest in his privacy and reputation, which triggered the right to due process. The court, therefore, concluded that a judicial hearing was constitutionally mandated prior to community notification under the Community Notification Law.


OUTCOME: The court held that both the Registration Law and the Community Notification Law were constitutional, but that the prosecutor's decision to provide community notification, which included the manner of notification, was subject to judicial review before such notification was given, and that such review was constitutionally required.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY

March 13, 1995, Argued  
July 24, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Companion case at Rendine v. Pantzer, 141 N.J. 292, 661 A.2d 1202, 1995 N.J. LEXIS 521 (1995)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 276 N.J. Super. 11, 647 A.2d 162 (1994).
Szczepanski v. Newcomb Hosp. Medical Ctr., 276 N.J. Super. 11, 647 A.2d 162, 1994 N.J. Super. LEXIS 359 (App.Div., 1994)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, medical center and nurse placement corporation, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded for a full and fresh reconsideration of appellee employee's application for counsel fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellee employee prevailed in a Law Against Discrimination, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-12d action against appellants, medical center and nurse placement corporation. Appellee prevailed in the underlying action and applied for attorney's fees pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-27.1. The trial court found that the contingent fee agreement between appellee and her attorney served as the upper limit on the fee that the court could award. The appellate court reversed and remanded for a complete consideration of the application, not to be limited by the contingent fee agreement. On certification, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, and reasoned that the reasonable fee award authorized by statute should not have been limited to the amount payable under the contingent fee agreement, nor should it have been proportionate to the damages recovered in the litigation. The court ruled that to so limit the fee award would dampen attorneys' enthusiasm for the vindication of civil rights. Moreover, the court held that the trial court should exercise its discretion to exclude from the lodestar calculation hours for which appellee counsel's documentary support was marginal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for a full assessment of what was a reasonable attorney's fee under the circumstances; notwithstanding the contractual fee agreement between appellee employee and her counsel.

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by, Sub nomine at Mocco v. Ashton, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 50645 (D.N.J., July 25, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 268 N.J. Super. 72, 632 A.2d 1222 (1993).

State v. Harris


A-3 September Term 1994


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


141 N.J. 525; 662 A.2d 333; 1995 N.J. LEXIS 321

July 12, 1995, Decided

On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Morris County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder and other felony offenses and was sentenced by the jury to death. Defendant appealed the convictions and death sentence entered by the Superior Court, Morris County (New Jersey) on grounds that included incorrect jury instructions on the state's burden to prove murder, the aggravated and mitigated factors considered in sentencing, and other claimed errors by the trial court.

State v. Reyes


A-85 September Term 1994


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


140 N.J. 344; 658 A.2d 1218; 1995 N.J. LEXIS 259


January 31, 1995, Argued  
June 5, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Reyes, 117 N.J. 634, 569 A.2d 1335, 1989 N.J. LEXIS 1304 (1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed defendant's conviction and granted a new trial on defendant's motion for post-conviction relief that alleged errors in the issuance of a diminished capacity jury charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of numerous burglary, murder, assault, and weapons possession charges after he broke into an apartment where his former girlfriend was living, killed the former girlfriend's roommate, and assaulted the former girlfriend, her sister, and her sister's boyfriend. On direct appeal, defendant challenged the jury charge on voluntary intoxication, and the intermediate court found no error and affirmed the conviction. On his motion for post-conviction relief, defendant challenged the diminished capacity charge and alleged ineffective assistance. The trial court denied relief, but the intermediate court reversed and granted a new trial. The court granted plaintiff's petition for certification and reversed. The court found that the trial court's diminished capacity charge reflected the controlling law at the time and emphasized plaintiff's burden to prove intent beyond a reasonable doubt. Although the law changed after defendant's trial and lessened the burden on defendants, any error in the charge was harmless because defendant was not entitled to it. Any error in the charge's failure to comport with the new standard was also harmless absent supporting evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment that found defendant was entitled to a new trial on his claims of an erroneous diminished capacity jury charge and ineffective assistance of counsel. Any error in the trial court's diminished capacity charge was harmless because defendant was not entitled to such a charge under the old law, and evidence did not support the requisite standard under the new law.

Resolution Trust Corp. v. Lanzaro


A-53 September Term 1994


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


140 N.J. 244; 658 A.2d 282; 1995 N.J. LEXIS 105


November 28, 1994, Argued  
May 15, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 271 N.J. Super. 189, 638 A.2d 812 (1994).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Resolution Trust Corporation, as receiver for savings bank, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the finding of the trial court that appellant had to pay appellee sheriff's fees from the execution sale of property secured by mortgages held by appellant where appellant was the successful bidder at sale.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Resolution Trust Corporation, in its capacity as receiver, filed a complaint for foreclosure, and appellee sheriff conducted the sale. Appellant was the successful bidder, and appellee assessed a fee pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 22A:4-8. The trial court found for appellee, and stated that the fee was not a tax and appellant was not exempt. The appellate court affirmed for the same reasons. On appeal, the court reversed and found for appellant. The court held that congress specifically exempted appellant from state and local taxation in respect to its acquisition of assets from failed thrift institutions. Pursuant to the Financial Institutions Reform, Recovery and Enforcement Act of 1989, 12 U.S.C.S.§ 1441a(b)(4), appellant was authorized to exercise all of the powers conferred on the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC) by the Federal Deposit Insurance Act, 12 U.S.C.S. §§ 1821, 1822, 1823. The court held that appellant was exempt from state and local taxation to the same extent as the FDIC pursuant to 12 U.S.C.S.§ 1825(b), and because the fee in question far exceeded the cost to appellee it was, therefore, a tax.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment for appellant Resolution Trust Corporation, and held that because congress had specifically exempted appellant from state and local taxation in respect to its acquisition of assets from failed thrift institutions the charge to have been imposed by appellee sheriff was essentially equivalent to a tax measured by the sale price of the foreclosed property and was, therefore, unlawfully imposed.

 

72 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Hovbilt, Inc. v. Twp. of Howell


A-9


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


138 N.J. 598; 651 A.2d 77; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 1176


September 26, 1994, Argued  
December 22, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 263 N.J. Super. 567, 623 A.2d 770 (1993).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed the tax court's decision that respondent township's error of misplacing appellant's application for farmland assessment on his real property was not correctable under the Correction of Errors Statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:51A-7.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer had received an agricultural exemption on his property for five years. In the sixth year, appellant prepared and filed his application for farmland assessment, but respondent township misplaced the application and no agricultural exemption was granted. Appellant sought to correct the mistake under the Correction of Errors Statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:51A-7 (the statute). The court, on appeal, affirmed the lower court's judgment and found that such an error was not correctable under the statute and that only mistakes that were indispensable and that could not plausibly be explained on the basis of tax assessor judgment or discretion were within the category of mistakes covered by the statute. The court further found that the correct assessment under the statute must have been readily inferable or have been subject to ready calculation on the basis on the assessment mistake and that appellant was not guaranteed that his property would again qualify as farmland, but merely wanted his property assessed and such did not fall within the statute.


OUTCOME: The court, on appeal, affirmed the lower court's judgment that appellant taxpayer's complaint against respondent township was not correctable under the Correction of Errors Statute because only mistakes in assessment that were indisputable, and not plausibly explained on the basis on an exercise of judgment were covered by the statute and there was no proof that appellant's property would have otherwise qualified for farmland exemption.

State v. Martini


A-16 September Term 1993


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


139 N.J. 3; 651 A.2d 949; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 1285


January 19, 1994, Argued  
December 21, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On proportionality review of a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by a jury in the Superior Court, Bergen County (New Jersey), for purposeful or knowing murder by his own conduct, felony murder, kidnapping and two weapons offenses, and alleged disproportionality of his death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant kidnapped a businessman and, after being paid $ 25,000 in ransom by the man's wife, defendant shot the man at close range and killed him. Defendant was subsequently found guilty by a jury of purposeful or knowing murder by his own conduct, felony murder, kidnapping and two weapons offenses and was sentenced to death, as well as other consecutive and concurrent sentences. Defendant alleged disproportionality of his death sentence and, on appeal, the court affirmed and held that there was no disproportionality in the death sentence. The court utilized the offender-oriented proportionality review which included frequency analysis and precedent-seeking review and found that defendant's case was reviewable in a universe of cases comprised of stranger kidnapping, contract and pecuniary-advantage killer cases in which the death penalty was sought. A high likelihood existed that the death penalty would be imposed for the type of murder that defendant committed because the aggravating factors outweighed any mitigating factors considered by the jury. Defendant failed in his burden to show an aberration in the result of or the presence of impermissible factors in the penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence because defendant failed to carry his burden to prove disproportionality in light of the comparison of similar death-penalty cases which included stranger kidnapping, contract and pecuniary advantage killer cases.

N.J. Coalition Against War in the Middle E. v. J.M.B. Realty Corp.


A-124/125 September Term 1993


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


138 N.J. 326; 650 A.2d 757; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 1283; 52 A.L.R.5th 777


March 14, 1994, Argued  
December 20, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 266 N.J. Super. 159, 628 A.2d 1075 (1993).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant anti-war coalition sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's holding that appellants did not have a constitutional right to freedom of speech for leafletting on societal issues on the premises of respondent shopping centers.


OVERVIEW: Appellant anti-war coalition was denied the right to leaflet at respondent shopping centers and sought judicial relief to force respondents to allow them to do so. The trial court denied appellants relief, finding that there existed no free speech right on respondents' private property and the appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed and held that regional shopping centers, such as respondents, were required to allow such speech activities under N.J. Const. art. I, § 6. The court found that there was no such right protected by U.S. Const. amend. I, but that the New Jersey provision was not restricted to government interference and that private property owners were required to allow political speech on their premises under certain circumstances. The public nature of regional shopping centers and their usurpation of the downtown business district were factors the court relied upon in its decision. The court held that leafletting and its accompanying speech were protected at regional shopping centers, but not smaller centers, and that respondents were permitted to institute appropriate restrictions on such activity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and held that respondent shopping centers, and all such regional shopping centers, were required to allow political speech in the form of leafletting and speech in support thereof on their premises with proper restrictions.

Murray v. Lawson


A-42


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


138 N.J. 206; 649 A.2d 1253; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 1168


November 7, 1994, Argued  
December 1, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States, whose order is reported at     U.S.     (1994).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the United States Supreme Court, appellant anti-abortion protestors challenged the issuance of an injunction that prohibited appellants from picketing within 300 feet of respondents, doctor and wife's, residence.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were anti-abortion protestors who were picketing in front of respondents', doctor and wife, residence; respondent doctor preformed abortions. The court, on remand from the United States Supreme Court, affirmed, as modified, a permanent injunction against appellants. The court, which found that the 300-foot restriction burdened more speech than was necessary and did not meet the mandates of a previous United States Supreme Court decision, reduced the distance to 100-feet. The court found that the injunction was not a pre-publication prior restraint or the result of a discriminatory licensing or regulatory system and did not forbid appellants from expressing their opinion or restrict appellant's activities merely because of the position that their message adopted. The court found that even if the injunction was a prior restraint, respondents were a captive audience with protectable privacy interests in their home and therefore public interest required protection of them as unwilling listeners.


OUTCOME: The court modified and affirmed the permanent injunction against appellant protestors, and prohibited any picketing within 100 feet of respondents', doctor and wife, residence, limited the size of the protestors to no more than 10 people for one hour every two weeks, and ordered appellant's to notify the local police in advance of any intended picketing, because respondents had a substantial, protectable privacy interest.

Desilets ex rel. Desilets v. Clearview Reg'l Bd. of Educ.


A-133 September Term 1993

May 3, 1994, Argued  
September 22, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 266 N.J. Super. 531, 630 A.2d 333 (1993).
Desilets ex rel. Desilets v. Clearview Regional Bd. of Educ., 266 N.J. Super. 531, 630 A.2d 333, 1993 N.J. Super. LEXIS 736 (App.Div., 1993)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, school board and principal, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that appellant principal's refusal to publish respondent student's movie reviews in the school paper violated respondent's rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. The superior court affirmed the trial court's judgment, but not its reasoning, which was that only respondent's state constitutional rights were violated.


OVERVIEW: Respondent student challenged appellant principal's decision not to publish two reviews of R-rated movies in the school paper. The trial court determined that the decision violated respondent's state constitutional rights. The court of appeals determined that respondent's rights under U.S. Const. amend. I were violated because appellants, school board and principal, failed to establish pedagogical interests that justified a restriction on respondent's free speech. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the school paper was not a public forum, but that appellants had not established a legitimate educational policy regarding publication of reviews of R-rated movies, and the reviews in dispute were merely brief descriptions with terse recommendations. Because appellants failed to establish legitimate pedagogical concerns regarding publication of the reviews, respondent's free expression rights were violated. The court did not reach the state constitutional claim, and noted that the Commissioner of Education had jurisdiction to consider claims such as this one.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' decision that appellant principal's refusal to publish respondent student's movie reviews violated respondent's First Amendment rights. While the school paper was not a public forum, the school did not have a legitimate educational policy dealing with R-rated movies, and appellants, school board and principal, did not establish legitimate pedagogical concerns over the publication of the reviews.

State v. Maldonado


A-25, A-46 September Term 1992


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


137 N.J. 536; 645 A.2d 1165; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 641


October 13, 1992, Argued  
August 1, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought reviews of judgments by the Superior Court (New Jersey), which upheld defendants' convictions for violations of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-9, that imposed strict criminal liability on manufacturers and distributors of certain controlled substances when death resulted from the injection of the substances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's were both convicted in separate cases of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-9, that imposed strict criminal liability on manufacturers and distributors of certain controlled substances (CDSs) when death resulted from the injection of the CDSs. One defendant obtained heroin for her friend and his brother. Defendant's friend died after injection of the heroin. The other defendant was convicted when two individuals died after they swallowed cocaine distributed by defendant, to avoid detection by the police. On appeal, the court affirmed because the state had the authority to define a crime without proof of mens rea so long as it had a rational basis. The court held that the control of drug distribution that caused death was a rational basis for the statute. The court held that the statute did not involve cruel and unusual punishment because the punishment fit the crime and did not go beyond what was necessary to accomplish a legitimate objective. The court held § 2C:35-9 did not violate due process and was not vague by using the term "not too remote." The court held that the remoteness issue should be charged to the jury and its meaning explained and related to the facts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for violation of the drug death statute that imposed strict criminal liability on manufacturers and distributors of certain controlled dangerous substances (CDSs) when death resulted from the ingestion of the CDSs. The court held that the statute was constitutional in all respects.

Pizzo Mantin Group v. Twp. of Randolph


A-58, A-59 September Term 1993


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


137 N.J. 216; 645 A.2d 89; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 635


November 29, 1993, Argued  
July 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 261 N.J. Super. 659, 619 A.2d 676 (1993).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's affirmance of the rejection by defendants, board and township, of plaintiff's application to develop a subdivision. Plaintiff argued that defendants failed to apply the Municipal Land Use Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:55D-1-129, and zoning ordinances and had no authority to base their decision on general statutory principles and purposes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff developer brought an action against defendants, board and township, under the Municipal Land Use Law (MLUL), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:55D-1-129, which challenged the rejection of plaintiff's application to develop subdivision. Defendants held that the plan complied with the subdivision ordinance and required no variances but was environmentally unsound and unsuitable. The appellate court reversed the trial court's affirmance of defendants' decision. Defendants then adopted an ordinance to increase the minimum lot size, rendering plaintiff's tract nonconforming. The appellate court denied plaintiff's motion to preclude application of the ordinance. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court, remanded, and held that defendants were required but failed to apply the standards governing subdivisions under the MLUL and municipal zoning ordinances in denying plaintiff's application and had no authority to apply the standards relating to the general welfare or purposes under the MLUL or general principles of sound planning. Further, the absence of express standards could have invited inconsistency, encourage controversy, and have led to arbitrary action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's reversal of rejection by defendants, board and township, of plaintiff developer's application to develop a subdivision, and remanded to the trial court. Defendants failed to apply the standards prescribed by the applicable subdivision and zoning ordinances and were without authority to base their determination on standards relating to public welfare and purposes and general principles of sound planning.

State v. DiFrisco


A-98 September Term 1993


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


137 N.J. 434; 645 A.2d 734; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 637


May 3, 1994, Argued  
July 27, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at State v. DiFrisco, 142 N.J. 148, 662 A.2d 442, 1995 N.J. LEXIS 526 (1995)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by DiFrisco v. New Jersey, 516 U.S. 1129, 133 L. Ed. 2d 873, 116 S. Ct. 949, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1207 (1996)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.
State v. DiFrisco, 118 N.J. 253, 571 A.2d 914, 1990 N.J. LEXIS 21 (1990)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Essex County (New Jersey), which refused to allow defendant to withdraw his guilty plea. The jury found that the aggravating factor, murder for hire, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(d), outweighed the mitigating factors and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant plead guilty to murder and was sentenced by the trial judge to death. On defendant's first review the court remanded the case for a new penalty phase hearing and the trial court refused to allow defendant to withdraw his guilty plea. The jury returned a death-penalty verdict and found that the aggravating factor, murder for hire, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(d), outweighed the mitigating factors and the trial court again sentenced defendant to death. On appeal the court affirmed because defendant's guilty plea was knowingly and voluntarily made and defendant knew that he could be sentenced to death. Defendant's counsel's erroneous belief that defendant would not be sentenced to death on a guilty plea was not ineffective assistance of counsel. The court held that defendant did not show that a juror who was partial sat as a result of defendant's exhaustion of peremptory challenges. The court further held that the trial court should have instructed the jury that defendant's allocution could be considered as a mitigation factor, but the error was harmless. The court held that the trial court's requirement that the jury deliberate further was not an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and sentence of death because defendant's guilty plea was voluntary and the jury found that one of the aggravation factors, murder for hire, outweighed the mitigation factors. The trial court should have instructed the jury that defendant's allocution could be considered as a mitigating factor, but the error was harmless.

Abbott by Abbott v. Burke


A-140, A-141 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


136 N.J. 444; 643 A.2d 575; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 624


May 24, 1994, Argued  
July 12, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Motion granted by, Remanded by Abbott by Abbott v. Burke, 149 N.J. 145, 693 A.2d 417, 1997 N.J. LEXIS 141 (1997)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Mercer County.
Abbott v. Burke, 119 N.J. 287, 575 A.2d 359, 1990 N.J. LEXIS 64 (1990)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant and cross-respondent guardians ad litem and respondent and cross-appellant state officials sought review of a judgment by the Chancery Division (New Jersey), which declared unconstitutional the Quality Education Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 18A:7D-1-37. The chancery division had based its decision on the act's failure to assure parity of regular education expenditures between special needs districts and the more affluent districts.


OVERVIEW: Appellants and cross-respondents were guardians ad litem of school children in the state's special needs districts. Appellants had contended that the Quality Education Act (QEA), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 18A:7D-1-3, had failed to assure substantial equivalence between the special needs districts and the richer districts in expenditures per pupil in regular education. The chancery division had declared QEA unconstitutional. Both appellees and respondent state officials had appealed. The court affirmed the decision of the chancery division. The court's decision was based on QEA's failure to assure parity of regular education expenditures between the special needs districts and the more affluent districts. The court affirmed the decision, but retained jurisdiction because the state informed the court that it did not anticipate that the QEA would in its present form control financing of public education within the state. The court held that the goal of parity could not be expected unless special supplemental programs and services were identified and provided to the students in special needs districts. That without the programs, those children would not have a chance to achieve success.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the chancery division, which found that the Quality Education Act was unconstitutional, was affirmed, because the act had failed to assure substantial equivalence between the special needs districts and the richer districts in expenditures per pupil in regular education.

George Harms Constr. Co. v. N.J. Tpk. Auth.


A-113, A-114 September Term 1993


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


137 N.J. 8; 644 A.2d 76; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 627; 146 L.R.R.M. 3037


January 31, 1994, Argued  
July 7, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractor challenged the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which sustained the validity of the resolution of appellee state agency, which required the lowest responsible bidder of its turnpike widening project to enter into a project-labor agreement with a designated union. The superior court held that the resolution was not in conflict with the right to organize and bargain under N.J. Const. art. I, para. 19.


OVERVIEW: Appellee state agency sought bidding for a widening project and adopted a resolution to award the project to the lowest responsible bidder to enter into a project-labor agreement with a designated union. Appellant contractor challenged an appellate court's judgment sustaining the validity of the resolution. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and held the resolution was invalid as inconsistent with current state bidding laws and the policies underlying them. Such policies did not contemplate the use of public-labor agreements by state agencies such as appellee to require contractors of public contracts to hire members of only designated labor organizations at the exclusion of all others. The court found that project-labor agreements requiring the designation of an exclusive labor organization for public construction work contravened N.J. Const. art. I, para. 19, which guaranteed those in private employment the right to organize and bargain collectively, by impermissibly allowing the state to select the employees' bargaining representatives. Further, such agreement violated the policies of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40A:11-13 encouraging free, open, and competitive bidding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order sustaining appellee state agency's resolution requiring the lowest responsible bidder of a public works contract to enter into project-labor agreements with a certain designated labor union. The resolution was invalid under current state bidding laws, which did not authorize the use of such agreements, and contravened policies encouraging competition for public contracts.

 

83 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bey


A-1 September Term 1993


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


137 N.J. 334; 645 A.2d 685; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 633


September 14, 1993, Argued  
June 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Proportionality Review of a Sentence of Death of the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a proportionality review of a sentence of death from the Monmouth County Superior Court (New Jersey) claiming imposition of the death sentence, when compared to sentences imposed on other defendants in other cases, was irrational or aberrant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sexually assaulted and murdered two women. Separate juries sentenced defendant to death for each of the murders. In the relevant universe of 298 death-eligible cases, 125 of which proceeded to the penalty-trial phase, defendant was the only prior murderer whose death sentence was affirmed. That fact alone did not compel a finding that defendant's death sentence was disproportionate. The cases of other defendants differed significantly from defendant's case. The court concluded the death sentence in defendant's case was not aberrant. As culpable as other defendants with prior murder convictions were, a jury could find defendant, with two convictions for murder and aggravated sexual assault, was particularly deathworthy. The court did not find a pattern of life sentencing or the taint of an invidious factor that would require reversal. The court lacked enough cases to conclude with any degree of statistical reliability whether race was working impermissibly in death sentencing. The court recognized its method of comparison, such as the inclusion of cases in which the death sentence had been reversed, was not perfect.


OUTCOME: No disproportionality in the imposition of the death sentence was found. The death sentence imposed on a young African-American man was proportionate. After conducting frequency and precedent seeking approaches to compare other death penalty eligible cases, defendant's death sentence was found neither random nor aberrational. The court did not sense that some invidious factor tainted the appellant's sentencing process.
