State v. Mortimer


A-11 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


135 N.J. 517; 641 A.2d 257; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 427


October 12, 1993, Argued  
May 26, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification of the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is reversed, the indictment is reinstated, and the cause is remanded to the Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court certified directly to itself from the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, the State's appeal of a trial court order dismissing an indictment charging that defendant had violated N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-4 on grounds that the statute violated defendant's U.S. Const. amend. I rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to fourth-degree harassment, a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-4. He had been charged with having painted on the house of a family of Pakistani descent a scurrilous, offensive allusion that incorporated a reference to the tika, a mark on the forehead of some Hindus. Before sentencing, however, defendant moved to dismiss the indictment on the basis of a United States Supreme Court's decision, in which the court declared unconstitutional a St. Paul, Minnesota hate-crime ordinance. Defendant argued that the decision rendered the N.J. statute unconstitutional because that statute violated his U.S. Const. amend. I, XIV rights. The trial court agreed with defendant's reading of the supreme court decision and dismissed the charge. On direct certification, the court held that the statute was not unconstitutional because it prohibited conduct, not expressions. The court also concluded that the fact that the crime was motivated by hatred could be used as an enhancement of the crime. The court reversed the order of the trial court and reinstated the indictment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the indictment was reinstated, because the charging statute did not violate U.S. Const. amend I. The statute neither regulated protected expression nor impermissibly punished motive.

State v. Vawter


A-15 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


136 N.J. 56; 642 A.2d 349; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 430; 63 U.S.L.W. 2015


October 12, 1993, Argued  
May 26, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged a judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which denied their motion to dismiss counts one through eight of an indictment charging them with hate crimes. Defendants contended that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C: 33-10 and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C: 33-11 violated U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged under New Jersey so-called hate crime statutes, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-10 and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:33-11. Defendants contended that the charges were unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the indictment, and the lower court granted leave to appeal. The court granted defendants' motion for direct certification. The court concluded that § 2C:33-10 and § 2C: 33-11 were under-inclusive and thus impermissible. The court found that § 2C:33-10 and § 2C: 33-11 were not narrowly tailored and where other content-neutral alternatives existed, a statute directed at disfavored topics was impermissible. The court determined that inasmuch as the language of § 2C:33-10 and § 2C:33-11 limited their scope to the disfavored topics of race, color, creed, and religion, they offended U.S. Const. amend. I.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which denied defendants' motion to dismiss counts of the indictment relating the New Jersey hate crimes statutes, and remanded the case for dismissal of such counts and for further proceedings. Statutes were limited to disfavored topics and offended First Amendment of United States Constitution because they failed strict scrutiny, as they were not narrowly drawn to achieve compelling state interest.

State v. Szemple


A-25 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


135 N.J. 406; 640 A.2d 817; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 411


October 12, 1993, Argued  
May 12, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 263 N.J. Super. 98 (1993).



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's order that denied defendant's motion for mistrial after determining that neither the marital-communications privilege nor the priest-penitent privilege protected admissions made by the defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder. The appellate court reversed the trial court's order that denied defendant's motion for a mistrial after the trial court determined that two statements made by defendant were not protected by any privilege. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court. Defendant's wife's father found a letter defendant wrote to his wife which contained a description of the murder defendant was charged with. The court held that the marital privilege, N. J. R. Evid. 28, did not apply to the letter because the confidential nature of the letter was destroyed when the wife's father found the letter. Defendant also confessed to the murder to a clergyman. The court held that the clergyman held the priest-penitent privilege, N. J. R. Evid. Rule 37 (now N. J. R. Evid. 50), and not the penitent, and that the clergyman could unilaterally waive the privilege without the consent of the penitent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court because the trial court correctly determined that a letter written by defendant to his wife was no longer privileged once the wife's father found the letter and that a clergyman could unilaterally waive the priest-penitent privilege.

Chase Manhattan Bank v. Josephson


A-22 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


135 N.J. 209; 638 A.2d 1301; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 297


October 13, 1993, Argued  
April 13, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 261 N.J. Super. 428 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant tenants sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which affirmed the lower court's order for possession in favor of plaintiff mortgagee, who foreclosed on the property that defendants occupied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant tenants rented a single-family for many years, first on a written lease basis then on an oral agreement month-to-month basis. The owner sold the property and the mortgagors financed the purchase in part with a loan from plaintiff mortgagee. The mortgagors subsequently defaulted on the loan and plaintiff filed a complaint to foreclose the mortgage. Instead of seeking a default judgment and order for eviction against defendants, plaintiff moved for summary judgment. The lower court granted plaintiff's motion and defendants appealed. The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and defendants sought review. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court. Defendants contended that the amended Anti-Eviction Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. tit. 2A, §§ 18-61.1 to 61.12, prohibited the court from entering an order for possession for plaintiff absent a showing of statutory good cause. N.J. Stat. Ann. tit. 2A, § 18-61.3b, applied the Act to foreclosing mortgagees. The court held that, as amended, the Act protected tenants from eviction by foreclosing mortgagees irrespective of whether their tenancy was established before or after the execution of the mortgage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court that had affirmed the order of the lower court. The court was convinced that the injury from a possible impairment to resale value because mortgagees' residential property would be of greater value in vacant condition than if occupied by tenants was substantially outweighed by the public concerns that the Anti-Eviction Act addressed.

Murray v. Lawson


A-42, A-65 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


136 N.J. 32; 642 A.2d 338; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 182; 62 U.S.L.W. 2647


October 25, 1993, Argued  
April 6, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinions are reported at 264 N.J. Super. 17 (1993) and 264 N.J. Super. 11 (1993).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants anti-abortion protestors challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which forbade defendants from picketing within the immediate vicinity of plaintiff doctors' residences.


OVERVIEW: Defendant anti-abortion protestors challenged the lower court's judgment that affirmed the order forbidding defendants from picketing within 300 feet of one of the plaintiff doctor's residence and within the immediate vicinity of the other plaintiff doctor's residence. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment upholding the first injunction and modified the lower court's judgment in the second injunction and remanded for a clarification of the restrictions contained in its injunction. The court held that the court had the power to enjoin the non-violent, non-criminal activity of defendants to protect plaintiffs' residential privacy provided the restrictions balanced defendants' free-speech rights and plaintiffs' residential-privacy interests. The court held that the injunctions were content neutral, and that a significant government interest existed in favor of protection of residential privacy, which justified the imposition of injunctive restrictions. The 300-foot restriction was narrowly tailored to protect plaintiffs' residential privacy, but the description within the immediate vicinity of contained in the second injunction was neither specific nor reasonably detailed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that forbade defendants from picketing within the immediate vicinity of plaintiff doctors' residences, upholding one injunction and modifying and remanding the second injunction for a clarification of the restrictions contained in its injunction. The court held that the court had the power to enjoin the non-violent, non-criminal activity of defendants to protect plaintiffs' residential privacy.

In re Adoption of Regulations Governing the State Health Plan


A-29 September Term 1993


Supreme Court of New Jersey


135 N.J. 24; 637 A.2d 1246; 1994 N.J. LEXIS 172


September 27, 1993, Argued  
February 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 262 N.J. Super. 469 (1993).
In re Adoption of Regulations Governing the State Health Plan, N.J.A.C., 262 N.J. Super. 469, 621 A.2d 484, 1993 N.J. Super. LEXIS 50 (App.Div., 1993)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On certification from the Superior Court (New Jersey), the court considered the question of whether the legislature may adopt a statutory amendment that prohibited an agency from adopting regulations that conflict with the amendment to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 26:2H-5.8(a), a subsection of the Health Care Cost Reduction Act.


OVERVIEW: The general issue was whether the legislature could adopt a statutory amendment that prohibited an agency from adopting regulations that conflicted with the amendment. More specifically, the issue concerned the constitutionality of an amendment to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 26:2H-5.8(a), a subsection of the Health Care Cost Reduction Act. The amendment restricted the authority of the Department of Health (DOH) to adopt regulations pertaining to the State Health Plan. The court held that as between the legislature and the DOH, the legislature had the power to formulate state policy on the controversial issue of health-care planning. The express statutory standards, the delegated authority to adopt non-conflicting regulations, and the permission to consider the State Health Plan constituted sufficient safeguards to withstand the DOH's due-process challenge.


OUTCOME: The court held that as between the legislature and the Department of Health, the legislature had the power to formulate state policy on the controversial issue of health-care planning. Therefore, the amendment to the Health Care Cost Reduction Act was properly adopted by the legislature and was not unconstitutional.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed his conviction for violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-3, known as the drug kingpin statute.

Defendant participated in four drug transactions with an undercover officer. While speaking with defendant, the undercover officer noticed significant traffic in and out of the house. The court affirmed the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appeals court upholding defendant's conviction because the state's evidence demonstrated that defendant had performed acts clearly prohibited by the drug kingpin statute.

State v. Patton


A-94 September Term 1992


Supreme Court of New Jersey


133 N.J. 389; 627 A.2d 1112; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 743


February 16, 1993, Argued  
August 3, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 256 N.J. Super. 413, 607 A.2d 191, 1992 N.J. Super. LEXIS 187 (1992).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision from the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which reversed defendant's conviction and reinstated an acquittal. It found that defendant's assertion of the privilege against self-incrimination was a complete defense to prosecution under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-10(c). The trial court convicted defendant of failing to deliver a controlled dangerous substance to a law-enforcement officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of failing to deliver a controlled dangerous substance to a law enforcement officer in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-10(c). The trial court granted defendant's motion in arrest of judgment and held that § 2C:35-10(c) impermissibly compromised defendant's privilege against self-incrimination. The state appealed, and the trial court reinstated the conviction. A lower appellate court reversed the conviction and reinstated the acquittal. Upon review, the court reversed. The court held that § 2C:35-10(c) was not unconstitutional because it provided transactional immunity for § 25:35-10(c) offenses to persons complying with its provisions, and use and derivative-use immunity in respect to all other offenses. Defendant's conviction was not barred by assertion of his privilege against self-incrimination where, if he had voluntarily surrendered to law-enforcement officers the cocaine unlawfully in his possession, the cocaine would not have been admissible in subsequent prosecution. The court also noted that the state would have had to prove that any other evidence admitted against defendant was unrelated to the surrender of the cocaine.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a decision that had reversed defendant's conviction of failing to deliver a controlled dangerous substance to a law-enforcement officer. The court found defendant's conviction was not barred by his assertion of his privilege against self-incrimination. The court found that the statute under which defendant was convicted was constitutional because it provided certain transaction and derivative-use immunities.

 

95 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Galloway


A-55 September Term 1992


Supreme Court of New Jersey


133 N.J. 631; 628 A.2d 735; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 745


November 30, 1992, Argued  
August 2, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the Appellate Division, which sustained the convictions for murder and third-degree child endangerment, and remand the matter for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which sustained defendant's convictions for murder and third-degree child endangerment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was taking care of his girlfriend's child. When the child started crying, defendant shook the child causing the child's death. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which sustained defendant's convictions for murder and third-degree child endangerment. The court held that the trial court's jury instruction which placed on defendant, who was asserting the defense of diminished capacity, the burden of proof to establish the existence of a mental disease violated defendant's due process rights. The court held that defendant presented sufficient expert testimony to warrant a jury instruction on diminished capacity because defendant presented evidence that his faculties were sufficiently affected to preclude him from engaging in purposeful or knowing conduct when he harmed the infant. The court held that defendant's confession was voluntary even though the police used psychological pressure and lied to convince defendant to confess. The court held that the evidence was not sufficient to justify submission of a third-degree child endangerment charge to the jury because defendant did not have a general and ongoing responsibility for the child.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court that sustained defendant's convictions for murder and third-degree child endangerment and remanded the case because the trial court improperly placed the burden on defendant to prove the existence of a mental defect, defendant was entitled to a jury instruction on diminished capacity, and the evidence was insufficient to justify submission of a third-degree child endangerment charge.

Herman v. Sunshine Chem. Specialties


A-101 September Term 1992


Supreme Court of New Jersey


133 N.J. 329; 627 A.2d 1081; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 727; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P13,568


February 16, 1993, Argued  
July 28, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 257 N.J. Super. 533 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is reversed and the judgment of the Law Division is reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, independent contractor and spouse, challenged an order from the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which reversed a judgment that awarded punitive damages to plaintiffs in a products liability action filed against defendants, manufacturer and distributor.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, independent contractor and spouse, filed a products liability action against defendants, manufacturer and distributor. Plaintiffs alleged that use of a cleaner caused plaintiff independent contractor to suffer from occupational asthma and that defendants knowingly placed the dangerous product into the stream of commerce. Following a jury verdict, the trial court awarded compensatory and punitive damages in favor of plaintiffs. The lower appellate court reversed the award of punitive damages. Upon review, the court reversed. The court found that plaintiffs had the burden of establishing the ability of defendant distributor to pay punitive damages because the absence of such evidence precluded the jury from having a proper foundation to assess damages. The court held that there was sufficient evidence of defendant distributor's financial condition where its president testified that the product in question was its best seller and that it accounted for one-third of its gross sales, and that he had sold the company's stock for $ 750,000. The court reasoned that the recent sale of the stock, in an arms-length transaction, was conclusive evidence of value.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order that reversed a judgment that awarded punitive damages to plaintiff independent contractor. The court held there was sufficient evidence of defendant distributor's financial condition for purposes of punitive damages where defendant's president testified regarding the company's gross sales and the amount for which he had recently sold the company stock.

OVERVIEW: Plaintiff police officer sought review of a decision dismissing him for insubordination, and the lower court affirmed the trial court's order affirming the dismissal. Members of defendant police department arrested plaintiff on suspicion of selling cocaine and directed him to provide a urine sample. Plaintiff refused to obey the order. Defendant dismissed plaintiff for insubordination. On further appeal, the court found that that the individualized-reasonable-suspicion standard sufficiently restricted the discretion of police supervisors to protect officers against arbitrary and discriminatory testing. The court found no testimonial compulsion in the collection and analysis of urine for illegal drugs. Further the court held that plaintiff had not carried his burden of persuasion that confusion over the privilege against self-incrimination led him to refuse to provide a urine sample for a drug test. Finally, the court held that the attorney-client privilege did not apply to plaintiff's statements to a union representative. The court affirmed the lower court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment because plaintiff police officer's refusal was not justified by reliance on his rights under the Fourth or Fifth Amendments, and his conduct constituted insubordination and justified his dismissal from defendant police department.

Lewis v. Lewis


A-113 September Term 1992


Supreme Court of New Jersey


132 N.J. 541; 626 A.2d 422; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 577


March 15, 1993, Argued  
July 1, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.



DISPOSITION: Consequently, we reverse the judgment awarding fees because of defendant's allegedly-frivolous cross-motion and remand the matter to the Chancery Division, Family Part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant husband sought a review of an order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed an order of the lower court pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A: 15-59.1 requiring defendant to pay plaintiff wife's lawyers the costs associated with the motions to enforce plaintiff's rights in a spousal support action.


OVERVIEW: After plaintiff wife filed motions to enforce her rights in a spousal support action, defendant husband filed a cross motion in opposition to the amount of spousal support, which had been awarded to plaintiff. However, the court reversed and remanded the order of the superior court holding that § 2A: 15-59.1 only applied to complaints, counterclaims, cross-claims, and defenses. OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded an order of the superior court, which affirmed on order of the lower court requiring defendant husband to pay plaintiff wife's lawyers the costs associated with the motions of a suit to enforce plaintiff's rights in a spousal support action. The statute under which defendant was ordered to pay plaintiff lawyers did not apply to the cross motions, which were filed by defendant.

McKeown-Brand v. Trump Castle Hotel & Casino


A-88 September Term 1992


Supreme Court of New Jersey


132 N.J. 546; 626 A.2d 425; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 578; 127 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P57,663


November 10, 1992, Argued  
July 1, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued March 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Reargued March 15, 1993. On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee appealed a decision by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a judgment that plaintiff's breach of contract complaint against defendant former employer violated N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:15-59.1b(2) by failing to state a cause of action that had a reasonable basis in law or equity and awarded defendant attorney's fees and costs.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the appellate division's ruling affirming a judgment that plaintiff former employee's action for breach of an employment contract against defendant former employer was a frivolous action in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:15-59.1 and awarding defendant attorney's fees. The court held that § 2A:15-59.1 applied sanctions to parties, but not to attorneys because applying it to attorneys would raise a question of whether the statute impinged on the court's exclusive power under N.J. Const. art. VI, § 2, para. 3 to discipline attorneys. The court found that § 2A:15-59.1 did not deny plaintiff due process because an application for counsel fees under it required a detailed affidavit of costs and had to be brought by a motion under N.J. Ct. R. 1:6, thereby providing the non-prevailing party with adequate notice of the claim. The court held that plaintiff did not act in bad faith as she made an honest attempt to press a perceived, if ill founded, claim and she relied on counsel's advice in good faith.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's decision and limited the application of a New Jersey statute proscribing frivolous lawsuits to parties while holding that it did not provide for the award of counsel's fees and costs against attorneys. The court held that plaintiff former employee did not act in bad faith in asserting her claim for breach of contract against defendant former employer.

Wyzykowski v. Rizas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New Jersey


132 N.J. 509; 626 A.2d 406; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 1250


November 30, 1992, Argued  
June 29, 1993, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For modification and affirmance


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant former mayor, but mayor when he applied, challenged a judgment from the appellate court (New Jersey), which affirmed a decision of the lower court upholding the planning board's proceedings but invalidating his approvals for a construction project, because of conflicts of interest. Plaintiff property owners brought the original action, claiming the principal use of the property required a use variance from the board of adjustment.


OVERVIEW: In reconsidering defendant former mayor's application proposing a construction project and addressing plaintiff property owner's concerns, the court held that treating apartments as an accessory to commercial use was inappropriate because apartments were not a conditional use in the zone where they were proposed. The court found that the mayor's appointed building official should have been disqualified because he was acting in a quasi-judicial capacity. Because most New Jersey communities were governed by citizens of other callings who served their communities part time, the court asserted that they should not be deprived of the ordinary citizen's rights, but should also not create a pecuniary personal interest that conflicted with the fiduciary duty owed to the public. The court found that public skepticism and disillusionment were heightened by the timing and nature of the application and, therefore, the judgment of the appellate court invalidating the approvals was affirmed, but, modified because rather than remanding to the planning board, the court ordered that the remand be to the zoning board of adjustment where defendant could seek a variance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment that invalidated defendant former mayor's development approvals, as sought by plaintiff property owners, and modified the judgment remanding the matter to the planning board for a new application. Unless the ordinance was amended to permit residential apartments as a principal use in the zone where they were proposed, an application would have to be made to the zoning board of adjustment.

In re Comm'r of Insurance's Order Regarding the Rate Filing by the Mkt. Transition Facility


A-62 September Term 1992


Supreme Court of New Jersey


132 N.J. 209; 624 A.2d 565; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 102


January 4, 1993, Argued  
May 24, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 256 N.J. Super. 158, 606 A.2d 851 (1992).
In re Commissioner of Insurance's Order Regarding Rate Filing etc., 256 N.J. Super. 158, 606 A.2d 851, 1992 N.J. Super. LEXIS 168 (App.Div., 1992)



DISPOSITION: The Judgment of the Appellate Division is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Commissioner of Insurance, challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which invalidated appellant's order imposing transitional assessments on insurance companies for their asserted failure to meet quotas established by the Fair Automobile Insurance Reform Act of 1990, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 17:33B-1 to -63, and directed appellant to implement a rate increase assessment only.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, Commissioner of Insurance, challenged the lower court's order that denied him the authority to impose transitional assessments on insurance companies because of the insurance companies' asserted failure to meet depopulation quotas under the Fair Automobile Insurance Reform Act of 1990 (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 17:33B-1 to -63. The quotas were designed to transfer drivers from the Market Transition Facility (MTF), a government agency created to provide temporary automobile insurance coverage to high-risk drivers, into the private insurance market. The lower court directed appellant to implement the MTF rate increase he had already found to be necessary. The court affirmed. The court held that appellant lacked the authority to impose the transitional assessments either as a regulatory adjustment or as a revenue-raiser under the Act, and that the assessments were not sustainable as a penalty or enforcement measure. The intractable problem of providing insurance for high-risk drivers required legislation to resolve definitively the extent and measure of appellant's authority under the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that appellant, Commissioner of Insurance, lacked the authority under the Fair Automobile Insurance Reform Act (Act), to impose transitional assessments on insurance companies. The court held that the insurance companies' asserted failure to meet depopulation quotas established under the Act, did not justify appellant's assessments, but that under the Act, he could to implement a rate increase.

April 1, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinions are reported at 247 N.J. Super. 146 (1991) (In re Township of Warren); 247 N.J. Super. 186 (1991) (In re Township of Denville); and 247 N.J. Super. 203 (1991) (In re Borough of Roseland).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state public advocate sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed appellee state housing council's adoption of N.J. Admin. Code, tit. 5, § 92-15.1, an occupancy-preference regulation enacted pursuant to appellee's power under the Fair Housing Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:27D-301-329.


OVERVIEW: Appellee state housing council adopted a regulation that authorized municipalities that sought substantive certification of regional fair-share plans for low- and moderate-income housing to offer an occupancy preference. Pursuant to the Fair Housing Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:27D-314, appellee granted certification to six municipalities. Appellant state public advocate sought review and the appellate court affirmed. On review, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that appellee's adoption of the occupancy preference, in the context of its existing methodology for calculating and allocating indigenous, present, and regional need for affordable housing, was invalid because it was inconsistent with and undermined the methodology adopted by appellee for calculating and allocating regional fair share of low- and moderate-income housing. Further, the appellate court erred in its summary disposition of both of appellant's discrimination claims.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment of the trial court which affirmed appellee state housing council's adoption of an occupancy preference regulation because the regulation conflicted with the enabling statute's dictate that affordable housing be made available on a regional basis.

State v. Martini


A-126 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


131 N.J. 176; 619 A.2d 1208; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 33


May 5, 1992, Argued  
February 9, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court in Bergen County (New Jersey), which convicted defendant of capital murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for murder, felony murder, possession of a handgun with intent to use it unlawfully against another, kidnapping, and possession of a handgun without a permit. Defendant kidnapped the victim in an attempt to gain quick cash and then shot the victim in the back of the head three times when he thought that the victim might attempt to get away. A jury trial convicted defendant on all charges and sentenced him to death. On appeal, defendant argued over 25 points of error. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, but remanded the matter for proper sentencing on the kidnapping charge. The court held that the trial court had conducted a sufficiently thorough voir dire to probe into the potential biases of the prospective jurors, that the state was not required to make every aggravating factor a subject of the indictment, and that defendant's request to speak with his girlfriend prior to cooperating with police was not an indication of a desire to remain silent. The court also held that the same evidence could be used to prove multiple aggravating factors so long as the jury was properly instructed on their use.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting defendant of capital murder and sentencing him to death was affirmed but remanded for proper sentencing for kidnapping, because the trial court had conducted a sufficiently thorough probe for bias during voir dire that any defect in jury selection was harmless error and that the state was not required to charge defendant in the indictment with every aggravating factor used during the penalty phase of the trial.

State v. Shaw


A-32, A-33


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


131 N.J. 1; 618 A.2d 294; 1993 N.J. LEXIS 19


October 27, 1992, Argued  
January 12, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinions are reported at 253 N.J. Super. 187 (1991), and 253 N.J. Super. 197 (1991).
State v. Santiago, 253 N.J. Super. 197, 601 A.2d 714, 1991 N.J. Super. LEXIS 439 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1991)
State v. Shaw, 253 N.J. Super. 187, 601 A.2d 709, 1991 N.J. Super. LEXIS 412 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the decisions of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the sentences imposed on defendants by the trial court. The state asserted that the no-appearance/no-waiver agreement in defendants' plea bargains were enforceable and proper under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-12.


OVERVIEW: In separate cases defendants were charged with selling drugs within 1000 feet of a school pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7. Defendants entered into plea agreements that granted a waiver of the mandatory prison term in exchange for their appearance at the sentencing hearing. Both failed to appear, and the prosecutors withdrew the waivers of mandatory sentence. The trial courts sentenced the defendants with mandatory terms of parole ineligibility. The intermediate appellate courts reversed the imposition of the mandatory sentence and either imposed sentence or remanded for renegotiation of the plea agreement. On appeal the court reversed and remanded and held that the trial courts were required to determine whether the enforcement of the plea agreements would be fundamentally unfair. The trial courts were required to test the prosecutors' inclusion of the no-appearance/no-waiver provision in the plea agreements against the prosecutors' stated guidelines governing whether to grant or deny leniency. If defendants' failure to appear at sentencing warranted revocation of the waiver provision in the pleas, the trial courts were required to sentence in accordance with the drug laws.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that found the no-appearance/no-waiver agreements in defendants' plea bargain imposed an undue burden on judicial sentencing authority and held that the provisions were enforceable because once accepted by the trial court the plea agreement governed the sentencing. Remand was appropriate to determine whether enforcement of the agreements would be fundamentally unfair.

Communications Workers of Am. v. Florio


A-105/106 September Term 1992


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


130 N.J. 439; 617 A.2d 223; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 1081


October 13, 1992, Argued  
December 29, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Department of Personnel.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, state employees and legislators, sought a stay pending review of claims that scheduled layoffs by defendants, the governor of New Jersey and executive branch officials, were in violation of Appropriations Amendments Act, Senate Bill 996, which directed that personnel reductions were to be accomplished by layoffs of managerial and other exempt personnel outside the collective bargaining units.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, state employees and legislators, filed complaints against defendants, the governor of New Jersey and executive branch officials, seeking a judgment that defendants were in violation of provisions in the 1993 Fiscal Year Appropriations Act, as amended by Senate Bill 996, L. 1992, c. 99, which restricted layoffs of state personnel, and requesting a stay of scheduled layoffs. Plaintiffs' requests for a stay were denied by the appellate division; the cases were consolidated and certified for review. The court held that the Appropriations Amendments Act, Senate Bill 996, § 1 was void because it violated the separation-of-powers principle as embodied in N.J. Const. art. III, para. 1, by allowing the legislature to interfere excessively with the governor's constitutional authority to manage government. The court held that the 1993 Appropriations Act, Senate Bill 1000, L. 1992, c. 40, § 38 was also unconstitutional insofar as it purported to dictate to the executive whom he may and may not lay off. However, the discretionary guidance provisions of the Appropriations Act did not offend the separation-of-powers principle and were therefore constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the requests for a stay pending review of claims by plaintiffs, state employees and legislators, that defendants, the governor of New Jersey and executive branch officials, were in violation of legislation, which restricted layoffs to managerial and other exempt personnel outside the collective bargaining units. The court held that the legislation was void because it interfered with the governor's constitutional authority.

In re "plan for Orderly Withdrawal from N.J." of Twin City Fire Ins. Co.


A-101 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


129 N.J. 389; 609 A.2d 1248; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 419


March 17, 1992, Argued  
July 29, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Twin City Fire Ins. Co. v. Fortunato, 506 U.S. 1086, 113 S. Ct. 1066, 122 L. Ed. 2d 370, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 1003 (1993)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from and on certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 248 N.J. Super. 616 (1991).
In re "Plan for Orderly Withdrawal" of Twin City Fire Ins. Co., 248 N.J. Super. 616, 591 A.2d 1005, 1991 N.J. Super. LEXIS 201 (App.Div., 1991)



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment of the Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld the order issued by the Commissioner of Insurance conditioning the termination of its authority to write insurance in New Jersey.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurer challenged the order of the Commissioner of Insurance conditioning termination of its authority to write insurance. The intermediate appellate court upheld the order but remanded the matter for reconsideration. The commissioner approved petitioner's plan for withdrawal subject to various conditions. Petitioner challenged the "forfeiture condition," which required petitioner's affiliates to terminate business in New Jersey within five years, and the "new-business condition," which required petitioner to comply with the Reform Act during its withdrawal. The new-business condition did not violate due process rights because the justification for imposing that condition was compelling. Due process was not offended by requiring petitioner to comply with the Reform Act during its withdrawal. The regulatory interest was adequate to sustain the forfeiture condition against a substantive due process challenge. The conditions did not deny petitioner equal protection of the laws under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The forfeiture condition was not a compensable taking of property under either the federal or state constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the intermediate appellate court, which upheld the insurance commissioner's conditions upon petitioner insurer for terminating its authority to write insurance. The court found the conditions did not violate the due process or equal protection clauses of the state or federal constitutions and that the conditions did not amount to unconstitutional takings.

State v. Bey


A-51 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


129 N.J. 557; 610 A.2d 814; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 435


October 8, 1991, Argued  
July 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his sentence of death which had been determined by a jury in the Superior Court, Monmouth County (New Jersey), which was the result of his second sentencing. Dedfendant's first sentence of death was reversed and remanded.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death by a jury. On appeal, the conviction was affirmed but the sentence was reversed and remanded. At the second sentencing, the jury again returned a death sentence. On the second appeal, defendant challenged that the original conviction did not establish death eligibility, and that the errors in the sentencing proceeding required reversal. The court affirmed both the conviction and the sentence, agreeing with defendant that some errors were present, but their cumulative effect did not require reversal. Because the evidence showed that defendant intended death for his victim, the jury had a rational basis for its verdict. As for the sentencing, defendant's equal protection challenge to the jury venire failed as there was a fair cross section of the population, the aggravating circumstances were admissible, the admissibility of the mitigating evidence was ordered to be considered in the light most favorable to defendant; the prosecutorial misconduct was harmless, and he had the effective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction against defendant for murder, and the sentence of death. In its examination, the court found that, though errors had been committed, the cumulative effect was not such to call for reversal, and that the jury's verdict had a rational basis. The court also found no merit in defendant's claims against the racial composition of the jury and the presentation of aggravating and mitigating factors.

State v. Marshall


A-199 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


130 N.J. 109; 613 A.2d 1059; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 437


January 21, 1992, Argued  
July 28, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On proportionality review of a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for proportionality review of a death sentence pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3e that was imposed in the Superior Court of Atlantic County (New Jersey), which convicted defendant for the murder of his wife.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried and convicted of the murder of his wife by contract and was sentenced to death. Pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3e, defendant requested a proportionality review of his death sentence after the court affirmed defendant's conviction under a separate opinion. The court first gave structure to and defined the proportionality review process that it followed in reviewing defendant's petition. The court determined that it would use the information at its disposal to identify similar cases in terms of blameworthiness, then examine the prosecutorial and juries patterns for frequency or of frequency of death verdicts in similar cases, and then finally look at the comparison cases to identify disproportionality. The court found the process to be thorough and constitutional. From its analysis using the composite of the measures of frequency in the proportionality review process, the court affirmed defendant's death sentence because it concluded that capital death sentencing for contract murderers was not random or an aberration.


OUTCOME: Judgment sentencing defendant to death for the contract murder of his wife was affirmed, because the composite measures for the statutory proportionality review of death sentences showed that capital death sentencing for contract murderers was not random or an aberration.

Bernardsville Quarry v. Borough of Bernardsville


A-94/95 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


129 N.J. 221; 608 A.2d 1377; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 412


February 18, 1992, Argued  
July 23, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from and on certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is hereby affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff quarry and defendant borough petitioned and cross-petitioned for certification from a judgement of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's upholding of defendant's ordinance that required quarries to be licensed and prohibited quarrying operations below a certain depth.


OVERVIEW: Defendant borough enacted an ordinance regulating quarries that imposed a licensing requirement and limited the depth below which property could not be quarried. Plaintiff quarry commenced a suit, contending that the ordinance effectively prohibited its use of its property as a quarry thereby constituting a compensable governmental taking. The trial court ruled against plaintiff, but remanded for defendant's reconsideration. The appellate court affirmed. On the parties' petition and cross-petition for certification, the court affirmed. Defendant had the power to enact the licensing ordinance and to require the licensing of quarries. The evidence demonstrated a reasonable basis for defendant to believe that the operations of the quarry below the permissible level would create genuine and substantial risks of harm to the public and the environment, thus, the ordinance advanced a valid public purpose. Further, the interference with plaintiff's property interests was not excessive or unreasonable, nor did it deprive the property of substantial value or prevent its use for other economically viable purposes. Thus, the ordinance did not effectuate an unconstitutional taking of property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision upholding defendant borough's enactment of a quarry licensing and regulation ordinance because the ordinance was validly enacted to advance a public purpose and its application against plaintiff quarry did not operate as a governmental taking without due process because the interference with plaintiff's property was not unreasonable nor did it render it without value.

State v. Vasquez


A-86 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


129 N.J. 189; 609 A.2d 29; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 414


March 3, 1992, Argued  
July 22, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 250 N.J. Super. 457 (1991).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the judgment of the appellate division of a superior court (New Jersey), which instructed the superior court to resentence defendant for violation of probation without the parole disqualifier, which had been waived by the state at the original sentence as part of the recommended plea bargain.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered into a plea agreement for a school zone drug offense, which provided that the three-year parole ineligibility term fixed by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C: 35-7 would be waived according to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C: 35-12, and the trial court imposed a probationary term. Later, defendant pleaded guilty to probation violation, and the trial court imposed a four-year custodial term with three years of parole ineligibility. The appellate court remanded for resentencing without the period of parole ineligibility, and the court affirmed the judgment. The court remanded to the trial court for resentencing, because the trial court felt compelled to impose the parole disqualifier, and the statutes did not compel the imposition of a mandatory period of parole disqualification with respect to resentencing for the probation violation. The sentencing scheme applicable to school zone drug offenders did not preclude the imposition of a period of parole disqualification in conjunction with a sentence following violation of probation, which was a matter of sound judicial discretion to be exercised in appropriate cases based on adequate reasons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, vacated the sentence for violation of probation, and remanded to the trial court for resentencing, because the trial court was not compelled to impose a parole disqualifier upon resentence for the probation violation but had discretion to vary the disqualifier in balancing the factors.

Ran-Dav's County Kosher v. State


A-157 September Term 1990


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


129 N.J. 141; 608 A.2d 1353; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 415; 61 U.S.L.W. 2077


May 7, 1991, Argued  
July 22, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 243 N.J. Super. 232 (1990).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, purveyors of kosher foods, sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld regulations governing kosher products, N.J. Admin. Code tit. 13, § 45A-21.1 et seq, in an enforcement action brought against appellants. Appellants contended that the regulations violated the establishment clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 4.


OVERVIEW: The state brought an enforcement action against appellants, purveyors of kosher foods, for alleged violations of regulations governing the preparation, maintenance, and sale of kosher foods. N.J. Admin. Code tit. 13, § 45A-21.1 et seq. Appellants challenged the constitutionality of the regulations. The constitutional claims were brought before the appellate court while the trial court retained jurisdiction of the enforcement action. The appellate court upheld the regulations. The court reversed and remanded to the trial court for dismissal of the charges. The court held that the regulatory scheme violated the establishment clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I and N.J. Const. art I, para. 4. Applying the Lemon test, the court held that the regulations fostered excessive government entanglement with religion by requiring strict compliance with the laws and customs of the Orthodox Jewish religion and by involving the government in disputes over kosher laws. The regulations violated the effects prong of the test because the secular goal of protecting consumers from fraud could not be separated from the primary effect of promoting religious tenets.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded to the trial court for dismissal of the charges, which were brought against appellants, purveyors of kosher foods, for alleged violations of state regulations governing the preparation, maintenance, and sale of kosher food products. The court held that the regulatory scheme violated the establishment clauses of the federal and state constitutions.

State v. Cannon


A-104 September Term 1990


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


128 N.J. 546; 608 A.2d 341; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 403


January 2, 1991, Argued  
July 14, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 7, 2005.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, ISP Panel.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state appealed defendant's admission into Intensive Supervision Program (ISP) by the Superior Court, Law Division, ISP Panel (New Jersey), after she pleaded guilty to theft by deception, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:20-4.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to a second-degree crime and was sentenced to prison. Seven months later, she was released and admitted into New Jersey's Intensive Supervision Program (ISP), a sentencing alternative that released certain defendants from prison under strict supervision. Plaintiff state appealed defendant's ISP admission and the appellate court denied plaintiff's application for a stay. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court agreed to determine whether it had power to sentence first- and second-degree offenders to probationary terms in the face of ISP's inconsistency with N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:44-1d, which required that all such offenders be imprisoned. The enactment of ISP exceeded the court's power because it conflicted with a valid exercise of legislative power to determine punishment, thus ISP was an impermissible sentencing alternative. Although the legislature's subsequent support of ISP through continued appropriations was strong evidence of approval, it was insufficient to operate as an implicit repeal. In light of the executive and legislative support of ISP, the court suspended the effective date of its judgment to permit legislative action in the interim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's admission into Intensive Supervision Program (ISP), because despite her completion of the program which rendered the decision without practical effect as to her, ISP was an impermissible sentence as to first- and second-degree offenders, due to its conflict with a statute that mandated imprisonment for such offenders.

Phillips v. Curiale


A-93 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


128 N.J. 608; 608 A.2d 895; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 401


February 18, 1992, Argued  
July 13, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 245 N.J. Super. 418 (1991).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant soldier sought review of the decision from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which, on remand, affirmed the dismissal of his tort claim against appellee comrades. Appellant asserted that retroactive application of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 38A:13-1.2 was not required by the statute and was manifestly unjust.


OVERVIEW: Appellant soldier suffered a paralyzing injury while on military training maneuvers. He brought an action against appellee comrades, the State of New Jersey, and the manufacturer of the vehicle in which he was riding. The trial court dismissed appellees and the state. The court reversed and held that appellees were not immune from suit. In response, the state enacted N.J. Stat. Ann. § 38A:13-1.2 which granted immunity to national guard members for tort injuries. The trial court applied § 38A:13-1.2 retroactively and dismissed appellees from the action. The intermediate appellate court affirmed the decision. On further appeal, the court reversed the dismissal of appellees and held that the legislature did not intend retroactive application. The court applied a two-part test to determine whether the legislature intended retroactive application and then whether such application would result in either an unconstitutional interference with vested rights or a manifest injustice. The court concluded that retroactive application was not intended but delayed the decision to allow the legislature to clarify its intention.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that dismissed appellee comrades from the tort action brought by appellant soldier and held that a tort immunity statute did not apply to appellant's action because the legislature did not intend for the statute to have retroactive application.

Crespo v. Stapf


A-77 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


128 N.J. 351; 608 A.2d 241; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 392


January 6, 1992, Argued  
June 25, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 248 N.J. Super. 349 (1991).
Crespo v. Stapf, 248 N.J. Super. 349, 591 A.2d 277, 1991 N.J. Super. LEXIS 168 (App.Div., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant attorney, as intervenor, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's ruling that plaintiff employee's product liability claim against defendant foreign company was time-barred as N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:14-22 did not operate to toll the statute of limitations because that statute imposed an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee was injured by defendant foreign company's machine. Defendant attorney failed to timely inform plaintiff of a potential product liability claim and was sued for malpractice. Defendant attorney's successful defense was that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:14-22 tolled the statute of limitations on the product liability claim. Plaintiff sued defendant foreign company. The trial court granted defendant foreign company's motion for summary judgment, holding that the statute imposed an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce as it tolled the limitations period against non-resident defendants. Defendant attorney intervened and sought review. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the statute, which prior to its 1992 amendment facially discriminated against non-resident defendants, was subject to and failed the strict scrutiny test. The statute also failed the balancing test because defendant foreign company would have had to move to New Jersey to receive the benefit of the tolling provision. The court refused to find that the statute would be void prospectively, as policy considerations did not merit prospective application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because the statute, which purported to toll the statute of limitations against non-resident defendants, was discriminatory on its face and failed both the strict scrutiny and balancing tests under the Commerce Clause and thus was void. The court refused to apply its decision prospectively, rather than retroactively, because policy considerations did not warrant prospective application.

Ampro Fisheries v. Yaskin


A-62/63 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


127 N.J. 602; 606 A.2d 1099; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 374


February 3, 1992, Argued  
June 4, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 247 N.J. Super. 111 (1991).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state agencies and respondent fisheries corporation sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld the general validity of N.J. Admin. Code tit. 7:25, §§ 22.2(a)2, 22.3(b)2, and 22.3(b)3 but held that the regulations were invalid as applied to the Delaware Bay in respondent's action that attacked the validity of the regulations that prohibited fishing for menhaden within certain areas.


OVERVIEW: Respondent fisheries corporation filed an action against appellant state agencies that attacked N.J. Admin. Code tit. 7:25, §§ 22.2(a)2, 22.3(b)2, and 22.3(b)3, which prohibited fishing for menhaden within certain areas. The appellate court upheld the general validity of the regulations but held that the regulations were invalid as applied to the Delaware Bay. On appeal, the court granted both parties' petition for certification, affirmed in part, and reversed so much of the appellate court's judgment that held that the regulations were invalid as applied to the Delaware Bay. The court held that the 1905 compact between Delaware and New Jersey had not applied because there was no dispute between the states regarding the fishing regulations but simply a dispute between New Jersey and respondent. The court additionally found that the regulations had not discriminated against interstate commerce and the incidental burden imposed on interstate commerce by the regulations was not clearly excessive in relation to the putative local benefit. The court determined that the regulations were not ultra vires because they were well within the scope of the enabling act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment that upheld the general validity of menhaden fishing regulations but reversed so much of the judgment that held invalid the regulations as applied to Delaware Bay because the compact between New Jersey and Delaware had not applied. Moreover, the court found that the regulations were not in violation of the Commerce Clause.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed the dismissal of the indictment against defendants for selling drugs and other related offenses, based on entrapment as a matter of due process.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted for selling drugs and other related offenses. Defendants moved to dismiss the indictment on the ground that they had been entrapped. The trial court dismissed the indictment and concluded that defendants had been entrapped as a matter of due process. The intermediate appellate court affirmed, and the state appealed. Defendants were the ones who first expressed the idea to commit the crime, and the police merely supplied the means with which to effectuate the end.

Brady v. N.J. Redistricting Comm'n

SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY

April 6, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition for an exercise of original jurisdiction (Brady v. The New Jersey Redistricting Commission).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff residents sought review of the decision of the appellate court (New Jersey), which denied an injunction barring the redistricting plan (plan) under the Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 19:46-6 to -14, and plaintiff, resident, petitioned for original jurisdiction under N.J. Star. Ann. § 19:46-13, to contest the validity of the plan.


OVERVIEW: The Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 19:46-6 to -14, created the Redistricting Commission. Plaintiff residents sought an injunction barring the redistricting plan (plan), which was denied by the trial court and certified to the court on its own motion, and plaintiff, resident, relied on N.J. Star. Ann. § 19:46-13, to request original jurisdiction in the court. The court affirmed the trial court and upheld the plan. The court held that legislature in invoking the court with original jurisdiction violated N.J. Const. art. VI, § 3, p. 2, however, the Redistricting Commission was an administrative agency; thus, judicial review was proper. The court held that congressional redistricting was one of those limited "special purposes" which a "temporary commission" was permissible under N.J. Const. art. V, § 4, p. 1, and that a valid delegation occurred because the enabling legislation offered directives to ensure that the plan was consistent with the requirements of law. The court found that the Commission was required to satisfy "one person-one vote", that plaintiffs failed to point to any classification that excluded them, and that plaintiffs' equal-protection claim was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the Redistricting Commission's plan and held that the legislature violated the New Jersey Constitution when it provided for original jurisdiction to the court, that judicial review was proper as an appeal from an administrative agency, and that the equal protection claim was without merit because plaintiff residents failed to point to any classification that excluded them.

State v. Anderson


A-131 September Term 1990


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


127 N.J. 191; 603 A.2d 928; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 20


March 26, 1991, Argued  
March 16, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is reversed. The conviction for perjury is vacated, and the cause is remanded to the Law Division for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's conviction for perjury, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:28-1, based on allegedly false testimony given by defendant in a capital murder trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly gave false testimony during a capital murder trial which resulted in an acquittal. Defendant was acquitted of one count of perjury, under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:28-1, and convicted on the second count after the trial judge determined that the element of materiality existed as a matter of law as required by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:28-1b. The appellate court affirmed the conviction but questioned the constitutionality of the perjury statute. The court granted defendant's petition for certification limited to the issue of the constitutionality of the perjury statute. The court reversed because it found that the portion of the perjury statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. §2C:28-1b, which declared that whether a falsification was material was a question of law was an unconstitutional violation of defendant's right to a jury trial under N.J. Const. art. I, para. 9, 10 as to all of the elements of the offense, including materiality. The court found that the error was not harmless and thus vacated the perjury conviction. The court remanded the cause to the trial court for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court that affirmed defendant's perjury conviction, vacated the conviction, and remanded to the trial court because a jury should have heard the issue of materiality as an element of the offense of perjury. The court found that the portion of the statute which determined that the issue of materiality was a question of law violated the right to a jury trial for all elements of the crime.

DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is affirmed.

Stat. Ann. 46:16-1.1 after respondent town foreclosed on his property without adequate notice.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bona fide purchaser bought respondent property owner's property from respondent town at a foreclosure sale. Prior to the foreclosure sale respondent property owner, who had no notice of the impending foreclosure, had obtained an order vacating the tax foreclosure judgment. The court held that respondent property owner should have recorded the vacation order in the county deed book, but that the balance of equities tipped in favor of finding that it was entitled to the return of its property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order refusing to grant appellant bona fide purchaser's quiet title action. The court held that respondent property owner did not properly record the vacation order but that the balance of equities lay in finding that respondent property owner was entitled to its property because respondent town had failed to provide proper notice of the foreclosure.

Madden v. Delran

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bar association challenged the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which sustained the constitutionality of New Jersey's system of requiring attorneys to represent indigents without receiving compensation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bar association brought an action challenging the constitutionality of New Jersey's system of requiring licensed attorneys to represent indigents without compensation. The trial court upheld the constitutionality of the system, and appellant challenged the judgment. The judgment of the trial court was affirmed but modified to require every New Jersey municipality to provide a public defender for the municipal courts. OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed but modified to require every New Jersey municipality to provide a public defender for the municipal courts. The court held that it was not deprivation to require New Jersey attorneys to represent indigents without compensation.

State v. Lagares


A-54 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


127 N.J. 20; 601 A.2d 698; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 10


November 18, 1991, Argued  
February 6, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 247 N.J. Super. 392 (1991).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, which affirmed defendant's sentence and found that N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:43-6f, which imposed an extended term of imprisonment for repeat drug offenders, was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed the order which affirmed his sentence under N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:43-6f, arguing that it violated the separation-of-powers doctrine and equal protection and that the fine violated equal protection and was cruel and unusual punishment. On appeal, the court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:43-6f was unconstitutional because it lacked of any guidelines and any avenue for effective judicial review. Thus, it significantly increased the danger of uneven application and violated separation of powers. The court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:43-6f did not violate equal-protection rights because it was rationally related to the legitimate governmental interest of battling crime by punishing recidivists more severely. The court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:35-15 did not violate defendant's equal-protection rights by imposing a fine because it reasonably furthered the legitimate interests of providing for the strict punishment, deterrence, incapacitation of the most culpable and dangerous drug offenders, and facilitating rehabilitation. Thus, the court reversed and remanded that part of the order imposing an extended-term sentence on defendant and affirmed in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court with respect to its affirmance of defendant's extended-term sentence because the lack of guidelines or avenues for judicial review in the statute violated separation of powers. The court affirmed the remainder of the appellate court's judgment because the extended sentence and fine statutes were rationally related to a legitimate governmental interest and did not violate equal protection.

State v. Mitchell


A-20


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


126 N.J. 565; 601 A.2d 198; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 7


October 9, 1991, Argued  
January 28, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, which reversed the trial court's decision to deny defendant's petition for post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The state appealed the order which reversed the decision to deny defendant's petition for post-conviction relief. The state argued that defendant could have raised the issue on direct appeal and that there was a sufficient factual basis for defendant's plea. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the appellate court and reinstated the trial court's order which denied defendant's petition for post-conviction relief. The court held that defendant did not claim excusable neglect, allege any facts excusing his delay, or constitute a petition to correct an illegal sentence. Thus, his petition was time-barred. The court held that defendant did not show he was an innocent party who mistakenly pled guilty or received a manifestly improper sentence. Thus, barring his petition under N.J. R. Crim. P. 3:22-12 did not create an "injustice" that would have justified relaxation of the N.J. R. Crim. P. 1:1-2. The court held that defendant's petition did not raise an issue of constitutional dimension and that denying the relief did not result in a constitutional violation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and reinstated the trial court's judgment which denied defendant's petition for post-conviction relief. The court held that defendant failed to bring his appeal within the five years required by the rules of criminal procedure and that defendant made no showing that barring his petition would have been an injustice that required the court to relax the five year rule.

 

128 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dillihay


A-1 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


127 N.J. 42; 601 A.2d 1149; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 5


September 11, 1991, Argued  
January 27, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 241 N.J. Super. 553 (1990).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's decision to merge convictions for drug possession violations under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5 and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7 and further concluded that the minimum mandatory sentence required by § 2C:35-7 should be imposed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of marijuana and phencyclidine with intent to distribute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5, and possession of the same substances with intent to distribute within a school zone, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7. All violations were a part of the Controlled Dangerous Substances Act and were the result of defendant's arrest for narcotics activity at a bar located within 1000 feet of a school zone. The trial court merged the school zone convictions with the other convictions because to uphold both convictions would have violated defendants rights of due process under U.S. Const. amend XIV and double jeopardy under U.S. Const. amend. V and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 11. The trial court did impose the mandatory minimum sentence as set forth in § 2C:35-7 for school zones. The appellate division affirmed, and the court also affirmed. The court found that the legislative purpose of the school zone statutes and double jeopardy rights required the merger of the convictions with the imposition of the mandatory sentence on a defendant convicted of the related offenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the merger of the convictions and the imposition of the mandatory minimum sentence because the legislative purpose of enacting the school zone statutes was best served, consistent with double jeopardy principles, by the merger of the convictions and by construing the two sections of the Controlled Dangerous Substances Act to require that any imposed sentence include the mandatory minimum sentence.

 

129 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brana


A-71 September Term 1991


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


127 N.J. 64; 601 A.2d 1160; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 6


September 11, 1991, Argued  
January 27, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions for controlled dangerous substances offenses imposed in the Superior Court, Camden County (New Jersey), alleging that his simultaneous convictions under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5 and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7 violated double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of four offenses; he filed a motion for the merger of two of them, contending that his third-degree charge for distribution of cocaine within a school zone, a violation of the school-zone statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7, merged with his first-degree conviction of distribution of more than five ounces of cocaine, a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5. The trial court denied the motion and convicted defendant of all charges. On appeal, the court reversed the denial of merger, and remanded the case to the trial court for an amended judgment vacating defendant's conviction for distribution of cocaine within a school zone under § 2C:35-7. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed. The court held that multiple punishments for related convictions under § 2C:35-5 and § 2C:35-7 violated double jeopardy. Because § 2C:35-5 carried the greater punishment of the two provisions, its minimum mandatory sentence was the one to which defendant was subject. Defendant's claims of ineffective assistance of counsel and the unconstitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-15 were summarily rejected.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated defendant's conviction of distribution of cocaine within a school zone because federal double-jeopardy principles required that offense to merge with his conviction of the more grave offense of distribution of more than five ounces of cocaine; in all other respects, the trial court judgment was affirmed.
