State v. Purnell


A-123


Supreme Court of New Jersey


126 N.J. 518; 601 A.2d 175; 1992 N.J. LEXIS 13


May 7, 1991, Argued  
January 15, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Superior Court, Camden County (New Jersey), which sentenced him to death after he was convicted of murder and related offenses.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and related offenses. The intermediate appellate court sentenced him to death, and defendant appealed. The order sentencing defendant to death was vacated, but the convictions of murder and related offenses were affirmed. The court held that defendant was denied the right to a fair trial on the issue of his death-eligibility, because the state did not submit to the jury for consideration the non-capital verdict of felony murder, and that defendant could not be sentenced to death without having the jury make the choice between all available verdicts of capital and non-capital murder. The court also held that the voir dire on racial bias was adequate, and that the evidence did not indicate the need for a passion or provocation jury charge.


OUTCOME: The order of the Superior Court that sentenced defendant to death was vacated, but the convictions of murder and related offenses were affirmed. The court held that defendant was denied the right to a fair trial on the issue of his death-eligibility, because the state did not submit to the jury the consideration of the non-capital verdict of felony murder.

State v. Erazo

January 29, 1991, Argued  
August 8, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction for murder is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the Law Division for re-trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Essex County (New Jersey), which convicted him of capital murder and possession of a weapon for an unlawful purpose.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder and possession of a weapon for an unlawful purpose. He appealed his conviction, and the capital murder conviction was reversed, but his conviction for possession of a weapon for an unlawful purpose was affirmed. The court held that the trial court's jury instruction regarding manslaughter impermissibly shifted to defendant the burden of proving passion or provocation. The court also held that the trial court failed to instruct the jury that defendant could be convicted of capital murder only if he knowingly or purposely caused the death of the victim, as distinguished from knowingly or purposely causing serious bodily injury resulting in death. Therefore, the court concluded that the errors were not harmless, and that defendant's capital murder conviction had to be reversed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for capital murder was reversed, but his conviction for possession of a weapon for an unlawful purpose was affirmed. The court held that the trial court's jury instruction regarding manslaughter impermissibly shifted to defendant the burden of proving passion or provocation.

State v. Budis


No. A-139


Supreme Court of New Jersey


125 N.J. 519; 593 A.2d 784; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 91


March 26, 1991, Argued  
August 6, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 243 N.J. Super. 498 (1990).
State v. Budis, 243 N.J. Super. 498, 580 A.2d 283, 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 351 (App.Div., 1990)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Division is affirmed, and the matter is remanded to the Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision by the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, which reversed defendant's conviction and remanded the matter for a new trial, holding that the jury should have been told that an alternative source existed for the victim's sexual knowledge.


OVERVIEW: The state appealed the appellate court's decision to reverse defendant conviction's for aggravated sexual assault, arguing that, under the Rape Shield law, N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:14-7, the trial court correctly excluded evidence of prior sexual abuse on the alleged victim by another. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate court and held that the probative value of the evidence outweighed its possible prejudicial effect. The court held that the admission of the evidence was admissible because it rebutted the inference that the alleged victim acquired the knowledge to describe sexual matters from her experience with defendant. The court held that the evidence of the alleged victim's prior sexual abuse could have been admitted without undue prejudice by allowing the defense to cross-examine the police rather than the alleged victim. The court held that defense counsel was not permitted to try to show on cross-examination of the alleged victim that her prior sexual abuse enhanced the likelihood that she initiated the encounters with defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court which reversed defendant's conviction in the trial court and remanded the matter for a new trial. Defendant was entitled to a new trial because the evidence that the alleged victim had been previously sexually abused by her step-father was admissible to show that she had the knowledge to describe sexual matters other than any alleged incident with defendant.

Elmora Hebrew Ctr. v. Fishman


No. A-78


Supreme Court of New Jersey


125 N.J. 404; 593 A.2d 725; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 84


February 26, 1991, Argued  
July 29, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 239 N.J. Super. 229 (1990).
Elmora Hebrew Center, Inc. v. Fishman, 239 N.J. Super. 229, 570 A.2d 1297, 1990 N.J. Super. LEXIS 66 (App.Div., 1990)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant synagogue challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirming the decision of the trial court, that entered a judgment reflecting a religious tribunal's resolution of appellant's employment dispute with appellee rabbi.


OVERVIEW: Appellant synagogue employed appellee rabbi, but removed him from his position despite an employment contract. Appellee contested appellant's action, and appellant filed an action alleging, inter alia, that appellee had disrupted religious services and the orderly transaction of the congregation's business, had prevented other rabbis from serving the congregation, had diverted contributions and grant moneys, and had refused to surrender possession of appellant's rabbi residence. The court directed the parties to submit the dispute to the rabbinical court for resolution. On appeal, the court also affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that by submitting to the jurisdiction and authority of the rabbinical court, appellant waived its U.S. Const. amend. I right to challenge the tribunal's decision.


OUTCOME: The order that entered judgment on a religious tribunal's resolution of appellant synagogue's employment dispute with appellee rabbi was affirmed, because the parties were bound by the religious tribunal's decision due to their plenary agreement to submit their disputes to that body for its adjudication, and appellant's continuing appeals presented insufficient grounds to question the validity of that earlier submission.

 

135 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Holloway v. State


Nos. A-69, A-70, A-71, A-72


Supreme Court of New Jersey


125 N.J. 386; 593 A.2d 716; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 88


January 3, 1991, Argued  
July 29, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 239 N.J. Super. 554 (1990).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pool manufacturers sought review of an order of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, which reversed the trial court's ruling that dismissed respondent state's claim for reimbursement of medical expenses as time-barred and reinstated the claim.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pool manufacturers challenged the order which reinstated respondent state's claim for reimbursement of medical expenses under the theories of subrogation, contribution, and strict liability. On appeal, the court reversed and held that respondent's subrogation claim for medical expenses could not have been greater than the subrogee's claim and that, because the subrogee's claim was time-barred, respondent's claim was time-barred as well. The court held that the result was not altered by the doctrine of nullum tempus because it was no longer applicable to governmental claims. The court held that respondent was entitled to contribution from appellants and that the proper comparison of fault was the fault of each third-party defendant with that of the injured party. The court held that respondent's potential liability under the Tort Claims Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. 59:1-1-12-3, did not bar its recovery against appellants based on strict products liability because appellants could have been held liable even in the event of product misuse if the misuse was objectively foreseeable and it was not clear, as a matter of law, that respondent's conduct was not foreseeable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order reinstating respondent state's claim for subrogation and contribution of medical expenses because the subrogation claim was time-barred and the method for comparing fault between respondent and appellant pool manufacturers was faulty. The court affirmed the appellate court's order regarding respondent's strict liability claim because respondent's action could have been foreseeable to appellants.

Alexander's Dep't Stores v. Paramus

Supreme Court of New Jersey


125 N.J. 100; 592 A.2d 1168; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 82

July 24, 1991, Decided

Stat. Ann. §§ 52:27D-301 to -329.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a developer, an administrative agency, a city, its council, planning board, and mayor, adopted an ordinance implementing a plan to rezone property to provide land for the construction of affordable housing to comply with the New Jersey Fair Housing Act of 1985 (FHA), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 52:27D-301 to -329. Plaintiff commercial property owners filed a complaint, challenging the validity of the ordinance. Defendants filed motions to dismiss, which were denied. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that plaintiff commercial property owners' challenge to the validity of a zoning ordinance by defendants, a developer, an administrative agency, a city, its council, planning board, and mayor, which implemented a plan to comply with the New Jersey Fair Housing Act, was not subject to the jurisdiction of defendant administrative agency.

Supreme Court of New Jersey


125 N.J. 193; 593 A.2d 251; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 76; 22 ELR 20155


October 22, 1990, Argued  
July 23, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 235 N.J. Super. 582 (1989).
Gardner v. New Jersey Pinelands Com., 235 N.J. Super. 382, 562 A.2d 812, 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 323 (App.Div., 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner sought review of the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's order granting summary judgment in favor of defendant state agencies, in an action brought by plaintiff for inverse condemnation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowner brought an action for inverse condemnation against defendant state agencies, alleging that the revised comprehensive management plan (CMP) enacted pursuant to the New Jersey Pinelands Protection Act (Act), N. J. Stat. Ann. § 13:18A-1-29 resulted in an unlawful taking of his property. The trial court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment, and the appeals court affirmed. The court affirmed, holding that in light of the environmental sensitivity of the area, the CMP did not constitute an unlawful taking, as it did not deprive plaintiff of the economic or beneficial use of all or most of his property, sufficiently diminish the value or profitability of his land, or otherwise interfere with his ownership interest. Finally, the court held that plaintiff's equal protection rights under N. J. Const. art. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant state agencies. The court held that regulations enacted pursuant to the New Jersey Pineland Protection Act did not constitute a taking of plaintiff landowner's property, and that the regulations did not deprive plaintiff of equal protection under the law.

Town of Morristown v. Woman's Club of Morristown


No. A-121


Supreme Court of New Jersey


124 N.J. 605; 592 A.2d 216; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 73

July 15, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 242 N.J. Super. 654 (1990).

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order from the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed a lower court's order granting tax exempt status under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-3.52 because appellee leased part of the building for commercial purposes. Appellee's charitable nonprofit activities included conducting social services for hospitalized and nursing home patients, raising scholarship funds, serving as hospital and church volunteers, and organizing holiday drives to benefit needy families. The court concluded that that the classification of exempt property based on the characteristics of the property and the status of the owner were necessary to fulfill the public purpose of preserving historic sites. The court affirmed the lower court's decision, which upheld the tax exemption for the historic home, finding that the exemption statute existed exclusively to secure the preservation and maintenance of historic sites, and that purpose was served by appellee.

The exemption statute existed to secure the preservation of historic sites, and appellee served that purpose with the subject historic home.

State v. Ivory


No. A-126


Supreme Court of New Jersey


124 N.J. 582; 592 A.2d 205; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 67


March 12, 1991, Argued  
July 11, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court's order convicting him of possession of cocaine and marijuana with intent to distribute within 1,000 feet of school property. Defendant contended that the athletic field in question did not constitute "school property" for purposes of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of cocaine and marijuana with intent to distribute, while within 1,000 feet of school property. A police officer stopped and searched defendant as he rode his bicycle within 1,000 feet of an athletic field, which was owned by the local board of education, was leased to a secondary school, and was regularly used for public and parochial school activities. N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-7 prohibited the distribution or possession with intent to distribute a controlled substance while on school property used for school purposes, which was owned or leased to any elementary or secondary school or school board, or within 1,000 feet of such property. The court found that so long as there were indicia from which an objectively reasonable person could know that the school property was used for school purposes, lack of knowledge by a drug dealer that he was within 1,000 feet of school property was irrelevant. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment sustaining defendant's conviction, finding that the legislature intended that athletic fields connected to the educational mission of a private high school to be protected safety zones.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, which sustained defendant's conviction for possession of cocaine and marijuana with intent to distribute, while within 1,000 feet of school property. The legislature intended that athletic fields connected to the educational mission of a private high school be protected safety zones and "school property" for purposes of the applicable statute.

Twiss v. State


No. A-122


Supreme Court of New Jersey


124 N.J. 461; 591 A.2d 913; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 63


February 26, 1991, Argued  
June 24, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 239 N.J. Super. 342 (1990).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the judgment the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which denied its motion to dismiss the complaint filed by respondent businessman in his action to compel appellant to release to him the amounts owed to owners of unclaimed property.


OVERVIEW: Respondent businessman located owners of unclaimed property that had been delivered to appellant state and offered to tell them of the unclaimed property for a contingent fee based on the amount in the account. He claimed that he was entitled to know the amounts in the accounts. The court held that the Uniform Unclaimed Property Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 46:30B-1 et seq. (UUPA), prohibited disclosure of the amounts to anyone who did not have a beneficial interest in the account. The UUPA was enacted after the lower court decided the case and it had retroactive application because there was no express limit to prospective application. The court held that though UUPA required holders of unclaimed property to submit written reports to appellant including the amounts due, this information was not for disclosure pursuant to the public's right to know because there was a right to confidentiality in bank records and § 46:30B-761 provided that the bank information was deemed confidential when delivered to appellant. The court held that respondent had no beneficial interest in the accounts or the welfare of the owners and was not entitled to the information.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and reinstated the trial division's judgment that granted appellant state's motion to dismiss respondent businessman's complaint. The court held that appellant was not obliged to disclose the amount in an unclaimed bank account to respondent, who was a third party who had no beneficial interest in the account.

State v. Perry


No. A-3


Supreme Court of New Jersey


124 N.J. 128; 590 A.2d 624; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 45


October 9, 1990, Argued  
May 20, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Camden County (New Jersey), which convicted him of murder and related offenses in the strangling death of the victim. Defendant was sentenced to death and appealed as of right pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(3) contending that the trial court erred in submitting aggravating factors to the jury pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and related offenses in the strangling death of a victim that resulted from a fight between them in a dispute over drugs and money. Defendant was sentenced to death and contended that the trial court erred in submitting aggravating factors of torture and depravity of mind to the jury pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(c). The court agreed and held that the act of strangling the victim could not be an aggravating factor pursuant to § 2C:11-3c(4)(c) because it was not torture in that there was no proof that defendant intended to inflict extreme pain other than that incidental to the strangulation that caused the victim's death. Further, the court found that the state failed to prove that defendant had a "depravity of mind" or killed the victim for pleasure or for no reason whatsoever. Because torture and depravity should not have been submitted to the jury as aggravating factors, the court reversed the death penalty and remanded the case to the trial court for further sentencing as directed by the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder and related offenses but reversed the imposition of the death penalty and remanded the case for imposition of sentence on this charge. The court held that torture and depravity of mind were improperly submitted to the jury as aggravating factors because they were not supported by competent evidence and could not sustain implementation of the death penalty.

OVERVIEW: Appellant insurance guaranty association was created as a state entity to collect assessments to cover unpaid claims against insolvent insurers. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. State


Nos. A-136, A-137, A-138, A-200


Supreme Court of New Jersey


124 N.J. 32; 590 A.2d 191; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 38


January 15, 1991, Argued  
May 16, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a decision by the Superior Court, Chancery Division (New Jersey), that granted a preliminary injunction in favor of respondent insurance companies. Respondents had filed suit challenging the facial constitutionality of the Fair Automobile Insurance Reform Act of 1990, N.J. Stat. Ann. §17:30A-1 et seq. The appellate division granted appellant leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurance companies filed suit against appellant state, challenging the Fair Automobile Insurance Reform Act of 1990, N.J. Stat. Ann. §17:30A-1 et seq. Respondents contended that the act constituted confiscatory taking and that the costs could not be passed on to customers. The lower court granted respondents a preliminary injunction and appellant sought review of that decision. The court reversed the decision in part and modified it in part, holding that the act was not facially unconstitutional as a confiscatory taking. In considering the act's terms and the interrelationship of its provisions, the legislative history, and the regulations, the court concluded that the act absolutely prohibited rate relief to insurers in the form of direct passthroughs of surtaxes and assessments in standard ratemaking. Nonetheless, the legislature's assurance of an adequate rate of return and the creation of a special rate-relief process showed that not all insurers were kept from earning a fair rate of return. The commissioner was able to consider the surtaxes and assessments in affording rate relief to an insurance company otherwise unable to earn a fair rate of return.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and modified in part, holding that the Fair Automobile Insurance Reform Act did not, on its face, impose a confiscatory taking, and that it met the minimal requirements for constitutionality under a substantive due process analysis. Therefore, respondents insurance companies were not entitled to an injunction.

 

145 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Olivio


No. A-58


Supreme Court of New Jersey


123 N.J. 550; 589 A.2d 597; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 36


November 26, 1990, Argued  
May 1, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 237 N.J. Super. 428 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a reversal by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), of defendant's conviction for sexual assault on a mentally defective person, under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2c(2), which held that the state had not proved that the victim was mentally defective, and that there was no evidence that defendant knew or should have known that the victim was mentally defective.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of sexual assault on a mentally-defective person under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2c(2) by a jury at trial. The appellate court reversed the conviction, holding that the state had failed to prove that the victim was mentally defective and that defendant knew or should have known that the victim was mentally defective. The state challenged the reversal, and the court reversed the decision of the lower court and remanded the case for a new trial. The court held that a person was mentally defective within the meaning of § 2C:14-2c(2) if, at the time of the sexual activity, he or she was unable to comprehend the distinctively sexual nature of the conduct or was incapable of understanding or exercising the right to refuse to engage in such conduct with another. Further, the court found that there was sufficient evidence adduced in the trial to satisfy the test, as well as the statutory requirement ascribing to defendant knowledge of the mental condition of the victim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court, which reversed defendant's conviction for sexual assault on a mentally disabled person. The court held that there was sufficient evidence adduced in the trial showing victim satisfied the test of being a mentally disabled person, as well as the statutory requirement ascribing to defendant knowledge of the mental condition of the victim.

Velasquez v. Franz


No. A-91


Supreme Court of New Jersey


123 N.J. 498; 589 A.2d 143; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 32


November 26, 1990, Argued  
April 25, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which dismissed his complaint against defendants, manufacturer, and trustee based on res judicata principles.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee brought an action against defendants, manufacturer, and trustee after he lost most of his right hand while operating a molding machine. Plaintiff commenced the action in federal court against defendant manufacturer, and against defendant trustee as recipient of the corporate assets, because defendant manufacturer was dissolved as a corporation under Illinois law 13 days before plaintiff's accident. The federal court dismissed the case against defendants. Plaintiff did not appeal the federal court decision, but filed a complaint virtually identical to his federal complaint in state court. The trial court dismissed the complaint against defendants. The lower court affirmed the trial court's dismissals based on res judicata. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court declared that to authorize plaintiff's nearly identical application to state court after an unfavorable federal decision would be an endorsement of forum sampling. The court held that because the parties and facts were identical in both the federal and state matters, res judicata applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment favoring defendants, manufacturer, and trustee. The court held that because the parties and facts were identical in both the federal and state matters, res judicata applied, and plaintiff employee was not allowed to forum-shop.

State v. Apportionment Com


Supreme Court of New Jersey


125 N.J. 375; 593 A.2d 710; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 836


February 25, 1991, Argued  
February 27, 1991, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state of New Jersey, democratic members of the Apportionment Commission, and other interested parties, sought review of the decision of the Law Division (New Jersey), which held that the census data received by the governor was the official decennial census within the meaning of N.J. Const. art. IV, § 3 and that its delivery on January 30, 1991, constituted full receipt of the census.


OVERVIEW: A declaratory-judgment action was filed to determine whether the census data received by the governor on January 24 and 30, 1991, constituted the official decennial census within the meaning of N.J. Const. art. IV, § 3, para. 1. The court affirmed the lower court's declaratory judgment that the census data received by the governor's office on January 24 and 30, 1991, constituted the official decennial census and that the delivery on January 30, 1991, constituted the full receipt of the census because a literal adherence to the words selected in art. IV, § 3, para. 1 carefully defining the mechanics and administration of government was mandated. The court held that the meaning of the phrase official decennial census embraced the data officially delivered to the governor by the census bureau on January 24 and 30, 1991. The court held that it did not express an opinion on whether the census was complete for all federal purposes but limited its decision to the meaning of official decennial census within the constitution. The court held that communications surrounding the delivery of the census figures to the governor indicated that the census bureau regarded the census as complete.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that held that the census data received by the governor's office on January 24 and 30, 1991, constituted the official decennial census and that the delivery on January 30, 1991, constituted the full receipt of the census because a literal adherence to the words selected in the constitution carefully defining the mechanics and administration of government was mandated.

Drew Assocs. of NJ, LP v. Travisano


Nos. A-28, A-29


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


122 N.J. 249; 584 A.2d 807; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 11


October 10, 1990, Argued  
January 31, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 235 N.J. Super. 194 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent limited partnership sought review of an order from the Superior Court (New Jersey), which upheld the recording requirements of the Cooperative Recording Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 46:8D-1 to -18, but invalidated the transfer tax applicable under the statute to transfers of ownership of cooperatives. Respondent contended that the Act was unconstitutional because it treated pre-Act and post-Act cooperatives differently.


OVERVIEW: Respondent limited partnership, which was establishing a cooperative, challenged the constitutionality of the Cooperative Recording Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 46:8D-1 to -18, which exempted units formed prior to the Act's 1988 enactment. The court found that the fact that other available methods might achieve the same ends did not warrant respondent's conclusion that the recording requirements of the Act were arbitrary and unnecessary. The court noted that cooperative ownership constituted a hybrid form of property, and it was reasonable to treat the transfer of cooperative interests differently from the transfer of other real estate interests. The court affirmed the portion of the lower court's order finding no due process or equal protection violations. However, the court reversed that portion of the lower court's order concluding that there was no rational basis for distinguishing between pre-Act and post-Act cooperatives with respect to the transfer tax. The court noted that post-Act cooperatives were genuinely different from pre-Act cooperatives in their administrative expenses and benefits received, and therefore deserved different treatment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the portion of the lower court's order finding that the cooperative housing legislation did not violate respondent limited partnership's equal protection or due process rights. However, the court reversed the portion of the lower court's order with respect to the transfer tax, reasoning that pre-Act and post-Act cooperatives were genuinely different and deserved different treatment under the statute.

State v. Marshall


No. A-3


Supreme Court of New Jersey


123 N.J. 1; 586 A.2d 85; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 17


January 30, 1990, Argued  
January 24, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Superior Court, Atlantic County (New Jersey), which rendered a jury verdict that convicted defendant of conspiracy to commit murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:5-2 and 2C:11-3a(1) or (2), and murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:2-6 and 2C:11-3a(1) or (2), and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried and convicted by a jury of conspiracy to commit the murder of his wife and of the murder of his wife as an accomplice who procured the commission of the murder by payment or promise of payment of money. The trial court sentenced defendant to death as required by the jury's verdict. The court affirmed. As to issues arising out of the pretrial proceedings, the court held that there were no errors in the search warrant and subsequent admission of evidence, the trial court properly changed venue because of pretrial publicity, and the jury selection process was valid. As to the trial itself, the court rejected defendant's arguments that there was erroneous admission of hearsay evidence, that defendant's counsel was ineffective, and that there was insufficient evidence to convict. The court also held that while the prosecutor made some improper remarks during trial, they did not constitute reversible error. As to the sentencing phase, the court held that the jury properly considered aggravating and mitigating factors in determining defendant's sentence. Finally, the court affirmed the trial court's ruling that there was no Brady violation by the prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of conspiracy to commit murder and murder and the sentence of death. There was no cumulative error in defendant's pretrial proceedings, trial, sentencing proceeding, or Brady hearing which warranted the reversal of defendant's conviction and sentence.

State v. Oglesby


No. A-1


Supreme Court of New Jersey


122 N.J. 522; 585 A.2d 916; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 6


September 11, 1990, Argued  
January 23, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court, Camden County (New Jersey), convicting him of capital murder and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial for capital murder, he relied on the affirmative defense of insanity under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:4-1. Evidence indicated that he sustained a brain injury when he was 16 and his life since was a chronicle of bizarre, violent behavior, and mental illness. During one hospitalization, he was diagnosed with schizophreniform disorder. In its jury instructions, the trial court charged not only on that defense, but also on diminished capacity under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:4-2. On review, the court reversed defendant's conviction and sentence of death. The court found that the diminished capacity instruction improperly placed on defendant the burden of establishing by a preponderance that he suffered from a mental disease or defect at the time of the homicide and impermissibly relieved the state of part of its burden of proving that the homicide was knowing or purposeful. The court found the instruction was defective, had the capacity to produce an unjust result and, as such, constituted plain error. The court also found the failure to provide a limiting instruction concerning aggravating factor was an independent ground for vacating defendant's death sentence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for capital murder, vacated the imposition of the death penalty, and remanded because the jury instruction on diminished capacity was defective and constituted plain error.

STATE v. MOORE


No. A-88


Supreme Court of New Jersey


122 N.J. 420; 585 A.2d 864; 1991 N.J. LEXIS 15


May 8, 1990, Argued  
January 23, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Essex County (New Jersey), which convicted him of two counts of capital murder and two counts of weapons related offenses. Defendant was sentenced to death and appealed as of right pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(3), contending the trial court erred in charging the jury that he had the burden of proof as to his diminished capacity when he committed the murders.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of capital murder and two counts of weapons related offenses in the deaths of his pregnant wife and minor child. Defendant contended the trial court erred in instructing the jury that he had the burden of proof on the issue of diminished capacity. The court agreed and reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court noted that a shortly after the verdict, the U.S. Supreme court denied certiorari in a case which ruled that a defendant's due process rights were violated where he had the burden of proof as to the existence of a mental disease or defect. The court held that the charge was erroneous and it resulted in prejudicial harm to defendant because the only issue at trial was not whether defendant committed the murders, but whether he had a diminished state of mind that negated the requisite state of mind required for murder. The court found that defendant had presented medical evidence that he had a stress-induced psychosis that grew out of a borderline personality disorder which affected his cognitive faculties, and a jury could have concluded that the state failed to prove the mens rea required for murder.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case to the trial court because defendant's due process rights had been violated where he was required to assume the burden of proving that he had diminished capacity of his cognitive faculties at the time he killed his pregnant wife and minor child. The court held that the jury instruction placing the burden of proof on defendant was erroneous and caused prejudicial harm to his defense.

Holmdel Builders Ass'n v. Holmdel


Nos. A-103, A-104, A-105, A-106, A-107, A-108, A-109


Supreme Court of New Jersey


121 N.J. 550; 583 A.2d 277; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 316


February 13, 1990, Argued  
December 13, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 232 N.J. Super. 182 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant builders' associations sought review of a decision by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which deemed mandatory development fees invalid taxes. The appellate court also found that appellants' cases against respondent townships should be dismissed because a trade organization did not have standing to seek refunds on behalf of its members.


OVERVIEW: Appellant builders' associations challenged development fees charged by respondent townships in their efforts to comply with the Fair Housing Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. 52:27D-301 et seq. On appeal, the appellate court found that the development fees were invalid taxes, but that appellants lacked the standing to request refunds on the taxes for its member. The court affirmed the decision regarding the standing issue, but reversed the decision concerning the fees. The court held that fact that respondents sought to accomplish the general-welfare goal of affordable housing by development fees rather than by mandatory set-asides did not negate a real and substantial relationship of such development fees to the regulation of land. Although the development-fee measures were not site-specific in the same sense as mandatory set-asides, they implicated land-related regulations because they were specifically designed and applied to aid in the creation of affordable residential housing under N.J. Stat. Ann. §52:27D-312.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court was affirmed in part by the court and reversed in part. The court held that appellant builders' associations did not have standing to request refunds on the taxes which they attacked. The court also held that development fees could be charged by municipalities in their efforts to comply with the laws regarding low-income housing but that such fees must be adopted in the proper manner.

State v. Kiett


No. A-23


Supreme Court of New Jersey


121 N.J. 483; 582 A.2d 630; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 197


September 12, 1989, Argued  
November 14, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Kiett, 114 N.J. 470, 555 A.2d 598, 1989 N.J. LEXIS 779 (1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which denied his motion to withdraw his plea of guilty to murder, agreed to in order to avoid exposure to an inapplicable death penalty.


OVERVIEW: At age 17, defendant was indicted on charges of knowing or purposeful murder by his own conduct (capital murder) among many other crimes. Under a misapprehension that the death penalty was applicable to juveniles, defendant pleaded guilty to murder pursuant to a plea bargain that removed the risk of the death penalty. The appellate court rejected defendant's request to withdraw the plea and defendant appealed. Reversing the judgment of the appellate court, the court held that defendant's belief, incorrect as a matter of law, that he was subject to the death penalty was a sufficient basis for withdrawal of his guilty plea because avoidance of the death penalty was a substantial factor in his decision to plead guilty. The court found that the penalty proceeding itself was improper in that it simply determined whether or not there might be another penalty proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court which denied defendant's motion to withdraw his plea of guilty to murder, agreed to in order to avoid exposure to an inapplicable death penalty. The court held that defendant could withdraw his plea of guilty to murder because the plea, entered to avoid the death penalty, was made without a full understanding that as a matter of law he could not have been put to death as a juvenile.

State v. Harvey


No. A-85


Supreme Court of New Jersey


121 N.J. 407; 581 A.2d 483; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 186


March 27, 1990, Argued  
October 18, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied New Jersey v. Harvey, 499 U.S. 931, 111 S. Ct. 1336, 113 L. Ed. 2d 268, 1991 U.S. LEXIS 1661 (Mar. 18, 1991)
Decision reached on appeal by State v. Harvey, 151 N.J. 117, 699 A.2d 596, 1997 N.J. LEXIS 261 (July 30, 1997)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Middlesex County (New Jersey), which convicted him of capital-murder and sentenced him to death, on the grounds that the trial court erred in instructing the jury during the guilt phase of defendant's trial.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of knowing and purposeful murder, felony murder, first-degree robbery, and second-degree burglary. Following a penalty-phase proceeding on the capital-murder conviction, the trial court sentenced defendant to death. Defendant appealed contending that the trial court's failure to instruct the jury separately for the crimes of knowingly or purposely causing death (intentional murder) and of knowingly or purposely causing serious bodily injury resulting in death (serious-bodily-injury murder) required reversal of his conviction. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and held that where the evidence provided a rational basis for a jury to convict defendant of either intentional or serious-bodily-injury murder, the trial court erred by not instructing the jury to specify which, if either, of those findings formed the basis for a conviction. The court held that where police failed scrupulously to honor defendant's right to cut off questioning and failed to give fresh Miranda warnings before resuming questioning, the illegality rendered the suspect's subsequent inculpatory confession unconstitutionally compelled as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of capital-murder and remanded the case for a new trial. The court held that the trial court's failure to instruct the jury separately for the crimes of knowingly or purposely causing death (intentional murder) and of knowingly or purposely causing serious bodily injury resulting in death (serious-bodily-injury murder) required reversal of defendant's conviction.

Catania v. Haberle


Nos. A-114, A-115


Supreme Court of New Jersey


123 N.J. 438; 588 A.2d 374; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 1364


October 2, 1990, Argued  
October 2, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, candidate for state assembly, county republican committees, and state republican committee, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that affirmed the trial court's ruling in favor of appellee secretary of state that failure to give notice within the seven day statutory period of a vacancy on the ballot invalidated appellant candidate's selection at the subsequent party-committee meeting.


OVERVIEW: Appellant candidate for state assembly filed an action to compel respondent secretary of state to place his name on the ballot. The court reversed the appellate court's order that failure to give notice within the seven day statutory period of a vacancy on the ballot invalidated appellant's selection as a candidate at the subsequent party-committee meeting because the seven day deadline of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 19:13-20(b)(1) was not mandatory and appellant properly filled the vacancy. The court held that the only timetable in the statute for holding the party-committee meeting to fill the vacancy was the requirement that the candidate's name be sent to respondent 48 hours before the general election. The court held that the unimportance of the timing of the notice was underscored by the fact that it was not required to be given in writing or to be mailed. The court held that the amendment to N.J. Stat. Ann. 19:27-11.1 which foreclosed the party from selecting a candidate if a write-in effort was attempted in the primary but failed was not retroactive and did not apply to this case where the amendment occurred after the vacancy but before it was filled.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the finding that invalidated appellant candidate's selection because the seven day deadline was not mandatory and appellant properly filled the vacancy.

May 7, 1990, Argued  
August 30, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at State v. Clausell, 161 N.J. 331, 736 A.2d 524, 1999 N.J. LEXIS 1024 (1999)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County.
State v. Wright, 118 N.J. 193, 570 A.2d 958, 1989 N.J. LEXIS 1632 (1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.J. Ct. R 2:2-1(a)(3) from the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County (New Jersey), which convicted him of capital murder, three counts of aggravated assault, possession of a firearm with the purpose to use it unlawfully against the person of another, and possession of a handgun without a permit.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of capital murder, three counts of aggravated assault, possession of a firearm with the purpose to use it unlawfully against the person of another, and possession of a handgun without a permit. Defendant appealed to the court pursuant to N. J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(3). The court reversed defendant's conviction for capital murder because it determined that the trial court failed to instruct the jury that defendant could be convicted of capital murder only if he knowingly or purposely caused the death of the victim, as opposed to knowingly or purposely causing serious bodily injury that resulted in death. There was a rational basis in the evidence for the jury to have found defendant intended to cause only serious bodily injury. The court reversed defendant's convictions for aggravated assault because the trial court did not inform the jury that it could convict defendant of aggravated assault only if it found that defendant was aware that it was practically certain that when he pointed the gun at the victim, he was pointing it at or in the direction of persons other than the victim. The court also discussed errors that might arise again on retrial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's capital murder conviction because it found that the trial court failed to instruct the jury that defendant could be convicted of capital murder only if he knowingly or purposely caused the death of the victim. The court reversed defendant's convictions for aggravated assault because the trial court did not correctly instruct the jury that defendant must have been fairly certain that he pointed the gun at others.

State v. Hamm


No. A-68


Supreme Court of New Jersey


121 N.J. 109; 577 A.2d 1259; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 131


February 27, 1990, Argued  
August 6, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who had two previous convictions for driving while intoxicated, pleaded guilty to a third charge after his motion for a trial by jury was denied. The Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, affirmed the denial of the motion, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Facing his third charge of driving while intoxicated, defendant pleaded guilty to the offense after his motion for a jury trial was denied. The court upheld the lower court's order affirming the denial of his motion. It concluded that the statutory penalties imposed by the state for the offense did not signal the legislature's intent to treat DWI as the functional equivalent of a crime, noting that third-time offenders such as defendant would not face a sentence of more than six months. It found that the 10-year license suspension for third offenders, although a heavy burden, was not more of a loss than that of a business or professional license, which could be taken away with no more than a hearing. Moreover, it noted, at one time repeat offenders faced the permanent loss of their licenses. It added that no other loss of privilege, franchise, or right of citizenship flowed from a DWI conviction. Therefore, it concluded, DWI in New Jersey could not be regarded as a "serious" offense as defined by the United States Supreme Court and did not entitle defendant to a jury trial under U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OUTCOME: Finding that the consequences of driving while intoxicated (DWI) did not make it a "serious" offense for purposes of entitling an offender to a jury trial, the court upheld the lower court's order affirming the denial of defendant's motion for a jury trial on the DWI charges, to which he pleaded guilty.

State v. Crandall


Nos. A-97, A-98


Supreme Court of New Jersey


120 N.J. 649; 577 A.2d 483; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 114


May 8, 1990, Argued  
July 31, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 231 N.J. Super. 124 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that convicted him of aggravated sexual assault, sexual assault, and endangering the life of a child by using a procedure authorized by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:84A-32.4, which allows a child victim to testify via closed-circuit television.


OVERVIEW: It was alleged that defendant sexually abused a friend's daughter while she was under his care. The state's motion was granted to permit the child victim to testify via closed-circuit television pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:84A-32.4. Defendant was convicted of aggravated sexual assault, sexual assault, and endangering the welfare of a child. Defendant challenged the conviction. The appellate division determined that the statute was constitutional, but remanded for a N.J. R. Evid. 8 hearing. On the state's petition and defendant's cross-petition the court found the statute was constitutional. Additionally, the court concluded that an evidentiary hearing under N.J. R. Evid. 8 was not required because the judge could have determined from the various factors under child abuse statutes whether the use of such procedure was necessary to protect the welfare of the child. The trial court should have considered if the child witness would have been traumatized by the presence of the defendant and whether such emotional distress suffered by presence of defendant was more than de minimis requiring that the confrontation clause be denied for furtherance of public policy.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the trial court's findings concerning the child's objective manifestations of fear were sufficient to meet constitutional standards, but it should have also considered the outlined factors in making particularized findings regarding the likelihood of emotional distress suffered by a child witness. The appellate division's remand for hearing under evidence rule was reversed as it was not required.

Twp. of W. Milford v. Van Decker


No. A-159


Supreme Court of New Jersey


120 N.J. 354; 576 A.2d 881; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 107


May 8, 1990, Argued  
July 24, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 235 N.J. Super. 1 (1989).
West Milford v. Van Decker, 235 N.J. Super. 1, 561 A.2d 607, 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 263 (App.Div., 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that reversed the judgment of the tax court, which held spot assessments as long as within the common-level range were permissible under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:51A6, despite discriminatory affects.


OVERVIEW: Respondents purchased a single family home that was later assessed at a higher property value. Respondents contended that such reassessment was discriminatory because appellant only reassessed properties which had been sold. Appellants claimed even if their acts were discriminatory it was within the perimeters of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:51A6, chap. 123. The passaic county board of taxation rendered judgment for respondents. Appellants challenged the board's decision, which the tax court reversed. It found that the assessments were permissible as long as it was within chap. 123's establishment range. The appellate division reversed the tax court holding raising the assessments only of recently-sold homes denied taxpayers equal protection. The court held that chap. 123 was not applicable in egregious cases of discrimination. The spot assessments violated both federal and state constitutions as taxpayers were protected under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, from being selected for discriminatory treatment by subjecting them to taxes not imposed on others.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division holding that spot assessments were in violation of the federal and state constitutions because comparable properties that had not been assessed carried a much lower tax burden than those recently sold. Such discrimination of appellants under the equal protection clause was not overridden by statute.

State v. Johnson


No. A-7


Supreme Court of New Jersey


120 N.J. 263; 576 A.2d 834; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 96


November 27, 1989, Argued  
July 19, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Gloucester County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court, Law Division, Gloucester County (New Jersey), which convicted the defendant of two counts of knowing and purposeful murder, one count of robbery in the first degree, one count of theft of property, two counts of possession of a dangerous weapon for an unlawful purpose, and one count of unlawful possession of a weapon, and sentenced the defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted the defendant of two counts of knowing and purposeful murder, robbery in the first degree, theft of property, two counts of possession of a dangerous weapon for an unlawful purpose, and unlawful possession of a weapon. The defendant received a death sentence for one of the murders, a life sentence with 30 years of parole ineligibility for the second murder, and consecutive sentences totaling 35 years with 17.5 years of parole ineligibility on the remaining counts. On review, the court reversed the defendant's conviction and remanded the case for a new trial, because the court found that the police failed to scrupulously honor the defendant's right to remain silent. Therefore, the defendant's confession was inadmissible. The court further held that under the "fruit of the poisonous tree" doctrine, the defendant's subsequent taped confession, as well as incriminating statements made immediately thereafter, were also inadmissible. The defendant's consent to search his room was likewise tainted by the illegally coerced confession, but the fruits of that search were admissible on retrial under the inevitable-discovery doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, holding that the police illegally obtained the defendant's confession by failing to scrupulously honor the defendant's right to remain silent. Accordingly, the confession and all evidence which derived from the confession was inadmissible. The court found that the evidence obtained from the defendant's room in his parents' residence was similarly tainted, but admissible under the inevitable discovery rule.

State v. Savage


No. A-4


Supreme Court of New Jersey


120 N.J. 594; 577 A.2d 455; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 97


November 6, 1989, Argued  
July 19, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by State v. Savage, 138 N.J. 271, 649 A.2d 1290, 1994 N.J. LEXIS 904 (1994)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.
In re Savage, 233 N.J. Super. 356, 558 A.2d 1357, 1989 N.J. Super. LEXIS 218 (App.Div., 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right, pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(3), from the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey), which convicted him of capital murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of capital murder and sentenced him to death. The court reversed the judgment of conviction for capital murder, and remanded the cause for a new trial because it found that defendant was denied effective assistance of counsel. The court found that under the totality of the circumstances, defense counsel's strategy at trial was robbed of any presumption of competence and was deficient. Defense counsel's strategy at trial was not preceded by thorough investigation and consideration of all plausible options. At a minimum, an expert appraisal of defendant's mental condition and the effect of cocaine on his mental state was critical to making a competent strategy decision with respect to a diminished capacity or insanity defense. The court found defense counsel's reasoning that he failed to do so because "nothing jogged his mind" to be wholly unacceptable and indicative of his lack of expertise with respect to the special considerations in a capital case. The court also found that by failing to inquire into critical facts in support of his case in mitigation, counsel's performance in the penalty phase, as in the guilt phase, was deficient.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's conviction of defendant for capital murder and reversed his sentence and remanded the matter for a new trial because it found that defendant was denied effective assistance of counsel, in violation of the federal and state constitutions.

 

162 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. McDougald


No. A-83


Supreme Court of New Jersey


120 N.J. 523; 577 A.2d 419; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 88


January 3, 1990, Argued  
July 12, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey), which convicted him of the brutal murders of two people and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of two brutal murders and sentenced to death. He challenged his conviction and sentence. On appeal, the court affirmed appellant's murder conviction, set aside the death penalty, and remanded the case to the trial court for a new sentencing proceeding. The trial court's voir dire procedure did not deprive appellant of an impartial jury trial. The State did not exercise its peremptory challenges systematically to exclude racial minorities and women from the jury. Because the record contained nothing that would have supported a "serious bodily injury" murder charge, the trial court's failure to give that charge was not error. The trial court's language adequately explained and differentiated between active participation and accomplice liability for murder. The jury properly considered mitigating factors, and photographic evidence was properly admitted. The trial court's charge on the N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3(c)(4)(c) aggravating factor, however, was defective and warranted a new penalty phase hearing. The court noted that, at the new sentencing proceeding, sufficient evidence supported the resubmission to the jury of the § 2C:11-3(c)(4)(c) aggravating factor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for the brutal murders of two people, but set aside the death penalty and remanded the case for a new sentencing proceeding. The court held that the trial court's charge on the aggravating factor involving torture or aggravated battery was defective and warranted a new penalty phase hearing.

State v. Hightower


No. A-1


Supreme Court of New Jersey


120 N.J. 378; 577 A.2d 99; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 89


September 26, 1989, Argued  
July 12, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County (New Jersey), which convicted him of murder and several related offenses and which sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the murder of a convenience store clerk and several offenses related to that murder. Following the penalty phase, defendant was sentenced to death. Defendant challenged his conviction and his sentence. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction, and, based upon the state's concession, set aside the death sentence and remanded the matter for a new sentencing proceeding. The court rejected defendant's claims that his counsel provided ineffective assistance due to his counsel's failure to make certain objections during the jury selection process, his failure to object to the admission of hearsay statements, and his failure to object to an inappropriate comment made by the state during closing argument. Under the facts of the case, the court also held that the trial court did not err in failing to instruct the jury on serious bodily injury murder. The court, however, set aside the death sentence because the trial court's charge requiring juror unanimity on a mitigating factor violated the principles subsequently enunciated by the state supreme court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of murder and several related offenses, set aside the death sentence, and remanded the case for a new sentencing proceeding. The court rejected defendant's claims that, during the guilt phase, his counsel rendered ineffective assistance. The court, however, vacated the death sentence because the trial court's charge requiring juror unanimity on a mitigating factor was legally incorrect.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state and intervenor parents challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which summarily affirmed the trial court's order allowing appellee newspaper to receive unredacted transcripts, subject to certain restrictions, of a protracted criminal trial involving the alleged sexual abuse of intervenors' minor children.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied appellee newspaper's application to unseal the transcripts of a criminal trial involving the alleged sexual abuse of children. On appeal, the intermediate appellate court reversed, holding that the sealing of the transcripts was inconsistent with the trial court's obligation to consider the least intrusive alternative in restricting media access to criminal trials. On remand, the trial court interpreted the intermediate appellate court's opinion as vesting the choice between receiving redacted or unredacted transcripts in appellee. Appellee elected to receive unredacted transcripts subject to certain conditions. The state and intervenor parents appealed. The intermediate court summarily affirmed. On further appeal, the court exercised its appellate jurisdiction and supervisory authority over the administration of justice and reviewed the intermediate appellate court's initial decision, from which no appeal had been taken. The court reversed and remanded the matter to the trial court. The procedure for providing the transcripts to appellee should have been entrusted to the trial court. Appellee was entitled to receive only redacted transcripts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision allowing appellee newspaper to receive unredacted transcripts, subject to certain conditions, of a protracted criminal trial involving the alleged sexual abuse of intervenor parents' minor children. The court held that the procedure for providing the trial transcripts should have been entrusted to the trial court and that appellee was entitled to receive only redacted transcripts.

State v. Pennington


No. A-51


Supreme Court of New Jersey


119 N.J. 547; 575 A.2d 816; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 72


November 6, 1989, Argued  
June 21, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court, Bergen County (New Jersey) for capital murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder, felony murder, and possession of a firearm with the intent to use it unlawfully. Defendant appealed the capital conviction as of right, pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(3). The state conceded that the trial court failed to instruct the jury that defendant was not guilty of capital murder if he knowingly or purposely caused serious bodily injury that resulted in death, as distinguished from knowingly or purposely causing the death of the victim. The appellate court held that the trial court's failure required reversal of defendant's conviction for capital murder. The court found that the instructions in the case were insufficient and that the court could not determine whether the jury convicted defendant of purposely or knowingly causing death or purposely or knowingly causing serious bodily injury that resulted in death. Without that determination, the court noted, defendant could not be convicted of capital murder. The court held that the error was reversible because the evidence was "minimally adequate" to provide a rational basis for the jury to find that defendant intended to cause serious bodily injury.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendant's capital murder conviction. The court held that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury that defendant was not guilty of capital murder if he knowingly or purposely caused serious bodily injury that resulted in death, as distinguished from knowingly or purposely causing the death of the victim.

Post-conviction proceeding at, Petition denied by State v. Long, 170 N.J. 86, 784 A.2d 718, 2001 N.J. LEXIS 1135 (Sept. 6, 2001)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Petition denied by State v. Long, 178 N.J. 250, 837 A.2d 1093, 2003 N.J. LEXIS 1603 (Nov. 17, 2003)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Petition denied by State v. Long, 183 N.J. 215, 871 A.2d 93, 2005 N.J. LEXIS 402 (Mar. 14, 2005)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Long, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2216 (App.Div., Aug. 14, 2009)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Long, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2350 (App.Div., Sept. 26, 2013)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Long, 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2446 (App.Div., Oct. 15, 2014)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied Long v. Johnson, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 126440 (D.N.J., Aug. 9, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County.
State v. Long, 204 N.J. Super. 469, 499 A.2d 264, 1985 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1447 (Law Div., Jan. 7, 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Atlantic County (New Jersey), which convicted him of murder in a liquor store holdup and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's capital murder conviction of defendant. The court held that, in order to establish death-eligibility under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c, the jury must have determined that defendant had the knowledge or purpose to kill and not the knowledge or purpose merely to inflict serious bodily injury that happened to result in death. The court held that the trial court erroneously failed to explain to the jury that the indictment embraced two forms of murder, one capital and the other non-capital. Because the murder verdict could rationally have embraced capital or non-capital murder, the death penalty could not be sustained. If a sentence of death was to be imposed, the guilt phase of the capital murder charge had to be retried from the beginning, and because the robbery that established the single aggravating factor charged, murder in the course of robbery, was inextricably bound to the murder count, that robbery count had to be established in the murder proceeding. In all other respects, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's conviction of defendant for capital murder because the trial court had failed to instruct the jury that the indictment embraced capital and non-capital forms of murder. The court vacated the death sentence and remanded for further proceedings in accordance with its opinion. In all other respects, the court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Coyle


No. A-33


Supreme Court of New Jersey


119 N.J. 194; 574 A.2d 951; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 66


March 29, 1988, Argued  
June 11, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his murder conviction found and death sentence imposed by the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County (New Jersey), contending that it committed several instances of reversible error during his trial and penalty phase hearing.


OVERVIEW: After his release from an eight-year prison term for murder, appellant rented a home and developed a close friendship with his next-door neighbors, victim and his wife. After a period of time, appellant and victim's wife's relationship developed into a love affair. One evening, victim and his wife argued over her relationship with appellant. Wife went to appellant's home where victim followed. After a confrontation, appellant shot and wounded victim and then killed him. Appellant was convicted of purposeful murder in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3a(1) and was sentenced to death. On appeal as of right, the court reversed and remanded for a new trial. The court held the trial court comitted plain error when it did not distinguish between these two findings, and reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court convicting appellant of purposeful murder and sentencing him to death was reversed and remanded for a new trial because it was plain error to fail to distinguish in its charge to the jury between purposely or knowingly causing serious bodily injury resulting in death, and purposely or knowingly causing death.

Abbott v. Burke


No. A-63


Supreme Court of New Jersey


119 N.J. 287; 575 A.2d 359; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 64


September 25, 1989, Argued  
June 5, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 14, 1990.
Petition granted by Abbott v. Burke, 134 N.J. 555, 636 A.2d 515, 1993 N.J. LEXIS 1593 (1993)
Decision reached on appeal by Abbott by Abbott v. Burke, 136 N.J. 444, 643 A.2d 575, 1994 N.J. LEXIS 624 (1994)
Related proceeding at Education Law Center ex rel. Burke v. New Jersey Dept. of Educ., 396 N.J. Super. 634, 935 A.2d 858, 2007 N.J. Super. LEXIS 359 (App.Div., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the State Board of Education.
Abbott v. Burke, 117 N.J. 51, 563 A.2d 818, 1989 N.J. LEXIS 1090 (1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant students sought review of the decision of respondent State Board of Education (New Jersey) adopting the recommendations of respondent Commissioner of Education which asserted that students in poorer districts were being afforded a thorough and efficient education.


OVERVIEW: Appellant students challenged the constitutionality of the Public School Education Act of 1975 (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:7A-1 to 52, claiming that it did not afford a thorough and efficient educational funding system as required by a prior court decision. While the court had declared the Act constitutional, appellants attacked it as having failed to achieve its constitutional goal. After an administrative law judge determined that there were educational disparities between rich and poor districts, respondent Commissioner of Education declined to accept his recommendations. On review, respondent State Board of Education adopted that decision. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the Act was unconstitutional as applied to poorer urban school districts because the constitutional mandate had not been fulfilled. Thus, the court ruled the Act had to be amended, or new legislation passed, to assure that poorer urban districts' educational funding was substantially equal to that of richer districts. Regardless, poorer urban districts were required to have a budget per pupil sufficient to address their special needs and approximately equal to the average of the richer districts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of respondent State Board of Education supporting respondent Commissioner of Education's recommendation not to accept the findings of the administrative law judge, because respondents had not succeeded in affording a thorough and efficient educational funding system giving poor urban districts substantially the same opportunities as richer districts.

In re Preis


No. A-49


Supreme Court of New Jersey


118 N.J. 564; 573 A.2d 148; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 47


January 29, 1990, Argued  
May 8, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon certified review, respondent state challenged a decision from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), granting petitioner applicants permits to carry guns in their capacity as private security guards pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:58-4d.


OVERVIEW: Respondent state sought review of the appeals court's decision issuing gun-carrying permits to petitioner applicants for use in petitioners' capacity as private security guards. Petitioners were former police officers whose current employer, a licensed private security company, required petitioners to carry guns in the performance of their duties. Both petitioners had received the blessing of the local police chief to carry firearms. Upon certified review, the court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:58-4d did not create a preference in favor of private security guards in obtaining gun permits. Under the statutory standard, gun permits could be issued only to those who could establish an urgent necessity for protection of themselves or others. Petitioners failed to meet this standard. Legislative intent in passing stringent gun control laws was to keep firearms out of the hands of citizens and did not grant private security guards a preference in this regard. The court reversed, revoking the issuance of gun permits to petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's issuance of gun permits to petitioner applicants because petitioners failed to show an urgent necessity to carry guns to protect themselves or others, even though petitioners were former police officers who worked as private security guards and were required to carry personal firearms as part of their job duties.

State v. Kelly


No. A-28


Supreme Court of New Jersey


118 N.J. 370; 571 A.2d 1286; 1990 N.J. LEXIS 32


November 28, 1989, Argued  
April 4, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 226 N.J. Super. 267 (1988).
State v. Kelly, 226 N.J. Super. 267, 543 A.2d 1055, 1988 N.J. Super. LEXIS 244 (App.Div., 1988)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of her conviction, by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), of possession of a weapon under circumstances not manifestly appropriate for such lawful uses as it may have, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:39-5d. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in refusing to instruct on self-defense as a justification to the offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of a weapon under circumstances not manifestly appropriate for such lawful uses as it may have, in violation of N. J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:39-5d, after she slashed her former live-in companion repeatedly with a carpet cutting razor with which she had armed herself for protection based on his prior physical abuse. She sought review, contending that the trial court erred in refusing to give her requested instruction of self-defense as a justification to the charged offense. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. It held that the trial court did not err in refusing to give a self-defense instruction because self-defense did not excuse possession of a weapon in violation of § 5d except in rare and momentary circumstances where an individual arms himself spontaneously to meet an immediate danger.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the trial court did not err in refusing to give the requested instruction of self-defense where the statute prohibited the possession of implements as weapons, even if possessed for precautionary purposes, except in situations of immediate and imminent danger.

In re C.V.S. Pharm. Wayne

Supreme Court of New Jersey

Appellant, the State Board of Pharmacy, charged respondent with violating N.J. Stat. Ann. § 45:14-12f, which provided that it was unprofessional conduct for a pharmacist to offer premiums or rebates in connection the sale of drugs and medications.
