The court also ruled that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate division, which found in favor of respondent pharmacist on review of an administrative order from appellant, the State Board of Pharmacy, finding respondent guilty of unprofessional conduct. The court ruled that the statutory prohibition against offering premiums or rebates in connection with the sale of drugs and medications was constitutional.

State v. Davis


No. A-2


Supreme Court of New Jersey


116 N.J. 341; 561 A.2d 1082; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 105


January 3, 1989, Argued  
August 3, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County (New Jersey), which convicted defendant of several offenses, including capital murder, and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the brutal killing of a woman acquaintance. Defendant pled guilty some charges against him but not others. The jury convicted defendant and found that the state had proven aggravating factors. They also found two mitigating circumstances had been shown but concluded that the mitigating factors did not outweigh the aggravating factors, and sentenced defendant to death. He was sentenced on the non-capital counts to an aggregate term of life. Defendant's case was tried before two cases determining the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3, the capital punishment act were decided. The jury charge in defendant's case failed to comply with the requirement that to impose the death penalty the jury must be convinced beyond a reasonable doubt that the statutory aggravating factors of section 2C:11-3 outweigh the mitigating factors. The charge would have permitted imposition of capital punishment on a finding that the aggravating and mitigating factors balanced. The court found that a retrial of the penalty phase was required because the weighing process employed in the penalty phase was not consistent with that stated in the newly decided cases.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's sentence and remanded for a new trial because the cased did not take into account newly decided cases, which required that mitigating factors and aggravating circumstances be weighed at the penalty phase in capital cases.

State v. Yoskowitz


No. A-77


Supreme Court of New Jersey


116 N.J. 679; 563 A.2d 1; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 91


January 17, 1989, Argued  
July 19, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification the Superior Court, Appellant Division, whose opinion is reported at 224 N.J. Super. 375 (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for certification of review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which found that defendant's subsequent indictment for arson and attempted theft by deception after defendant pleaded guilty to a disorderly-persons offense of filing a false police report was not barred by double jeopardy or the mandatory joinder rule but was barred by principles of fundamental fairness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant paid someone to take his car so that he could collect insurance. Defendant pled guilty to a disorderly-persons offense of filing a false police report but was subsequently indicted for arson and attempted theft by deception and pled guilty with right to appeal. On review, the appellate court held that principles of fundamental fairness barred defendant's prosecution for the subsequent crime because of the mishandling of the case by the prosecutors and investigating officers. On certification by the state, the court reversed the decision of the appellate court and remanded the case for consideration of whether principles of fundamental fairness were violated. The court held that defendant's subsequent indictment did not violate the double jeopardy clauses of the federal and state constitutions nor N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-10a(3) because the offense elements, the evidence for the offenses, and the conduct and the harm to be prevented were different. The mandatory joinder rule of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-8(b) was not violated because the disorderly-persons offense was not a crime and the court did not have jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's determination that defendant's subsequent prosecution for arson and attempted theft by deception after pleading guilty to filing a false police report was fundamentally unfair due to the prosecutor's and officers' handling of the case and remanded for the trial court to determine whether the principles of fundamental fairness and reasonable expectations barred the subsequent prosecutions.

Accountemps Div. of Robert Half, Inc. v. Birch Tree Group, Ltd.


No. A-89


Supreme Court of New Jersey


115 N.J. 614; 560 A.2d 663; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 85


January 31, 1989, Argued  
July 13, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 224 N.J. Super. 163 (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an out of state employment agency, challenged a ruling of the Superior Court (New Jersey), Appellate Division, which reversed a trial court ruling and held that the Private Employment Agency Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:8-24 to -42, applied to appellant and barred appellant's enforcement of a contract with appellee employer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, an out of state employment agency, contracted with appellee employer to provide a temporary accountant for appellee's New Jersey company. The contract provided that appellant was entitled to a percentage fee if appellee decided to hire the temporary employee, but appellee refused to honor the contract when it hired the employee full-time. Appellee claimed that because appellant was not licensed under the Private Employment Agency Act (the Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:8-24 to -42, the contract between appellant and appellee was unenforceable. The trial court ruled in favor of appellant. The appellate court reversed and held that the Act applied to appellant and operated to make the contract unenforceable. The court reversed. The court held that although the Act applied to out of state employment agencies, this issue had not been previously ruled upon and therefore the ruling was to be applied only prospectively. The court allowed appellant to enforce its contract despite its failure to comply with the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the ruling which reversed a trial court ruling in favor of appellant, an out of state employment agency, in its action for damages against appellee employer. The court held that the relevant statute barred appellant's enforcement of a contract with appellee employer, but that its ruling was to be applied prospectively only, therefore the contract was enforced.

 

175 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bolte


No. A-152


Supreme Court of New Jersey


115 N.J. 579; 560 A.2d 644; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 87


April 25, 1989, Argued  
July 12, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 225 N.J. Super. 335 (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), granting defendant's motion for a suppression order.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff appealed a finding of the court of appeals that a police officer, in hot pursuit of a person suspected of numerous motor vehicle and disorderly persons offenses, could not make a warrantless entry into defendant's home to effect an arrest. The Appellate Division remanded for entry of an order suppressing the evidence of defendant's refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test on the ground that it was the product of an unlawful arrest. The appeals court held that the hot pursuit exception to the warrant requirements of U.S. Const. amend IV were limited to serious offenses, and concluded that even absent a serious offense limitation, exigent circumstances did not exist in this case. The court here affirmed the decision, finding no substantial police need for the warrantless invasion of defendant's home. Hot pursuit alone was an insufficient justification for the warrantless arrest in this case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of a suppression order after finding that police had no substantial need for the warrantless invasion of defendant's home. State failed to justify the warrantless intrusion in this case.

State v. Pitts


No. A-29


Supreme Court of New Jersey


116 N.J. 580; 562 A.2d 1320; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 73


March 15, 1988, Argued  
June 21, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court, Camden County (New Jersey) after being convicted of murder and other associated offenses for which he received life imprisonment concurrent with a death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering two individuals as well as other offenses that occurred prior to the murders. Thereafter, defendant was given a life sentence for the murder of the male victim, and was sentenced to death for the murder of the female victim. Upon review, defendant argued that the New Jersey Capital Punishment Act infringed on rights afforded him under the New Jersey Constitution and that it was error for the trial court to deny a motion to sever the murder offenses from the offenses that occurred two days prior. The court held that the death penalty statute did not violate the federal or state constitutional prohibitions against cruel and unusual punishment. Further, the court held that there was clearly a connection between the murders and the other offenses and that defendant was not significantly prejudiced by the joinder. Defendant also argued that the court erred in not instructing the jury that the evidence relating to the prior offenses were not to be considered as aggravating factors when determining the penalty. The court agreed and affirmed the convictions and penalties imposed; however, the death penalty was remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and sentencing as to one murder and the prior offenses; however, the death penalty was remanded for resentencing because the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury that the evidence relating to the prior offenses were not to be considered as aggravating factors in determining punishment.

 

177 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hunt


No. A-3


Supreme Court of New Jersey


115 N.J. 330; 558 A.2d 1259; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 71


January 19, 1988, Argued  
June 9, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Hunt, 117 N.J. 152, 564 A.2d 873, 1989 N.J. LEXIS 686 (1989)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Hunt, 2011 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2926 (App.Div., Dec. 2, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court, Camden County (New Jersey) after he was convicted of murder and received a death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death following a dispute where defendant stabbed his sister's boyfriend for allegedly abusing her. The penalty phase charge to the jury failed to state that the death penalty could only be imposed if the jury was convinced beyond a reasonable doubt that aggravating factors outweighed mitigating factors. During deliberations, the jury indicated by a note that it could not find a unanimous decision with regard to weighing the factors. The trial court provided a supplemental charge to the jury on the issue but did not remind the jury that a non-unanimous verdict was acceptable. On review, the court held that in capital cases, jurors need not reach a unanimous verdict and a decision not to agree was a legally acceptable outcome which would result in a final verdict. Further, as long as one juror believed that the aggravating factors did not outweigh the mitigating factors, the jury could not impose the death penalty. By not ascertaining whether the jury's inquiry meant it had reached a verdict, defendant was deprived of the opportunity to receive a sentence other than death. As such, the death sentence was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder but reversed the sentence of death and remanded for a non-death sentence because the trial court failed to instruct the jury that it had to be convinced beyond a reasonable doubt that the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors, thereby, depriving defendant of the possibility of receiving a sentence other than death.

State v. Gonzalez


No. A-187


Supreme Court of New Jersey


114 N.J. 592; 556 A.2d 323; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 46


April 5, 1989, Argued  
April 24, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The judgment from the Superior Court, Law Division, Burlington County (New Jersey), that found a probable cause hearing was required for a complaint charging a traffic offense and dismissed defendant's complaint for possession of a controlled dangerous substance in a motor vehicle contrary to N.J. Stat. Ann. 39:4-49.1 for lack of a probable cause determination, was certified for direct appeal on motion of the Supreme Court of New Jersey.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped for speeding. After a pat down, the police officer found marijuana in defendant's pocket. The officer issued defendant a summons, which was part of a uniform traffic ticket, for the possession of the marijuana and for speeding. The complaint, also part of the uniform traffic ticket, was completed and signed by the police officer and filed with the municipal court. No hearing on probable cause was held. Defendant pled guilty to speeding and was found guilty on the marijuana charge. Upon review, the intermediate appellate court dismissed the complaint because of the absence of a probable cause hearing. The supreme court certified the case for direct appeal on its own motion. The supreme court found that under N.J. Ct. R. 7:3-1 and 7:6-1, the police officer issues a summons for a traffic offense without a probable cause hearing and attests to probable cause by signing the complaint. The supreme court vacated the judgment of the intermediate appellate court because Rules 7:3-1 and 7:6-1 nor the traffic ticket required or contemplated a probable cause hearing and the exigencies of law enforcement would make a hearing after the summons had issued infeasible.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated the order of the intermediate appellate court that dismissed defendant's complaint for a traffic violation because no probable cause finding was required for a complaint charging the commission of a traffic offense. Nonetheless, the supreme court remanded the matter to the municipal court for a new trial, as that it was undisputed that defendant received inadequate advice of his right to counsel.

State v. Churchdale Leasing


Nos. A-123-87, A-124-87


Supreme Court of New Jersey


115 N.J. 83; 557 A.2d 277; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 38


May 3, 1988, Argued  
April 10, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported 214 N.J. Super. 307 (1987).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, leasing, enterprise, trucking, and supply companies, challenged judgments of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed their convictions of commercial vehicle registration violations under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:3-20, and appellant leasing and enterprise companies' conviction of exceeding the weight requirements for commercial vehicles under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:3-84b.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, leasing, enterprise, trucking, and supply companies, were charged and convicted for violating the commercial vehicle registration law in N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:3-20b because they operated at a weight in excess of that maximum authorized amount specified on the registration certificate. In addition, appellants, trucking and supply companies, were also charged and convicted for violating the separate excess-weight law in N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:3-84b. The trial court imposed cumulative fines on the trucking and supply companies for their dual convictions under § 39:3-20b and § 39:3-84b. The appellate division affirmed the convictions on appeal. However, the cumulative imposition of penalties for violations of § 39:3-20b and § 39:3-84b was improper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions, and held that an owner of a vehicle registered at the maximum weight limit, operating in excess of that weight, could be found guilty under the registration law. The court also ruled that the state could not cumulatively punish offenders that violate the commercial motor vehicle registration requirements and the excess-weight law, and remanded for corrective assessment of penalties.

Lower Main Street Associates v. New Jersey Housing & Mortg. Finance Agency


No. A-11/12


Supreme Court of New Jersey


114 N.J. 226; 553 A.2d 798; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 20


September 13, 1988, Argued  
February 22, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 219 N.J. Super. 263 (1987).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borrowers with outstanding loans held by appellee New Jersey Housing Mortgage and Finance Agency, sought reversal of that part of an order of the Superior Court of New Jersey, which upheld two regulations passed under a new rule promulgated by appellee, N.J. Admin. Code tit. 5, § 80-5, restricting appellants' rights to profit from sales of their properties. Appellee challenged the invalidation of a third regulation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant borrowers had financed housing projects through a predecessor of appellee New Jersey Housing Mortgage and Finance Agency. Appellee adopted a rule, N.J. Admin. Code tit. 5, § 80-5, to regulate transfers of financed projects, and passed three regulations that appellants challenged on contractual, statutory, and constitutional grounds. The lower court struck down only one of the regulations. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court found that all three regulations were invalid. The court held that N.J. Admin. Code tit. 5, § 80-5.9 imposed closing fees that had no rational relationship to appellee's costs. The court found that N.J. Admin. Code tit. 5, § 80-5.10, which prohibited repayment without prior approval, was invalid because it had no standards to govern the discretion by which appellee would grant approval. The court held that N.J. Admin. Code tit. 5, § 80-5.8, which placed an eight percent cap on the return of equity, was invalid because it was inconsistent with the enabling legislation and also because appellee's memoranda showed that its true purpose was actually to regulate mortgage prepayment and not to cap equity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the part of the lower court decision invalidating a regulation imposing closing fees because the fees were arbitrary and exorbitant. The court reversed the lower court in part and held that the prepayment approval regulation was invalid because it had no standards to guide the use of discretion; and held that the equity cap regulation was invalid because it violated the enabling legislation and had an ulterior purpose.

Friends of Governor Kean v. New Jersey Election Law Enforcement Com.


No. A-86


Supreme Court of New Jersey


114 N.J. 33; 552 A.2d 612; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 10


September 23, 1985, Argued; Decision rendered September 27, 1985  
February 2, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 203 N.J. Super. 523 (1985).
 Friends of Kean v. New Jersey Election Law Enforcement Com., 203 N.J. Super. 523, 497 A.2d 555, 1985 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1469 (App.Div., 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New Jersey Election Law Enforcement Commission sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) in favor of respondents, the governor's reelection committee and candidates for the legislature. The judgment invalidated appellant's allocation rule, which sought to allocate to the governor's reelection campaign certain expenses of legislative candidates.


OVERVIEW: In Advisory Opinion 33-1981, appellant New Jersey Election Law Enforcement Commission decided to allocate to the gubernatorial candidate's expenditure limit a percentage of advertising expenditures made by a non-gubernatorial candidate of the same party that mentioned or advocated the election of the gubernatorial candidate. By virtue of AO 33-1981, sums of money spent by non-gubernatorial candidates would be allocated to the gubernatorial candidates and charged against their expenditure limits as if the gubernatorial candidates spent the money themselves. The effect, given the statutory limit on total permitted gubernatorial expenditures, would be to reduce, by that amount, the sum permitted to be spent on the gubernatorial candidate's own campaign. The validity of AO 33-1981 was questioned by respondents, candidates for the legislature and the governor's campaign committee. The appellate court invalidated the allocation rule, and the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that the allocation scheme, as applied to independent expenditures of other candidates, exceeded appellant's grant of authority under N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 19:44A-1 to -44.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court invalidating appellant New Jersey Election Law Enforcement Commission's allocation rule, which sought to allocate to the governor's reelection campaign certain expenses of legislative candidates. The court found that the allocation scheme, as applied to the independent expenditures of another candidate, exceeded appellant's authority.

Brown v. Newark


No. A-133/34


Supreme Court of New Jersey


113 N.J. 565; 552 A.2d 125; 1989 N.J. LEXIS 4


March 14, 1988, Argued  
January 18, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City of Newark challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reinstated several but not all of the sections of Newark, N.J., Rev. Ordinance, tit. 8, ch. 7, § 6, which regulated respondent peddlers.


OVERVIEW: Respondent peddlers challenged the validity of Newark, N.J., Rev. Ordinance, tit. 8, ch. 7, § 6. The trial court invalidated all or part of the 19 sections, and the appellate court reinstated seven of those sections. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the appellate court's judgment. The court used the rationally related level of scrutiny of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, the balancing test of N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1, and for substantive due process purposes, the arbitrary standard for legitimate legislative purposes. Newark, N.J., Rev. Ordinance, tit. 8, ch. 7, § 6(d), which prohibited respondents from remaining at the same location when a sale was not being transacted, was unconstitutionally vague. Newark, N.J., Rev. Ordinance, tit. 8, ch. 7, § 6(e), (f), (h), (l), (m), (o), (p), and (q), which governed the carts' sizes and locations protected the public against pedestrian congestion. Newark, N.J., Rev. Ordinance, tit. 8, ch. 7, § 6(n), which required respondents to give customers receipts and retain copies of the receipts, fulfilled a legitimate governmental purpose of avoiding altercations between respondents and consumers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment that reinstated several sections of an ordinance that regulated respondent peddlers. The section that prohibited respondents from remaining in the same location was unconstitutionally vague while sections governing the carts' sizes and locations and required sales receipts were rationally related and fulfilled legitimate governmental purposes.

State v. Williams


No. A-4


Supreme Court of New Jersey


113 N.J. 393; 550 A.2d 1172; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 117


November 10, 1987, Argued  
December 8, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey that convicted him of murder and imposed a death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant went to a nursing home with his brother, then robbed, raped, and murdered a nurse. The brother reported defendant's actions to the police a few days later. During the trial, summation, and sentencing phase, the prosecutor emphasized the brutality of the crime and the good character of the victim. The trial court denied defendant's pre-trial motions to allow attorney-conducted voir dire, to permit questioning on racial prejudice, to use a struck jury system to exercise peremptory challenges, and for more peremptory challenges. Defendant was convicted and sentenced to death. The court reversed the conviction and death sentence, finding that the voir dire was inadequate, forcing the defense and prosecution to exercise their preemptory challenges in an uninformed manner, and leaving the trial court unable to fairly evaluate the jurors' fitness to serve. The inadequate voir dire, combined with the trial court's failure to excuse one juror for cause which led to defendant's loss of a peremptory challenge, impaired defendant's right to an impartial jury to the extent that it compelled the reversal of the conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the murder conviction and the death sentence, finding that the trial court failed to assure that the jury was impartial and that there was reversible error during the penalty phase with respect to the jury instructions. The inadequate voir dire combined with an erroneous refusal to dismiss a juror for cause created the loss of a peremptory challenge and required reversal. The court remanded for a new trial.

State v. Gerald


No. A-6


Supreme Court of New Jersey


113 N.J. 40; 549 A.2d 792; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 107; 83 A.L.R.4th 331


May 5, 1987, Argued  
October 25, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division (Atlantic County).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Atlantic County (New Jersey), which convicted him of conspiracy to commit burglary, burglary, conspiracy to commit robbery, robbery, aggravated assault, felony murder, and purposeful or knowing murder and sentenced him to death. Defendant argued that he could be subjected to the death penalty only if his conduct, standing alone, resulted in the victim's death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of conspiracy to commit burglary, burglary, conspiracy to commit robbery, robbery, aggravated assault, felony murder, and purposeful or knowing murder and was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed all of the convictions except the capital murder conviction. The court held that on state constitutional grounds, a defendant who was convicted of purposely or knowingly causing serious bodily injury resulting in death under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3(a)(1) and (2), or either of them, as opposed to one who was convicted of purposely or knowingly causing death, could not be subjected to the death penalty because imposition of the death penalty was irrational and grossly disproportionate to the crime charged. The court also held that a sentencing court or jury in a capital murder case could not consider a co-defendant's sentence as a mitigating factor under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3(c)(5)(h). The court found that while the imputation of liability for the conduct of another sufficed for a murder conviction, the defendant's own conduct in the commission of the murder was a prerequisite to imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for conspiracy to commit burglary, burglary, conspiracy to commit robbery, robbery, aggravated assault, and felony murder, but reversed and remanded his conviction and death sentence for purposeful or knowing murder. A defendant convicted of purposely or knowingly causing serious bodily injury resulting in death was subject to imprisonment and not the death penalty.

State v. Rose

Supreme Court of New Jersey

Appeal after remand at State v. Rose, 122 N.J. 157, 584 A.2d 225, 1990 N.J. LEXIS 776 (1990)
Decision reached on appeal by State v. Rose, 2011 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 969 (App.Div., Apr. 18, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions for purposeful or knowing murder, possession of a sawed-off shotgun, possession of a sawed-off shotgun with a purpose to use it unlawfully against the person or property of another and hindering apprehension from the Superior Court of Essex County (New Jersey), and argued that he was deprived of his federal and state constitutional rights to a fair trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of purposeful or knowing murder, possession of a sawed-off shotgun, possession of a sawed-off shotgun with a purpose to use it unlawfully against the person or property of another and hindering apprehension and received a death sentence. State v. Zola

OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the brutal murder of a 75-year-old woman in which the victim was burned, strangled, stabbed and strapped to a bed and assaulted. Defendant was convicted of knowing and purposeful murder, burglary, aggravated sexual assault, kidnapping, and robbery. At the penalty phase the jury found two aggravating factors: N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(g), that the murder was committed during a felony, and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(c), that the murder was outrageously or wantonly vile, horrible, or inhuman in that it involved torture, depravity of mind, or an aggravated assault of the victim. On appeal, the State agreed that a retrial of the penalty phase was required because the jury charge failed to comply with the requirement that to impose the death penalty the jury had to find beyond a reasonable doubt that the statutory aggravating factors of § 2C:11-3, outweighed the mitigating factors. The court affirmed the convictions based on the evidence of experts on bodily fluids and the proof that defendant had the required mental state to justify conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for knowingly and purposeful murder, burglary, aggravated assault, kidnapping, and robbery. The court reversed and remanded the sentence of death for capital resentencing because the jury was not charged that to impose the death penalty the jury was required to find beyond a reasonable doubt that the statutory aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors.

Holder v. Polanski


No. A-108


Supreme Court of New Jersey


111 N.J. 344; 544 A.2d 852; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 90


February 17, 1988, Argued  
August 9, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife sought review of an order of the Superior Court of New Jersey, which previously denied her permission to move to another state with the children from her marriage to defendant husband. Plaintiff had moved but left the children with defendant for two years, and she sought custody again.


OVERVIEW: Two years prior to this ruling, plaintiff wife had been denied permission to move to another state with the children from her marriage to defendant husband. The trial court found that although the move would have been beneficial to plaintiff, she had not shown that the same benefits were not available in New Jersey, nor had she established that it was in the best interests of the children to be away from defendant. Plaintiff moved, and the trial court gave defendant residential custody of the children. On appeal, the court held that focus should not have been on the benefits that would have accrued to plaintiff in the move but on the best interests of the children and on the preservation of their relationship with defendant. The court held that plaintiff needed only to establish a good-faith reason for the move. The court found that defendant's visitation rights would not have been substantially affected by plaintiff's move and held that she should have been permitted to move with her children. In reversing and remanding, the court held that because the children had lived with their father during the last two years, the issue on remand was their best interest at the present time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the appellate court's order, which had denied plaintiff wife permission to move to another state with the children from her marriage to defendant husband. The court held that the original ruling should have permitted plaintiff wife to move out of state, and issue on remand was to be which custodial arrangement was in the best interests of the children.

State v. Koedatich


No. A-1


Supreme Court of New Jersey


112 N.J. 225; 548 A.2d 939; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 83


September 28, 1987, Argued  
August 3, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Morris County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions and death sentence for murder under N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:11-3a(1)-(2), kidnapping under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:13-1b, and aggravated sexual assault under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2a, from the Superior Court of Morris County (New Jersey). Defendant argued that the death penalty was unconstitutional, and the trial court's refusal to permit the defense to question the jury during voir dire was prejudicial error.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:11-3a(1)-(2), kidnapping, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:13-1b, and aggravated sexual assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2a, and received a death sentence. The court affirmed the convictions. The court found no reason to conclude that prosecutorial discretion in the administration of the death penalty was being abused or that it had resulted in an unconstitutionally arbitrary scheme of selection of cases for capital prosecution. The court found that defendant consented to a seizure of his vehicle and evidence obtained during a subsequent search was admissible. The court found that the trial court did not err when it denied defendant's motion for a change of venue because the voir dire in the case indicated no hostility to defendant by the jurors who served in his trial as to suggest a partiality that could not be laid aside. Because the trial court erred during sentencing when it required that mitigating factors must outweigh aggravating factors in order for a sentence less than death to be imposed, and that the jury had to agree unanimously on the existence of a mitigating factor, the death sentence was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder, kidnapping and aggravated sexual assault from the trial court because the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it denied defendant's motion for a change of venue, and evidence and testimony was properly admitted at trial. Because the trial court failed to instruct the jury properly in the sentencing phase, the death sentence was reversed and remanded for resentencing.

 

190 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bey


No. A-2


Supreme Court of New Jersey


112 N.J. 45; 548 A.2d 846; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 72


September 15, 1987, Argued  
August 2, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his jury conviction of murder and sentence of death from the Superior Court of Monmouth County (New Jersey) challenging the application of the death penalty statute due to his status as a minor at the time of the offense. Defendant also argued the trial court erred by not suppressing his confession and that prejudicial mid-trial publicity undermined his right to trial by a fair and impartial jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. While being interrogated by the police, defendant invoked his right to cut off questioning, but the interrogation continued as if nothing had happened. The court held the police did not scrupulously honor defendant's exercise of his right to cut off questioning. The police violated his rights, and his confessions were erroneously introduced at trial. Defendant also claimed prejudicial mid-trial publicity undermined his right to trial by a fair and impartial jury. Due to the highly prejudicial nature of the information contained in newspaper articles about the trial, and the realistic possibility that it reached the jurors, the trial court's refusal to poll the jury violated defendant's fair trial rights and required a new trial. Defendant sought to bar the "death qualification" of the jury on the ground that application of the death penalty to defendant was cruel and unusual punishment. The court held the death penalty statute exempting juveniles applied to defendant. The court concluded defendant could not receive the death penalty since he was a juvenile at the time of the murder.


OUTCOME: The convictions were reversed and remanded for new trial. The introduction of defendant's confession at trial violated his constitutional rights because police did not honor the exercise of his right to cut off questioning, and his rights to a fair and impartial jury were violated by the jury's potential exposure to prejudicial mid-trial publicity. It was not the intent of the legislature to have juveniles eligible for capital punishment.

 

191 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bey


No. A-5


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


112 N.J. 123; 548 A.2d 887; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 82


May 5, 1987, Argued  
August 2, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 24, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County (New Jersey), which convicted him of murder, felony murder, kidnapping, and aggravated sexual assault. The trial court sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed to assaulting, raping, robbing, and killing a woman. He was convicted of murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3a(1), (2), felony murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3a(3), kidnapping, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:13-1b(1), (2), aggravated assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:12-1b(1), aggravated sexual assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. §2C:14-2a(3) & (6), robbery, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1a(1)-(3), and theft, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:20-3a. The trial court sentenced him to death. On appeal, the court held that defendant's confession was voluntary and that his stated desire to lie down and think about what happened was not an invocation of his right to cut off interrogation. The court held that the trial court improperly instructed the jury that their findings on the existence of mitigating factors had to be unanimous. The court held that the jurors should have been permitted to determine individually whether mitigating factors existed and whether they were outweighed by the aggravating factors beyond a reasonable doubt, and then unanimously agree on the imposition of the death penalty. The court reversed the sentence and remanded for a new sentencing trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for capital murder because defendant's confession was voluntary and the trial court properly charged the jury on intoxication. However, the court reversed the death sentence and remanded for a new sentencing hearing because the trial court erroneously instructed the jury that the findings on mitigating factors had to be unanimous.

194 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Franklin v. New Jersey Dep't of Human Services


No. A-213/214


Supreme Court of New Jersey


111 N.J. 1; 543 A.2d 1; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 67


June 14, 1988, Argued  
June 24, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at     N.J. Super.     (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant aid recipients sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which upheld a regulation of respondent agency, N.J. Admin. Code tit. 10:82, § 5.10 (1987), that terminated Emergency Assistance benefits for emergency housing under the state Aid to Families with Dependent Children program after five months, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 44:10-1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant aid recipients filed an action to challenge respondent agency's regulation that terminated benefits for emergency housing under the Emergency Assistance (EA) program after 150 days under N.J. Admin. Code tit. 10:82, § 5.10 (1987). The court affirmed the decision of the lower court which upheld the regulation and found that the regulation was consistent with the goals of the state Aid to Families with Dependent Children program, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 44:10-1 et seq., of which the EA program was a part. The court noted that the reason for imposing the time limit was to move children and their families out of welfare hotels and other temporary housing and into a more permanent environment. The court also found that other programs existed to pick up where the EA program left off. The court also recognized that because of the myriad, extraordinary and complex factual issues presented and because of the limited ability of the court to control operations of the other branches of government, particularly with respect to appropriations, the court was hesitant to invalidate a legislative program and its attendant regulations before the program had a chance to be tested.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court to uphold respondent agency's regulation that terminated Emergency Assistance benefits for emergency housing under the state Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) program after 150 days because the regulation was consistent with the goals of the AFDC and the complexity of the issue necessitated that the regulation be given a chance to work.

 

195 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Strong


No. A-58


Supreme Court of New Jersey


110 N.J. 583; 542 A.2d 866; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 49


October 27, 1987, Argued  
June 16, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and denied defendant's motion to dismiss on the basis that defendant's indictment was based on evidence derived from defendant's earlier compelled testimony in violation of N. J. Stat. Ann. 2A:81-17.3, and U.S. Const. amend. V.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was involved in a robbery murder with other individuals. An order was granted pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:81-17.3, that granted defendant immunity and required defendant to testify against the other participants. Defendant testified before the grand jury and at trial against one of the participants. Defendant was later indicted for murder and moved to dismiss the indictment on the basis that the indictment was based on evidence derived from defendant's earlier compelled testimony and violated the statutory immunity under § 2A:81-17.3, and U.S. Const. amend. V. The indictment was based in part on testimony from a friend of the other participant that was convicted on defendant's testimony. The trial court denied defendant's motion and defendant plead guilty and reserved the right to appeal from the order. The lower court affirmed the trial court's order. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for a new hearing on defendant's motion. The court held that the state had the burden to prove by clear and convincing evidence that defendant's compelled testimony was not used as a basis for defendant's indictment, either directly or indirectly.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment and remanded for a new hearing on defendant's motion because the state had the burden of proof to prove by clear and convincing evidence that defendant's compelled testimony was not used either directly or indirectly as a basis for defendant's indictment.

 

196 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Bell v. Stafford


No. A-68


Supreme Court of New Jersey


110 N.J. 384; 541 A.2d 692; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 42


January 5, 1988, Argued  
May 26, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Bell v. Stafford, 108 N.J. 191, 528 A.2d 17, 1987 N.J. LEXIS 1018 (1987)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of New Jersey, which found that appellant's ordinance that regulated respondent citizen's use of billboards was unconstitutional. Appellant contended that the case should have been decided on nonconstitutional grounds.


OVERVIEW: Respondent citizen brought an action against appellant township in which respondent sought a declaratory judgment that appellant's ordinance regulating the use of billboards was unconstitutional. The trial court rejected respondent's argument, but the appeals court held that the ordinance was facially unconstitutional. The court affirmed the appeals court. The court stated that it was clear that an issue involving the constitutionality of the ordinance was properly presented. The court stated that because the exercise of U.S. Const. amend. I rights and freedom of speech were at stake, appellant could not seek refuge in a presumption of the ordinance's validity. The court held that appellant had not presented adequate evidence that demonstrated its ordinance furthered a particular, substantial governmental interest, and that its ordinance was sufficiently narrow to further only that interest without unnecessarily restricting freedom of expression. The court concluded that appellant had consequently failed to demonstrate a basis for upholding the constitutionality of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's judgment that appellant township's ordinance that regulated respondent citizen's use of billboards was unconstitutional. The court held that appellant had not presented adequate evidence that the ordinance furthered a particular, substantial governmental interest and that it was sufficiently narrow to further only that interest without unnecessarily restricting freedom of expression.

Market Street Mission v. Bureau of Rooming & Boarding House Standards, Dep't of Community Affairs


No. A-93


Supreme Court of New Jersey


110 N.J. 335; 541 A.2d 668; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 41


February 29, 1988, Argued  
May 19, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 217 N.J. Super. 56 (1987).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Bureau of Rooming and Boarding House Standards challenged an order from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that the Rooming and Boarding House Act of 1979 (act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 55:13B-1 to -21 was inapplicable to the operation of respondent charitable organization and reversed a penalty appellant imposed upon respondent for failing to comply with the act.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Bureau of Rooming and Boarding House Standards imposed a penalty upon respondent charitable organization for failing to comply with the fire safety provisions of the Rooming and Boarding House Act of 1979 (act), N.J. Stat. Ann. 55:13B-1 to -21. Respondent contested the penalty as inapplicable to charitable organizations. The lower court agreed and appellant sought review. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the lower court's judgment and found that the state had a legitimate concern for the regulation of fire safety in boarding houses as the residents were typically elderly, disabled, and poor, many of whom needed protection from building hazards. The court held that legislative intent appeared to not exclude all boarding facilities owned and operated by non-profit religious organizations as the one amendment to the act excluded only a certain group. Finally, the court held that appellant was entrusted to pursue the least restrictive means to achieve the state's concern and absent a finding that appellant unnecessarily intruded into respondent's religious affairs, the provisions of the act would apply to respondent.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of respondent charitable organization in its claim that appellant's statutorily imposed penalty was inapplicable was reversed and remanded on the grounds that the statute's application did not exclude respondent and that facially the statute did not unduly interfere with respondent's free exercise of religion or create an excessive state entanglement with religion.

 

198 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


In re Directive of the N.J. Dep't of Envtl. Prot.


Nos. A-127, A-128


Supreme Court of New Jersey


110 N.J. 69; 539 A.2d 1181; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 34; 27 ERC (BNA) 1668; 18 ELR 20933


November 18, 1986, Argued  
April 18, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Department of Environmental Protection.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant oil companies sought review of a decision of the Department of Environmental Protection (New Jersey), which determined that they were responsible for groundwater contamination and ordered them to fund construction of an alternate water supply for the population in the area or to be subject to treble damages.


OVERVIEW: Appellant oil companies were held responsible for groundwater contamination in a certain residential area and were ordered by appellee Department of Environmental Protection to fund construction of an alternate water supply or be subjected to treble damages. Appellants sought review and filed a motion for a stay of the order, but the motion was denied. The lower court, however, enjoined imposition of treble damages. On appeal, appellants challenged the constitutionality of the New Jersey Spill Compensation and Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 58:10-23.11, asserting that the treble damages provision violated their due process rights because it served to deter judicial challenge to state action. The court affirmed the directive after modifying it, holding that the treble damages did not have to be assessed if the party opposing such damages had an objectively reasonable basis for believing that the directive was either invalid or inapplicable, and that a decision by appellee to seek treble damages would be subject to judicial review. The directive issued by appellee complied with the statute and the provisions ordering payment of removal costs were valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the directive ordering appellant oil companies to pay for an alternate water source after modifying it to remove the threat of treble damages. The court held that a party who opposed treble damages should have been able to appeal the assessment as being invalid or inapplicable, and that future directives by appellee Department of Environmental Protection were subject to judicial review.

 

199 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Martin


No. A-84


Supreme Court of New Jersey


110 N.J. 10; 538 A.2d 1229; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 23


March 17, 1987, Argued  
March 24, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 209 N.J. Super. 473 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed his conviction for first-degree robbery, a Graves Act offense under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1, in which appellant used or possessed a firearm. He alleged that he was not notified that an extended term would be sought by the state and was denied a hearing on whether there was a prior conviction for a Graves Act offense.


OVERVIEW: The state convicted appellant of a Graves Act offense: first-degree robbery, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1, in which appellant used or possessed a firearm. The state contended that appellant had been convicted of a prior Graves Act offense, and, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7(a) and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7(c) and without prior notice to appellant, the state sought an extended sentence for appellant's offense. Appellant learned of the imposition of an extended sentence during sentencing and requested a hearing, which the trial court denied and the lower appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the matter and found that the statute clearly required notice and hearing before the imposition of a mandatory extended term based on a prior Graves Act conviction. The court held that in light of the clear statutory mandate, it was insufficient for the state to rely upon a presentence report indicating a prior conviction and the relative passivity of appellant at sentencing to support the imposition of the extended term without prior notice and hearing.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction was reversed and remanded on the grounds that fairness dictated that the state comply with the clear terms of a statute requiring prior notice and hearing before imposing a mandatory extended sentencing term regardless of whether there was a presentence report as to a prior conviction and whether appellant remained silent during sentencing.

 

200 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Riggs v. Long Beach


No. A-9


Supreme Court of New Jersey


109 N.J. 601; 538 A.2d 808; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 22


September 14, 1987 Argued  
March 23, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 212 N.J. Super. 69 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of a Superior Court (New Jersey), which found that Long Beach, N.J., Code 81-1C had a valid zoning purpose in an action against appellee township challenging the zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owners challenged a zoning ordinance, Long Beach, N.J., Code 81-1C, that was enacted shortly after negotiations with appellee township failed over the purchase of appellants' property. The lower court held that the ordinance was unreasonable, arbitrary, and void as unconstitutional. The appellate court reversed the decision because of the enactment of a later ordinance that was passed to correct defects. The court reversed the appellate court and remanded for review of the new ordinance, but the appellate court found that the new ordinance had a valid zoning purpose and again reversed the finding of the lower court. On further review, the court reversed the decision and remanded to the lower court. Appellee's attempt to link the reduction of lots to the designation of open space in a master plan by using the ordinance was nothing more than a red herring to divert attention from the true purpose of the ordinance, which was to reduce the number of lots as a means of reducing the fair market value of appellants' property. The court held the ordinances were invalid, were not rationally related to a valid zoning purpose, and were unreasonable and arbitrary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the prior decisions in favor of appellee township and held that the ordinances enacted were passed to divert attention from the true purpose, to reduce the number of lots as a means to reduce the market value of appellant owners' property. The ordinances were invalid and were not reasonably related to a valid zoning purpose.
